                               BORDERLAND









                              in childhood





one summer, which in point of date now lie many year behind we, four

boy use to play together, and to quarrel and make it up again with one

another—to live together through the long, golden day, that vivid,

eager life peculiar to child, in a curious, old-fashioned garden on

the bank of the river Tees, and on the Durham side of that stream. the

garden belong to a great house, not very old, though it be the abode

of an old family, solemn, not to say gloomy, in its dulness and

stateliness of appearance, and stand out in rather sombre contrast to

the wood which be behind it, and the terrace which slope down from

its front to the river-side. the name of the house be Thorsgarth; many

a spot hereabouts bear some name reminiscent of long-past Danish

occupation and scandinavian paganism. it be a characteristic give a

peculiar flavour to the language and nomenclature of the whole

country-side, and one, too, which have be sweetly sing by at least one

of our english poet. with this fact, these four particular boy be

probably unacquainted, and it be more than probable that if they have

know all about it they would have care less than nothing for the

circumstance. what could it matter to they that, a little far down

the stream, that sweet spot where they love to wade in the shallow,

and not far from which noisy Greta came tumble and laugh into the

arm of sedater tee—where the numerous wasp’ nest be to be find

under the bank, to destroy which nest they have go through such

delicious toil and peril, and where on sunny day the trout would lurk

in the pool amongst the big boulders,—what could it matter to they that

this scene have be immortalise by both poet and painter? to they it

be all their own paradise; the presence of an artist would have vex

and incommode they. there they kick, jump, splash, and generally

misconduct themselves in the sweet solitude and the generous sunshine

of that far-back summer, without a thought of its be hallowed ground.

three of they be not of an age at which the ordinary boy be give to

appreciate poetry. as for the eld of they, if he ever do read it, he

keep the fact to himself.



these four boy be all the son of gentleman, in the conventional

sense of the term—albeit their father be man of widely different

calibre, as regard not only worldly, but also mental and moral

characteristic.



the eld and the third in age be brother, Michael and Gilbert

Langstroth. their father’s be one of the old family in the

neighbourhood, and have be one of the rich, although many people have

begin to say that not much be now practically left to he except the

old house itself, the Red Gables, which stand in genial vicinity to many

other house, both great and small, in the great cobble-stone, slant

square, which form the west end of Bradstane town.



Michael Langstroth at this period be twelve year old, a noble boy to

look at, tall and broad, with a dark face, and a sweet, rather rare

smile. there be a good deal of unconscious pride in his manner and

bearing. perhaps his pierce gray eye, go with this dark

complexion, might really betoken that Norse descent in which his family

glory. all his action be, so far as one could judge, in harmony

with his outer appearance; without fuss or ostentation, but all

partaking of the intrinsically splendid, generous, and lavish. even at

this early time of their life, the other boy know that Michael hate

lie with an intensity which show itself more in sudden, violent

action than in word. they know that he resent any untruth amongst

they as if it have be a personal insult. there be, indeed, no doubt

that Michael be a son in whose proud look a father might glory; while

with all his strength and power there be in he other and quieter

charm, such as a mother might delight in. and Mrs. Langstroth do very

greatly delight in what seem to her her son’s high and noble

quality, during the short time that she be allow to do so.



‘I fear it will never last,’ she would say to herself, watch he with

prayer and trembling, as mother do watch those son who have a way of

turn into something so different from what the maternal yearning

would shape they into if, along with the yearning, the power exist of

fulfil they. ‘I fear it will never last. contact with the world will

harden he. Flattery will make he vain. universal homage will spoil

he.’ Mrs. Langstroth be a sweet and saintly lady, and her son Michael

a brave and noble boy; but what insignificant hen-mother exist who do

not think that the attention to herself and her matchless offspring must

of necessity be universal?



with pathetic, devoted blindness she would have prepare he to meet

this irresistible tide of flattery and greatness by keep he fast at

her own side, and never loose his lead string. the mention of a

public school draw tear from her eye, and set her gentle heart beat

wildly. it be write that her son Michael’s education—every branch of

it—be to be take out of her hand, and place in other, firm,

hard, sterner, and to they who can survive their roughness, kind

hand than even those of a mother.



Gilbert, Michael’s brother, be a well-grow boy, too, of ten, with a

small, rounder head, a narrow forehead, and blue-gray eye, which

have a trick of languish sometimes. he have an exquisitely soft and

melancholy voice, be slow of speech, and possess a graceful, though

by no mean effeminate figure. he be always, and apparently by nature,

courteous and gentle in manner and speech, seldom indulge in the

downright unflattering candour which Michael, for all he be so

gentlemanly, frequently use towards his companion. Gilbert never say

rude thing to any one, but he be not so popular with his comrade as

Michael.



the second boy, in order of year, be swarthy Roger Camm, the son of

the curate of Bradstane. eleven be the year he count in actual

point of time—thirty, perhaps, and those rough one, in his knowledge of

care and trouble, in his painful, enforce acquaintance with grief, with

contrivance and economy, and weary struggle to make both end meet.

for his father be not pass rich on forty pound a year—he be more

than pass poor on something less than a hundred, out of which he have

dolefully to ‘keep up the appearance of a gentleman,’ clothe and feed

his son and himself, and educate the former. his wife, poor soul,

exhaust with the endless and complicated calculation necessitate by

this ever-present problem, have some year ago thankfully close her

eye, and say good-bye to labour and grief. the curate and his lad

struggle on without she as well they could. all that Roger learn,

whether of solid instruction or flimsy accomplishment—little enough be

there of the latter to gloss his manner or appearance—he be teach by

his father, and that with fasting and prayer. along with his latin and

greek declension, he imbibe also the more bitter lesson of decline

fortune; for his father have marry late, and be not promote as he

grow old and more careworn. side by side with the first problem of

Euclid, as with the last, there be for ever present another, which it

would have require more than a mere mathematical head to answer, and

which yet imperiously demand some sort of a solution; it be the

problem which Mrs. Camm have carry with she to her grave, and it run:

‘give, not sufficient income to buy a proper supply of butcher’s meat,

cake, and ale, how to make water-porridge twice a day, with skim-milk

to wash it down, answer the same purpose as the more liberal diet, save

on certain rare and solemn feast-day, not specify in the calendar.’

and along with the invaluable rule, that preposition govern the

objective case (for the Rev. Silas Camm hold fast by the Lindley Murray

of his boyhood), Roger grasp and hold fast the axiom, so that he could

and do mould his conduct upon it, that to bear your hardship in

silence be necessary—that to utter one word of complaint, to look

greedily at occasional dainty, or to gorge in unseemly fashion on the

abundance at other man’s table, no matter what the size of the internal

void to be fill; to betray by word, look, or deed that you ever feel

the pinch of hunger at home—to do this be disgrace of the deep dye,

second only to lie and steal. by the time he be eleven year old,

Roger have digest these lesson thoroughly, and have, as it be,

assimilate they, so that they be in his system. sometimes, at the

abundant ‘spread’ on the Thorsgarth or Red Gables board, his sallow

young face would take a faint glow, his deep-set black eye would grow

wistfully misty, but never a word betray the bareness of the board at

home, nor the fact that his father might even then be ask a blessing

upon a bowl of oatmeal-porridge, sole reward of a hard day’s work. for

the living of Bradstane, although ancient, be not rich, and the parish

priest’s own stipend be not a fat one. Judge, therefore, how exceeding

short the curate must have come!



Roger be on good term with all his companion, and if they sometimes

wonder why he never use to ask they to go and play with he, or have

tea with he, they be quite satisfied with his explanation, that there

be no garden to his father’s house, and they agree with he, that

without a garden to play in there could be no fun. he and Michael

Langstroth, very dissimilar in almost everything, be fast friend,

while Gilbert Langstroth and the fourth and last of this party of boy

hang together in a lukewarm manner, the old and calm of they often

quietly instigate the mischief that the young one perform.



this fourth and young be Otho Askam, the only son of the master of

Thorsgarth, and heir to the sombre-look house, and the grand old

garden in which they all disport themselves. Otho, like his friend,

be tall for his age, and well set-up. one can but guess at the man to

come, in the little father of eight year old. but Otho give strong

sign of individuality even at this early age. the other boy, if they

have speak their mind, would have say that he be fitful and moody in

temper; that no one could tell what would please, what offend he; that,

when he be pleased, it be in a saturnine, mirthless style, strange in

so young a child; that when offend, his wrath be more deep than loud,

but that his brown eye glow, on such occasion, with a dull fire, and

his childish face in its anger take an expression of savage fierceness.

they could also have relate, these other boy, that when angry, Otho

never rest till he have revenge himself, either by damaging or

mutilate some of their cherished ‘thing,’ or by do they bodily

harm, as grievous as his childish brain and small hand could devise and

compass. by the end of the summer they have got use to it. they laugh

at he, and talk about his ‘little rage.’ they be big and

strong than he be. the young of they be two year his senior.

they use to tease he sometimes, on purpose to have the fun of see

what shape his vengeance would take, and would shout with laughter at

its feebleness when wreak.



there be on record one great occasion on which Michael Langstroth have

fail to see the amusing side of an escapade of Otho’s, and have take

upon himself to give he a sound hiding (with due regard, that be, to

the difference in their age and strength). it be sound enough,

however, for Master Otho to make the welkin ring again with his yell;

but the thrashing have be administer in payment, with interest, if

possible, of Otho’s wanton cruelty to a wretched, half-starve cat,

which he have pursue with the vindictive determination not so much to

compass its death, as to secure to it as long a term of torture as

possible, before that death should take place.



‘you cowardly little viper, you!’ Michael have shout, tower over he

after the first half of the pummelling have be administer. ‘don’t you

know that it’s nothing but a coward, and a dirty one, that hurt thing

that cann’t fight back? you miserable little beggar, you!’



‘Michael, I’ll kill you, I’ll kill you! I hate you! I wish the devil

would get hold of you. I’ll kill you some day for this. what do you hit

I for? I cann’t hit _you_ back, you great coward!’



at which there be a great laugh from the other boy, none the less

hilarious when the big lad, look scornfully down upon the little one,

say—



‘I do it for your good, and you ought to be thankful for it.’



Otho snuffle then, but take an early opportunity of lay a crooked

root in an unexpected and obscure spot, over which Michael trip

ignominiously, and nearly bark his shin, when the snuffle become a

joyful chuckle.



later in the same afternoon, Michael Langstroth find himself apart from

the other boy, in a lonely part of the garden, where a broad terrace

end, and rough, uncut grass, dot with wild plant, begin—the top of

the river-bank, in fact. the lad seat himself on this bank, under a

tree, just out of the broiling sun, and a silence and quietness fall

upon he, while he gaze before he into the gurgling, flow river. it

be a pastime he love. Shadowy, half-form thought pass through his

brain at such time, thought as vague as the murmur of the river;

intuition, impulse stir he, whose nature he do not now

understand, but which, for all that, might be not the less blessed and

fruitful in year to come, when he should have forget these, their

first upspringing; for thus it be, as well as in other way, that ‘the

thought of youth be long, long thought.’



the river be the thing which Michael remember long than anything

else. when a babe in his nurse’s arm he have leap at its sudden

shimmer through the tree, and since then its presence have be ever

with he, more or less. it have be his companion and confidant without

his know it. he go unconsciously to its side to think out his young

thought, and it carry all his vague meditation glide down its

stream as it flow between the two fair county of York and Durham. of

course, he be not conscious how potent be its presence in his life; he

would find that out only when he should come to move in other

scene—when he should get man for his companion instead of the stream.



little more remain to be say of they at this time, save that the

mother of the Langstroths and of Otho Askam be both live then,

young and beautiful woman, one of they, at least, wrap up in her

husband and her child. Otho be the only one of the lad who have a

sister, the little Eleanor, three year of age, and so much young than

they that she never share their sport; and they know nothing of she,

save when they see she sometimes walk on the upper terrace, lead by

her nurse or her mother, when she would sometimes stop and look at they

with a pair of great candid eye, and burst into a laugh at some of

their antic. a sturdy-look, not very pretty child, with little

resemblance to Otho in either expression or complexion.









                               CHAPTER I



                             OTHO’S RETURN





it be a dull morning in October, with a gray sky, low-hang cloud,

and muddy lane. the Tees Valley Hunt breakfast that morning at Sir

Thomas Winthrop’s, and the brother, Michael and Gilbert Langstroth,

ride slowly in company towards the house. Michael be now twenty-five,

and Gilbert just turn twenty-three. they have ride and hunt ever

since they have be able to stick on the back of a pony, and, despite

their change fortune—for the house of Langstroth be no more a

flourish house—they ride and hunt still. they feel a deep degree

of interest than usual in this particular breakfast, for it be know to

they, as to all the rest of the neighbourhood, that the long-close

door of Thorsgarth have at last be throw open; Otho Askam’s minority

be over, and he have come, or be on his way, to take possession of the

house of his father, and the abundant revenue and possession which

have be accumulate for he. it be now several month since he have

come of age, but he have not immediately repair to his home. now, Otho

Askam, Michael and Gilbert Langstroth, and their friend Roger Camm, have

all play together as child in the Thorsgarth garden, have shout

through its avenue, chase each other amongst its discolour marble

faun and nymph, and almost succeed, more than once, in drown

themselves in the water of swiftly-rush Tees, who flow beneath the

low terrace of the garden. that have be more than ten year ago, and

many change have take place since then. the Askam fortune have

accumulate; the death of both Mr. and Mrs. Askam have leave their

child—Otho, and a girl, Eleanor, several year young than he—under

the care of guardian, while their property increase. the Langstroths,

on the contrary, have go downhill to a certain extent. poor then, they

have become poor since, till now, Mr. Langstroth, their father, be a

hopeless, helpless invalid; Michael, the elder, be by way of earn

his living as a country doctor, this chance having be give to he by

the kindness of the old family friend and adviser, the little Quaker

Doctor Rowntree, whose assistant Michael be suppose to be. Gilbert

stay at home, tend his father, and devote his distinguished

arithmetical power to an endeavour to extricate the family fortune in

some degree from the confusion into which they have fall. as for Roger

Camm, whose father have be the curate of bradstane-on-Tees, he have

vanish for year past from the scene of his childhood; but he and

Michael, who have be friend in those former day, be friend still,

keep up a close correspondence. and if Roger by any chance imagine

that Gilbert have forget he, he be mistaken. Gilbert Langstroth have

a long memory.



it be not only these brother who look forward with interest to the

possibility of young Askam’s presence at the meet that morning. all the

country-side be more or less agog on the subject. Thorsgarth be a very

considerable house; the Askams be very considerable people in the

neighbourhood. every one be excited; many fair creature have go so

far as to say that they be ‘die to see he,’ die to know what he

be like, and if he be go to be an acquisition, or not, to their

society. People begin to recall thing, and to say to one another, ‘ay,

I remember how his mother use to ride to hound; what a woman she be!

how handsome, and what a temper!’ and then the voice would sink a

little, while for the benefit of some stranger it would be relate how

the late Mrs. Askam have come to her untimely end; how she would go out

one day, despite her husband’s expostulation; how she put her horse at

a certain fence, which he refuse; how she flog he on till he

unwillingly take the leap, and catch his leg in the top rail, pitch

his rider head foremost off he; and how Mrs. Askam be carry home

with a broken neck.



Half-forget thing like these be talk about. amongst all the

wondering and speculation there be little kindness, little personal

feeling. there be no matron who say, ‘ah, his mother and I use to be

great friend; I know I shall like he for her sake.’ for, as a matter

of fact, this reckless young woman, who have so untimely die, have not

make many friend during the year of her married life in Bradstane.

therefore, every one wonder; no one really care what sort of young

man Otho Askam might be.



Michael and Gilbert ride slowly on through the deep lane with their

tangle hedge (for some of the folk thereabout be not as particular

about their clipping as they might have be) in the damp morning air,

and, emerge from the lane, strike the stony road, with its rough

wall, over which they have to travel to arrive at Brigsdale Hall, Sir

Thomas’s place. they be to look at, as goodly a pair of brother as

any in whose company they be likely that day to find themselves.



‘be Magdalen come to the meet?’ ask Gilbert suddenly.



‘yes. she’s drive Mrs. Stamer to it. she’s stay with Miss

Strangforth, and Magdalen don’t know what on earth to do with she.’



‘ah!’ say Gilbert, with his slight, careless smile. ‘a meet must be a

godsend under such circumstance.’ and by and by he make some further

observation, to the effect that it be a picture of a day, and that the

scent would be grand, to which Michael assent cheerfully; and having

get that question settle, they ride up to the hospitable door,

deliver their horse over to a groom, and be show into the

dining-room.



Sir Thomas and his son Byrom be there, and the room be full of man,

old, young, and middle-aged, stand about, wait till their host

should give the signal to be seat.



it be immediately on go into the room that they see Otho Askam—for

he it must be, and no one else—lean his elbow on the shelf of an oak

sideboard, and listen to some remark of a neighbouring squire. he

be at one end of the room as they enter, and they at the other; but

it so happen that there be a little lane or vista from he to they,

so that they see he very plainly.



they look upon a tall young man, as big, as strong, and as broad as

themselves; and there all resemblance end. it would have be

difficult to say whether that face be young or old for its year.

Young Askam have a round, bullet-shape head, a dark complexion, and one

which be also red—a deep, but not yet a coarse red. his forehead,

though narrow, be not devoid of power. his smooth dark hair be clip

close. he wear a slight moustache, a mere line on his upper lip, save

for which his face be hairless, so that the full play of his lip be

see; and there be something fierce in the expression of those lip;

indeed, the whole face be a strange and fierce one. the dark eye be

sullen; the brow have a trick of draw down and together, quickly and

savagely, and then the whole face flush, and the mouth tighten, and

the finger close with a suggestive grip upon whatever might happen to

be in they at the moment. it be truly an angry-look face, devoid of

beauty; and yet, if one come to analyse the feature, it would be find

impossible to pronounce it a plain face. the voice, the manner, be

such as might be expect from the general outward appearance; that be

to say, the voice be abrupt, the sentence curt, and the word often

chop off short in utterance; the manner be brusque, and have a

touch of defiance in they.



at the moment when the Langstroths enter the room, Otho have his eye

fix upon his whip, which he be draw slowly through the finger of

his left hand. he smile, and the smile show a set of very white and

very strong tooth; it be not a gracious or a genial expression. Michael

Langstroth, look at he keenly and attentively, say to himself—



‘Humph! he be a magnificent animal, at any rate. I wonder if he be

anything else. I should not like to pronounce at a venture whether he

be a gentleman or a blackguard; perhaps a bit of both.’



at this juncture, Askam, the curious, sinister smile still on his face,

raise his eye, and encounter those of Michael Langstroth fix upon

he. the smile vanish, the frown descend, above a defiant and

inquire stare. evidently he say within himself, ‘who be that man? I

ought to know he.’



‘Halloa, Michael,’ cry the master of the house, at this point, ‘good

morning to you. I’m glad to see you. how be your father to-day?’



as he listen to this, young Askam’s frown disappear, and his look

clear, as if the thing that puzzle he have be make plain. by the

time that Michael have do talk to Sir Thomas, Otho be at his side.



‘I say—I ought to remember you. you be Michael Langstroth, ben’t you?’



‘yes, I be. I be just come to speak to you. this be my brother

Gilbert; you ought to remember he, too, if you remember I.’



Otho shake hand with they. his countenance be not the well suited for

express pleasure and geniality, but in a certain saturnine manner he

seem glad to see they both, though he do not say he be, but show

it by ask they many question, with an air of interest—question as

to what they have be do ‘all these year.’ and he stand talk with

they, and occasionally with Byrom Winthrop, who join they, until the

voice of Sir Thomas summon they to the table, when, by some mean,

Otho and Gilbert find themselves seat side by side, and Michael be

not very far away from they.



Otho Askam betray none of the awkwardness which would have be

natural to, and excusable in, a very young man, who suddenly find

himself a person of condition and importance amongst other, much old

and much well know than himself. at the same time his manner be

utterly destitute of anything like suavity or grace, or of aught that

could give a clue as to his real habit or taste in the matter of

society; none could discover from it whether he most haunted and good

love drawing-room, study, club, or stable.



he appear to be at his ease, and yet there be nothing easy about he.

he do not laugh at all. Michael, who watch he attentively, could not

detect anything more mirthful than that peculiar smile which have be on

his face when he first see he; and it be a smile which might have be

call sinister.



Gilbert and he seem to keep up an animate conversation, but Michael,

though near, could not hear, for the hum of talk around he, what they

say. he could only feel silently surprised that they have find any

subject in common, for Gilbert, when not engage in calculation, be

something of a bookworm, and love the flavour of a play or an essay,

and be well read in some of our old and less known dramatist.

Michael, though still uncertain whether Otho be most like a gentleman

or a blackguard, have an inner conviction that he be neither literary in

his taste nor yet devoted to account.



suddenly, in a momentary lull in the talk around he, he hear Otho say—



‘but Dusky Beauty be breed in these part. I’d take my oath of it.’



‘of course she be,’ reply Gilbert, with animation. ‘she be breed in

old Trueman’s stable, over in Friarsdale, out of Blue Blood, by——’



here the word be lose in the hum of renew talk, and Michael be no

less lose in astonishment. he feel quite feeble and bewilder with

surprise. in all the year that he have know his brother, he have never

at the end of the day, towards five in the afternoon, it come to pass

after Otho Askam’s arrival, which be, as it be, make public by this

the Bradstane neighbour—people in district like that be neighbour if

                               CHAPTER II



                     MAGDALEN—and the NEIGHBOURHOOD





Otho’s arrival have be in the early part of October. the intimacy

it certainly be a very quiet little town, as it be now, and probably

always have be.



‘Castle Bradstane,’ say an old chronicler, ‘standeth stately upon

Tese.’ at the time of which he write, he probably literally mean the

castle, the grim brown pile which stand on the Durham side of the

stream, cunningly plant just at an outward sweep of one of its many

curve. gradually it have fall into decay; other house and a small

town have gather about its foot. Ivy and other creeper and climber

now clung about its fierce old tower. wallflower, ragwort, and the

ivy-leave snapdragon peep and nod in at the narrow little slit of

window; kindly nature do all in her power to beautify what have be so

cruel and so hideous, till now the grim old fastness sit harmless aloft,

and the river rush and murmur far below, as of yore.



any one who choose, may learn how Walter Scott, with the seer’s eye of

genius, picture Bradstane Castle, and the prospect which from its

‘watch-tower high gleam gradual on the warder’s eye;’ and to this day,

the prospect upon which it look be little change. though the stream

sweep by beneath it, laden with the tale of several century more,

their woe and bloodshed, grief and tragic story, yet the outline of the

land itself, the wood, the hill, must be similar to what they be

stately upon Tese.’ the inhabitant, who gradually build house, and

have their great sin, their great fault, wrong, and iniquity; and

we be very much in the habit of speak in condemnatory term of they,

and of laud the beauty of the country, and the simpleness and

gentleness, and, above all, the naturalness and absence of pretension in

the life there. and, certainly, city life, carry to excess, have in it

a morbid feverishness and unrest which be no true life. but in country

life, when it be live in out-of-the-way spot—moorland farm, seclude

dale, place far from railway and traffic—there be often a certain

morbidness, as well as in the life of a town. the very solitude and

loneliness tend to foster and bring out any peculiarity, any morbid

characteristic, and to confirm and strengthen eccentricity and

idiosyncrasy. one of the good thing that much-abuse progress will do

in time, will be to sweep away some of these ugly old country habit of

indolence and cloddishness, and selfish, soulless sensuality, which

still exist, and that sometimes amidst the sweetest and most exquisite

natural surrounding.



at this later time of which I write, Bradstane be more the abode of

‘ten, be a month last Sunday.’

‘no.’

Magdalen proceed, in a languid, indifferent way, to draw she out. in a

the Dixons be well-to-do, prosperous, conventional tradespeople, more

charge sixpence where other people charge fivepence, by a consistent

there be other well-to-do tradesman in Bradstane, and many who be

but ill-to-do. there be the lawyer, Mr. Coningsby, who live not far

tunnel, or a narrow, dark passage—and look down it, one could see the

rush brown water flow ceaselessly on, without haste and without

rest, from the fastness whence they have spring—



               ‘where tee in tumult leave his source,

               thunder o’er Cauldron and High Force.’



such be, superficially, the outward aspect of Bradstane town, when Otho

such it have be for year back. it be not what be call ‘a grow
