exterior, objective side, be not likely to change very much.









                              CHAPTER III



                           LANGSTROTH’S FOLLY





one November evening, or rather, late in the afternoon, Otho have drop

in at the Red Gables, where he have find Gilbert and his father. Mr.

Langstroth receive the young man with urbanity; he have all along seem

satisfied with Gilbert’s new friend. Gilbert himself look up from his

desk, and greet the visitor tranquilly.



‘sit down, and make yourself at home,’ say he, push a tobacco-jar

towards Askam.



but Otho do not at once sit down. ‘will you come home and dine with

I?’ he ask, in his curt way.



‘I’m sorry I cann’t,’ Gilbert say, polite as usual. ‘you see these

paper? I have more than an hour’s work upon they yet.’



Otho never scoff at Gilbert’s ‘business,’ though he be ready to sneer

at that of any one else. all he utter now be a disappointed ‘Humph!’



‘stay and have dinner with we,’ say Gilbert. ‘how do you come?’



‘I ride.’



‘from home? on your way anywhere?’



‘no. I’m on my way from Balder Hall,’ reply Otho, with something like

a scowl.



Gilbert look at he, carelessly, it seem. then he say—



‘well, send your horse round, and stay, as I say—I want Askam to have

dinner with we,’ he add, turn to his father.



‘I wish he would. we shall be delighted, if he will take we as we be,’

respond Mr. Langstroth.



Otho still seem to hesitate a little, till Gilbert, with a rather

steady look at he, which be not see by his father, continue—



‘look here. I’ll propose something else. I’ve be tie down to this

work all day, and I haven’t have a turn out of door. Dine with we, as I

say, and afterwards I’ll walk back with you to your house. I have an

errand in the town. it’ll do you no harm to travel on your own leg for

once in a way, and you can send one of your fellow for your horse. how

will that do?’



Otho’s brow clear. ‘that will do very well,’ say he, take a chair.

‘it suit I down to the ground. get on with that work, and I’ll talk to

your father.’



Gilbert, have rung and give his order as to the accommodation of

Otho’s horse, turn his back upon they, and do not address another

word to they until the man announce dinner, when he put his paper in a

drawer which he lock, and give his arm to his father to support he to

the dining-room. Otho follow they. despite the poverty of the house of

Langstroth, the meal there be always rather choice, well cook, and

well serve. Mr. Langstroth, it be understand, depend a good deal for

his health of mind as well as of body upon the due observance of such

thing. soon after they have begin, Gilbert observe carelessly that they

haven’t see Michael all day; he have expect he to dinner.



‘he’s dining at Balder Hall,’ say Otho, even more curtly than usual.



‘ah! have he arrive when you leave?’



‘no.’



‘and how be my future sister-in-law?’



‘she say she be all right,’ be the gruff reply, as Otho fix his

eye for a moment upon Gilbert, a little defiantly, one might almost

have say. nothing more be say about any of these topic—Balder Hall,

or Michael, or Magdalen. when dinner be over, and they have go back to

the library, Gilbert settle his father with the great care,

arrange with his own hand his easy-chair, small table, reading-lamp,

and all his other requisite.



‘you willn’t mind my leave you for an hour or two?’ he ask.



‘not at all, Gilbert. you want some air and exercise. go and get it.’



‘would you like I to ask the doctor to call in?’



‘no, no,’ be the somewhat testy reply. ‘I see he often enough, without

you ask he to come.’



‘Michael be sure to look in on his way home, but I shall most likely be

back by then.—Now, Otho, if you’re ready.’



as they step out of the house, they become aware that a change have

fall over the weather, which have be cold. the sky be full of rack,

drive rapidly across it by a strong yet soft south-west wind. the moon

gleam fitfully through the cloud, and a gush of rain be blow

against their face.



‘Halloa! rain!’ exclaim Gilbert. ‘do you mind a drop of rain?’ he

add, ‘or will you ride home?’



‘oh, I’m not afraid of a little weather,’ reply Otho. ‘where do you

want to go?’



‘where you have never be yet,’ say his companion. ‘down to the

Townend, as they call it. come along. it ben’t much out of our way to

Thorsgarth.’



Otho follow in a docile manner. now that he have get what he seem to

have be aim at, his _tête-à-tête_ with Gilbert, all trace of

sullenness and impatience have vanish. bulldog, surly to all the world

beside, be tame and obedient to their master; and there be a good

deal of the bulldog in the way in which Otho follow Gilbert about.



when they have get through the busy and most inhabited part of the

town, they find themselves almost alone in a steep street, descend

rapidly towards the river. as they get far down it, the house

gradually become more bare and rough-looking; and, some of they, more

and more ancient in appearance. look down the hill, it appear as if

the street end in a _cul de sac_, as if there be no egress that way

from Bradstane town. and the wall which appear to shut the place in,

and block up the road at that side, consist of the frontage of two

high factory. there be in reality a narrow passage between they,

through which access be obtain to the river, and by mean of which

one arrive at an iron footbridge, ugly, but useful. this could not be

perceive at the distance they now be from the mill.



‘what on earth do you want down here?’ growl Otho, between two puff

at his pipe.



‘we possess a bit of property down there,’ Gilbert answer he. ‘it be

perfectly meaningless and perfectly useless to we. it cumber the

ground, and have swallow up a pot of money which we ought to be

enjoy the benefit of now. I sometimes walk down to it, to look at it,

and think what a folly it be. “Langstroth’s Folly,” it ought to be

call. Townend Mills be the name it actually bear. there it be!’ as

the moon shine out brightly for a few minute, and show the dark mass

of the factory rise almost directly in front of they.



‘you’re a queer one!’ say Otho, not without a kind of admiration in his

tone. ‘where’s the sense of fret yourself by come and look at

it? it’s like try to heal a raw by scrape.’



‘your simile would be just, if I do irritate myself,’ reply Gilbert,

gently. ‘my dear Otho’—he speak impressively, and lay his hand for a

moment on the other’s arm—‘I never let anything irritate I. I make it a

rule——’



‘never—I never say never,’ say Otho. ‘no say what will turn up.

leave it to chance. that’s the good way. besides, Mag—some one be

say to I, only the other day, that it’s only very young people who

_never_ do what they oughtn’t.’



it be on the tip of Gilbert’s tongue to say, ‘I know I be young, but,

then, I have take care to be very wise, too,’ because we be apt to

blurt out the thought near our heart. but he say quietly—



‘yes, I know I be young, but I have have a good deal to do that generally

fall to old people. with Michael choose to leave we and take his

own way, I have have a good deal to think about, and a good deal of help

to give to my poor governor in his business affair; and I soon find

that, if you want to get on at all in business, you _must_ keep your

temper, especially when you be a poor man, with fallen fortune,

against the world——’



‘be hang if I could ever keep my temper about business, or anything

else that go wrong with I!’



‘ah, you can afford to lose your temper,’ say Gilbert, in a cold voice,

which cause Otho hastily to say that he have mean no offence; and

Gilbert proceed—



‘so, as I say, I don’t let the Townend Mills irritate I, though one

might get irritated enough about they if one would; but I come and smoke

my pipe, and walk round they now and again, and think quietly. I feel as

if I might, some time or other, have a good idea on the subject, you

know—an idea that might be work into something. don’t you trouble

yourself about they. I willn’t detain you long. here we be!’



they have enter the long, narrow passage between the mill. it be now

late, get near ten o’clock, for they have not leave the Red Gables

till after nine. the clouded sky make the night darker—a darkness which

be deepen, if anything, by the occasional gleam of moonlight when

the rack part. at the end of the passage there be visible a kind of

gray shimmer, and in the interval between the gust of wind they could

hear the rush of the river.



‘wherever one go, one come upon that river,’ exclaim Otho, not as

if he be much delighted with the fact.



‘yes. tee keep we pretty well aware of his presence. it’s as twisted

and crook a stream as any in England, I should imagine. there be the

mill, Askam. now, I’ll tell you my object in life, if you like.’



‘what be it?’ ask Otho, with deep and unfeigned interest.



‘I wish—at least, I intend to overcome the obstacle raise in my way by

the idiot who build these mill. I like overcome obstacle. I intend,

some day, either to have they sell, and the price of they in my pocket,

or else to see they fill with machinery, and work again at a

profit.’



‘but you don’t understand how to manage mill,’ say Otho diffidently.



‘no, but I understand how to manage man. and I know a fellow who

understand how to manage mill—Roger Camm. do you remember Roger Camm?

he use to be a playfellow of our—the curate’s son.’



‘a swarthy fellow, very big and strong, who always look rather hungry,

and yet always say he ben’t when we use to go in to tea?’



‘the same. I see you have an accurate memory. I guess he be hungry too,

poor beggar. he be over here, a year or two ago, stop with Michael;

they be great chum. and he tell I all about himself. he cut the

Church. he say his governor never get anything out of it but

water-porridge and civil contempt from people who ben’t as good as

himself. he be rather bitter about it. anyhow, he cut it, as I say, and

take to the intelligent working-man line. he be foreman in a Manchester

factory now, and he know something about it all, I can tell you. I make

he promise that when I send for he he’d come and take the management

of this concern—“run it” for I, as they say in America.’



‘ah, and when will that be?’



‘when I find my purchaser or tenant,’ say Gilbert, as suavely as ever.

‘he tell I all the reason why these would never succeed as cotton

factory—they be the only mill in the place; the station be a mile

and a half away, and there be a steep hill, nearly half a mile long,

from here to the top of the town. oh, I’ve master the subject.

Jute—that be what I shall do with they—spin jute, and get woman and

girl out of Bridge Street for hand.’



‘yes?’ say Otho, tentatively, really interested, and ardently wish

that he understand a little more about it. ‘and your father

and—brother?’ Michael’s name seem rather to stick in his throat.



‘my father say he only wish I could. Michael be dead against it.

Michael would like to pull the whole place down.’



‘what for?’ ask Otho, sharply.



‘because he’s a fool,’ be Gilbert’s reply. the intimacy between he and

Otho have, it would seem, progress quickly.



‘because he’s a fool,’ repeat young Askam, lean his elbow on the

balustrade of the bridge, to which they have now advance, and stare

down into the rush brown river. the expression on the face, which the

darkness conceal, be not a pleasant one. ‘curse he!’ he mutter to

himself, so low that even Gilbert do not hear he; but the river

carry the sound, along with all the other message with which it be

laden, towards the sea.



‘come along!’ say Gilbert, after a brief, silent pause. ‘there’s no use

stay here any long.’



Otho raise himself from the bridge, and they retrace their way through

the silent passage, up the steep street, and to where a road to the

right lead in the direction of Thorsgarth. they have not speak a word

since leave the mill.



‘I think it’s rather late for I to be go with you,’ say Gilbert,

hesitate at the corner.



‘not a bit! what’s ten o’clock? you’ve get a key, I suppose? you say

you would come,’ say Otho, rapidly, and almost savagely. ‘and I want to

speak to you.’



‘oh, I be willing, and—well, Michael will see my father again before he

go to bed—sure to. he will be leave Balder Hall by now, I daresay.

they keep early hour there.’



‘where there’s an old woman like that precious Aunt Martha, they must,’

say Otho. ‘look here, Gilbert, how do your brother Michael get

Magdalen Wynter to accept he?’



‘by be the only man in the world who propose to she, or be likely

to do so,’ say Gilbert, cynically.



‘I don’t see that. she be the handsome woman I ever see.’



‘she haven’t a penny, and willn’t have. she ben’t popular—but the reverse,

and no man, except Michael, ever penetrate within those wall—oh, and

you,’ he add, with a laugh, as he turn to Otho.



‘you haven’t account for it yet,’ say the latter, sullenly.



‘well, say she be in love with he.’



‘in love with my eye!’



Gilbert laugh again. ‘I give it up,’ say he. ‘it’s a conundrum I have

often set myself, to no purpose. Michael be ten thousand time too good

for she; but that’s nothing to the point. I don’t know why she take

he.’



‘she order I off this afternoon, because he be come to dinner,’

Otho say, in a voice of choke anger. ‘she tell I my whole body

ben’t worth his little finger. she——’



‘you might be in love with her yourself,’ suggest Gilbert; and,

indeed, a less astute observer might have be strike with the same

idea.



‘I’ll be hang if I be—insolent minx!’ retort Otho, savagely. ‘no

girl shall behave to I as she have do, with impunity. she shall pay

for it. but, tell I, how long be they in make it up?’



‘oh, not long; about six week. he be home for his holiday one summer,

and we be talk together in front of the house. Miss Strangforth’s

carriage, with she and Magdalen in it, drive by. the old lady see we

bow, and stop. I introduce Michael; he fall in love on the spot,

then and there, over head and ear. Martha ask he to drive with they,

and he drive. drive deeply and deep into love, I suppose; and—yes, it

be just six week later, they be together at a picnic to Cauldron,

and they return engage. my father have never get over it.’



‘how?’ ask Otho, in the same strangle voice.



‘he think it so idiotic and imprudent. and so it be, and be. but

Michael have become a man over the doing of it. they stick to it, and

they have stick to it ever since. some day, I suppose they will be

marry; but I don’t know when.’



Otho make absolutely no reply to this prophecy. they turn in at the

Thorsgarth gate, and the subject be drop. but Gilbert know now why

Otho have give they his company at dinner, and why he himself have be

so earnestly pressed to go back to Thorsgarth after their walk.









                               CHAPTER IV



                    the FACULTY of CLOSE OBSERVATION





one night, during the winter which follow the conversation between

Otho and Gilbert, a large ball be give at a well-know house in the

neighbourhood of Bradstane, and present at it be both the Langstroth

brother, Magdalen Wynter, and even Otho Askam, little as he love such

entertainment. perhaps Gilbert have persuade he to go.



Magdalen be chaperone by a good-natured matron, who have marry off

all her own girl with credit and renown, and could therefore afford to

witness with complacent amusement the gasping and stuggling of those

who be still, as Otho might elegantly have put it, ‘in the running.’

she have not that dislike to Magdalen which animate more interested

person; she admire her beauty, and consider she ‘good form.’

Magdalen herself have never look well than she do that night, or

more haughtily and superbly independent of all outside support. she be

richly attire, for Miss Strangforth like her niece to dress

splendidly. she dance very seldom; it have never be her habit to do so

often; and as not even her rival, while in possession of their sense,

would have dream of say that this be because she could not get

partner, and as she sit out usually involve also the sit out

of some man who would otherwise have be free to dance with another

girl, and as the say man always look perfectly happy and satisfied in

their inactivity at such times,—her habit do not in any way make she

more popular.



to-night it be observe that she dance twice with she betrothed, twice

with Otho Askam, and once with Gilbert. perhaps that might have be

endure without much adverse criticism, but it be notice also, and

bitterly notice, that Otho dance with no one else, though both Michael

and Gilbert do.



on the following afternoon, Gilbert, return from a solitary,

meditative ride, far into the country—such a ride as he love to take,

and do take, almost every day—find himself outside the paling at one

side of the Balder Hall Park. look over they, he see within the

figure of Magdalen Wynter. she be pace quickly up and down a sort of

woodland path, which in summer must have be almost conceal, but

which be now plainly visible between the trunk of the naked

tree—visible, at any rate, to Gilbert as he sit on horseback. there be

a broad belt of rough grass, in which grow fern, and from which also

rise the leafless tree just speak of. then come the path on which Miss

Wynter be walk, and beyond that a glade, slope steeply down to

where tee flow by in one of his many curve.



Gilbert see a dark, close cap of velvet, and a pale face which droop

somewhat beneath it, a long, fur-border mantle tightly clip around

the wearer’s form, the bottom of a crimson kilting peep beneath it,

and a pair of small, well-shod foot. her back be turn to he, and he

stop and look over the paling till she turn, lift her head,

and see he. she give a little start.



‘oh, Gilbert, how quietly you must have come!’



‘not so very. the ground be hard and frosty, and my horse’s hoof ring.

it be your deep thought, Magdalen, which render you deaf to outside

thing.’



she have walk across the grass, which be dry and hard, and crunch

frostily under her foot, up to the pale, and hold up her hand to he.

Gilbert rarely meet his future sister-in-law, and it have be see that

in speak of she to his friend, Otho Askam, he do not employ term

exactly of enthusiasm; but if ever they do encounter each other,

whether by chance or design, he be always scrupulously amiable and

polite to she. whether this meeting have come about by chance—whether he

have intend that it should come about—this be a thing know only to

himself. as he look down now, into the marble paleness and wonderful

beauty of the girl’s face, he give no sign, and she say to he—



‘be you ride alone?’



‘yes, just as you see I. I have be a long way—nearly to

Middleton-in-Teesdale.’



‘I have be walk for an hour, up and down this path. I be begin

to find it rather monotonous, and be go in for some tea. will you

come and have some, too?’



‘with pleasure, if you can put up with such a feeble substitute for my

brother. I think the North Lodge be just round here, ben’t it?’



‘yes. I will meet you there. then we can walk to the house together.’



they do so, Gilbert dismount at the lodge, and lead his horse.

the short winter day be close in, gray and cold, as they go up the

avenue.



‘how do you enjoy the dance?’ he ask.



‘not much. I never do. do you?’



‘I always enjoy watch other people’s little game.’



she give a short laugh. ‘do you mean mine?’



‘your—nay. how can you have any?’



‘just what I be go to say. I mean, if you be look for what you

call “game” with I, your trouble must have be waste, that’s all.’



‘of course. no; I mean all the other girl, and their mother, and the

man, too, for that matter.’



she laugh again, shortly and contemptuously.



‘and Otho Askam,’ he pursue tranquilly.



Magdalen look up. ‘what? have he get plan, or “game,” as you call

they?’



‘I be amuse to see his devotion to you last night, and what a rage

those woman be in about it. his game be to avoid all the girl whom he

might possibly be suppose to be desirous of marry. he tell I so. he

be mortally afraid of be trap. and of course, he be even more

afraid of the mother than of the girl. you be quite harmless, you

see. you be promise to Michael. he feel so safe and happy with you.’



‘poor, innocent lamb!’



‘ben’t he? it show how blindly he trust in your probity, and in your

devotion to Michael. he come to Balder Hall sometimes, don’t he?’



‘yes.’



‘he never ask I to go with he.’



‘no?’



‘it be so amusing, I think. what do Michael say to it?’



‘Michael—oh, he laugh, and say it be very good of I to let he come,

and that it be a good sign for Mr. Askam’s future career that he

frequent any decent society at all,’ she say, with a short, dry laugh,

of which Gilbert’s answer one seem an echo, so much be they alike

in tone.



‘how beautiful of he! when you be married to he, Magdalen,’ he add,

speak very slowly, and openly watch she face—‘when you be marry

to Michael, and fairly establish as Mrs. Langstroth, for which

consummation you have wait so faithfully and so patiently,’—he dwelt

upon all his words—‘I should say that then Michael would find it rather

a bore to have Otho Askam come in, and you would, too. don’t you think

so?’



‘how can I tell? I should say that Otho Askam would find it a bore

himself, when I be married to Michael, if ever I should be. as you say,

I have wait a long time, and I may have a long one yet to wait,

before I be Michael’s wife.’



she speak with a dead monotony of tone, and a no less monotonous

expression in her face. they stand now in front of the house. Magdalen

beckon to a gardener’s boy, and tell he to send a groom for Gilbert’s

horse, after which they go into the house, into Magdalen’s

sitting-room, and she cast off her fur cloak, and begin to make tea,

with the firelight shine on her crimson gown. Gilbert sit in a low

chair and watch she, but say nothing. only when she hand he his

cup of tea, he say softly—



‘Magdalen, I do wish you and Michael could be married to-morrow.’



‘thank you.’



‘then your life would be bright.’



‘who tell you it be dull?’



‘all your action and word tell I so.’



‘you and I be what they call quiet people,’ she remark. ‘not

impressionable, and all that kind of thing.’



‘I believe that be the general opinion of our character.’



‘well, and people also seem to think that such creature—pachydermatous,

don’t they call animal with thick skin——’



‘have Michael be lend you some science primer to while away the

time in the winter evening? what a happy thought of his!’



‘you haven’t answer my question.’



‘they do—pachydermatous be the word.’



‘they seem to think that, because we be not all on fire, and jerk

about, for nothing, we can do without any excitement at all.’



‘I have observe the existence of the delusion you speak of. yes, thank

you, I will have some more cake.’



‘I don’t know what you think, but I feel to want more excitement than

most people, and I get less. last night, if I could have be sure that

Michael would not have misunderstood I, I would have dance every dance

with Otho Askam, if the result have be that not a woman would have

speak to I at the end of the evening. that’s the kind of feel I

have.’



‘I can quite understand it. I wish you would have try the

experiment—say with I.’



‘that would not have be at all the same thing. you be a very good

young man, and Otho Askam be consider rather a bad one.’



‘Michael be the good of we all.’



‘it be not that I like bad people, but I like to sing in a different key

from that use by all the rest. I should like to see they all look as

if the world be come to an end.... by the way, Gilbert, be you such

a very good young man? they say you be too great a friend of that timid

creature we have be talk about, who only dance with engaged girl,

to be very good.’



Gilbert start—within himself, not outwardly, stir his tea, and say

carelessly—



‘perhaps I cultivate he for reason like your own—because I be dull,

and it make people vex.’



‘perhaps. he be very rich, of course. Gilbert, you have wish I such

good wish about Michael, it be only fair that I should wish you well

in return. I wish Otho Askam would relieve you of those factory that I

have hear you speak of. then there would be more money in your coffer,

and perhaps more chance of that marriage come off, of which you have

be speak so kindly.’



‘you be very good,’ say Gilbert, lay down his empty cup; ‘but

gentleman be not in the habit of twist their friend’ pocket inside

out, for their own advantage.’



‘oh no! but if the friend have a lean towards commercial enterprise—a

speculative spirit. it would be an amusement for he.’



‘_if_ he have. but you know as well as I do that his taste be not of

that kind, but of the turf, turfy.’



Magdalen smile, and say, ‘I know that his taste be for anything

exciting, anything highly flavour. what will you wager that he be

tired of the turf in a year from now?’



‘nothing at all. if I do wager on that subject, I would wager to the

contrary.’



‘well, I think his superfluous cash would be more respectably employ

in set your factory go.’



here a loud ring sound through the house.



‘I should not wonder if that be Michael call,’ say Magdalen, and

she speak in hurried tone. ‘remember, Gilbert, not a word of this. I

feel well for speak to you; and Michael be good, oh, so much well

than any of we! and he have care of his own. and you will be my brother,

some day. do you understand?’



‘my dear Magdalen, of course! do not distress yourself, pray. there be

no need,’ he assure she, as Michael enter.



‘why, Gilbert, that be well,’ say he, with a look of great pleasure. ‘I

have often wish that you could spare time to ride out and have a chat

with Magdalen now and then. where do you meet?’



Michael sit half an hour, and then the brother ride home in company.

Magdalen, when the two young man have leave she, sit for a long time over

the fire, gaze into its glow, her elbow prop on her knee.



‘gilbert be very observant—remarkably observant,’ she think to

herself. ‘who would have think that he would see so quickly, and

Michael be so blind? and yet again, Gilbert see, but see only to

dissect—without any feeling, unless it be a feeling of pleasure in

show one his power. Michael do not see, but if he do he would

sympathise. he be grand—at least, he would be if he be awake. with all

his love for I, I have not be able to awaken he. his time be yet to

come. sympathise—yes; but what be sympathy? he cann’t give I what I

want. here be I, beautiful, yes, very beautiful, and very strong, and

with some brain in my head, though they all think I have none. and I

have to live, to vegetate, that is, as if I be some wear-out old

woman, as if I be my own great-aunt. it be horrible, horrible, and I

do not know how long I shall be able to bear it.’



a dreary blank seem to open before her mind’s eye, and still she sit

motionless, stare into the fire.



‘Michael be my lover—he do love I, too. he be the only friend I have,

for no one be fond of I. if they be kind to I, and really care for

I, I would not take their Otho Askam away from they. I wonder if they

know what he be, this creature that they make such a fuss about! perhaps

there would be no fuss if he be dance attendance on any one but

I—fuss, of course there would be no fuss. Gilbert and I know what he

be. he have not be able to conceal his miserableness from we. and we

know that he himself—the man—be not worth fight for. but I do not

mean to let they have he, all the same. it amuse I to keep he, and

to enrage they. and I shall go on amuse myself in that way. Michael be

very good, but he be not—amusing. if I be married to he, I wonder if

I should find it as dull as I do be engage to he. surely not.

but——’



here Miss Strangforth’s maid come in, and say her mistress be awake,

and be go to have a cup of tea, and would be glad if Miss Wynter

would go to she. Magdalen go instantly, and whatever the state of her

own heart, she do not let her great-aunt feel dull while she sit with

she.









                               CHAPTER V



                         GILBERT’S CAUTIOUSNESS





as the young man ride homeward, Michael again express his pleasure at

Gilbert’s visit to Balder Hall. Gilbert, for his part, be meditative

and rather silent during the first part of their ride, but be presently

rouse into animation by a remark of Michael’s. some day before,

Gilbert have be expound to Michael, as he be now and then in the

habit of do, just so much as he think fit for he to know of his

financial arrangement and scheme for the future. he have inform his

brother that the estate be be very gradually retrieve, that he,

Gilbert, begin to see daylight—a first glimmer, through the obscurity.

all his plan, he say, be work well, except one, which, if he

could only accomplish it, would give an impetus to everything else, and

shorten his work by year; and that one be, of course, the sale or

letting of the Townend factory. he could not sell they: he could not

find any capitalist to work they.



Gilbert have be very much in earnest when he speak—in his way of be

in earnest, that is, not vehemently, but gently. he speak of the mill,

even of the trouble they give he, with respect—a respect which he would

have accord to no other topic or kind of topic under the sun.

consequently, it have jar on his mood when Michael, lightly flick

his boot with his riding-whip (for he have look in at the Red Gables on

his way from his daily round), and glance round the room as he speak

with an absent look, ask—



‘then, have we no capital now?’



Gilbert look at he, almost sharply at first, and then with a patient

expression, like that of a conscientious teacher try to instil some

branch of knowledge into a peculiarly dense pupil.



‘not a quarter enough to set the mill agoe,’ he say. ‘and if we have,

it be too risky a venture for capital like ours, that have be snatch,

as it be, out of the gulf it have be fling into.’



‘but if it be too risky for our capital, surely it be too risky for that

of a stranger.’



‘that do not follow at all, as any business man could tell you. it

do not follow that because it would be risky for our capital, it would

also be the same for that of a stranger; it would entirely depend upon

who the stranger might be, and what the extent of his possession.’



to this Michael have make very little reply. Gilbert imagine that he have

forget it, but be undeceive as they now ride together in the winter

moonlight. it be yet early, but dark, save for the clear,

frosty-look crescent in the sky.



‘I meet Sir Thomas Winthrop this afternoon,’ observe Michael. ‘we ride

together for a little while, and we be talk about those factory.

Sir Thomas say he wonder my father do not pull they both down. the

land would sell fast enough, without they, for building, and they be in

want of cottage down there.’



there be a slight pause. then—



‘I daresay Sir Thomas Winthrop do wonder,’ say Gilbert, go

perfectly white with anger. ‘he would give the world to buy the plot

himself and build cottage on it for his farm-labourer and people. do

he think I be a fool?’



‘he never mention your name at all. it be my father of whom he be

speak, to whom the property belong,’ say Michael, a shade of

reserve in his tone, for it be quite true, and have strike even he more

than once, that Gilbert have a way of speak of their estate as though

it be not only manage, but own, by himself. Michael trust and

believe in he implicitly, but be not prepared to be so sharply take

up.



‘he be a meddlesome old imbecile, and I would thank he to mind his own

business,’ say Gilbert, who have somewhat recover his composure.

‘Michael, do you trust I, or do you not?’



as he speak, he almost pull up, and look his brother full in the

face. Gilbert’s countenance, at this period, be an old countenance

than that of Michael. his brow have already get the first coating, as it

be, of the network of little fine wrinkle which afterwards completely

cover it.



‘trust you? why, of course,’ say Michael, almost impatiently.



‘then, hearken to a word of advice. do not let Sir Thomas Winthrop, or

Sir Thomas anybody, even speak to you of our affair. I know what I be

about when I say so. do you think I’d discuss with any outsider the way

you treat a patient? I should know that you know a hundred time more

about such thing than I do, even if I might not suppose you

infallible. and if you trust I to be do the good for we all, you

must not discuss what I be do or not do, with any mortal soul.’



‘I tell you I be not discuss it,’ say Michael, his dark brow

draw together. ‘Sir Thomas begin. I meet he as I be ride from——’



‘Sir Thomas be d—d!’ say Gilbert, so heartily, and with such intense

emphasis, that Michael stare at he. this anger, passion, and violent

language, belong to a phase in his brother’s character of which he have

scarcely suspect the existence. this sudden display might have put a

suspicious man on the alert, but Michael Langstroth be not suspicious;

and, moreover, he be one of those who, while they can fight the world

well enough, can oppose an iron front to their enemy, and treat their

detractor with careless scorn, be very tender, very weak, very

sensitive where their friend and those they love be concern. he see

only that Gilbert be vex, and feel only that he be sorry to have

be the one to vex he. so to change the subject, he say—



‘well, I should be glad enough to see the factory work again; but I

must say I wish I have a couple of thousand to start with. I would be

married to-morrow.’



Gilbert, who have other view for his thousand than, to use his own

phrase, ‘to give they to Magdalen Wynter to buy furniture with,’ feel in

his secret soul that love must make any man small; that it might make

even a generous man selfish.



‘what interest could you pay?’ he ask.



Michael shrug his shoulder, know no reply to that question; and

Gilbert, in the tone of a tutor, who be master of his subject,

harangue a pupil who do not know its A B C, go on: ‘you be my

brother, and, of course, I would like to help you first, if I could; but

we cannot afford it, Michael. we must wait. it be our only course.

marriage must wait, and prosperity must wait. to hand you out a couple

of thousand now, would mean to throw our affair back for year; and as

for my father and I——’



‘oh, of course, I be joke,’ say Michael carelessly. ‘I know there be

no royal road to that kind of thing, but only hard work, and plenty of

it.’



he speak as if he consider the subject at an end, and they ride the

rest of the way in silence. Gilbert’s mind be busy, and his indignation

active in that he have such a mean-minded brother.



‘I verily believe he would accept the situation of overseer to the

parish pump, if it should give he fifty pound a year, and bring he

any nearer be married to that doll,’ he think; and this sarcasm

be, as it be, the froth or scum throw to the surface by an anger, a

fear, and an emotion which be at that time the deep thing he could

feel, and of which it be no more the adequate measure than a yard-stick

would be adequate for measure an ocean. and afterwards, when this

first ebullition of feeling be over, he fall to brood over the

matter in a way which be inevitable from his nature and temperament, as

well as from his upbringing, and the line in which his life have be

cast.



‘what will become of my work,’ he ask himself, as he often have ask

himself lately, ‘if my father be to die, as he might, any day? if he

be to die, and everything be to be divide! all that I have scrape

together with such toil, for so many year. one half of it as good as

fling into the gutter. where would my wage be then? Michael be not fit

to have control over money which have be earn by some one else. he

do not understand the subject, and never will. he would take his

share, marry that girl—if she would have he—and leave I with my life

to begin over again. as for the factory, if he be fool enough to

listen to Sir Thomas Winthrop, and repeat what he say, as if it be

something worth think about—why, if he can do that, he be capable of

follow out Sir Thomas’s idea too. it be enough to disgust any man,

and discourage he from anything like real work,’ Gilbert go on to

himself, ‘to think he have so precarious a hold as I have upon thing

which would not be exist now, but for his devotion. one ought to have

some more secure prospect, if only to give one a little heart in one’s

exertion.’



long after they have part and go their separate way, Gilbert be

silent, revolve this problem in his mind; and the more he think

about it, the big and uglier it grow.



‘Michael care for nothing but to gratify his own wish and impulse,’

Gilbert think, darkly, feel that this tendency of Michael’s

interfere disagreeably with certain plan and project of his own,

which he do not recognise as proceed from the same source.



after that, the conversation between they on such matter grow ever

rare and less expansive. Michael do not dwell on the matter, and, if

he have think about it, would have be too proud to allude to it after

Gilbert have ask he whether he trust he; and something, whether

pride or another feeling, hinder Gilbert from open out. every day

he grow more sedate, and his brow become grayer and more covered with

its network of little fine wrinkle.









                               CHAPTER VI



                      GILBERT’S ‘COUP DE THEATRE’





towards the end of every hunting season, those man in Bradstane and its

vicinity who belong to the institution know as the Tees Valley Hunt,

be in the habit of meeting at the King’s arm in Bradstane, and there

partake together of a luncheon, at which Sir Thomas Winthrop, the

master, preside, and after which he read out the statistic of the past

season, and lay before the assemble company any propose new

arrangement for the following year. nothing be decide then; a regular

meeting be call, to be hold a week later, in which the affair be

discuss in earnest, and real business be do. it have come to pass

with the lapse of year, that the gathering have become a very sociable

one, dear to the heart of those who partook in it; and they would not

have give it up on any account.



this luncheon usually take place in the beginning of March, and be

often a good deal talk about before it come off. it have be December

when the meeting take place between Magdalen and Gilbert, during which

each have silently give credit to the other for much keenness and

acuteness of observation. it have be cold and inclement then, and a

long bleak winter have follow, during which the interview have not be

repeat—at least, no such interview as that. it may be that Gilbert have

many a time ride over the wild road lead from Bradstane to

Middleton-in-Teesdale, for it be his habit daily to take a long walk or

a long ride. he may have travel over this road, solitary and sedate,

as his willnot and humour be, his lip move now and then, when he feel

himself to be quite alone on the silent road, as if he whisper to

himself endless calculation, but never too absorb to recognise an

acquaintance and acknowledge he if he meet he—never too abstract to

know his own whereabout amidst the moor and common, or intricate

cross country road.



and it be more than probable that Magdalen, on her part, have many a

dozen time pace that woodland path on which Gilbert have find she,

try, by the regular mechanical motion which, in her own mind, she

compare with that of a treadmill, to grind down or pace out some of the

suppress savageness and discontent which gnaw her soul. this walk

to and fro be almost her only mode of take outdoor exercise. with all

her veiled eagerness, her bitter sense of the consume dulness of her

life, she never leave the Balder Hall ground on foot, never seek any

companionship with outside thing or people. for she there be no long

ramble, no casual, friendly greeting with farm or cottage folk whom she

might see on the way.



this seclusion on her part be a subject on which she and Michael have

occasional difference of opinion, which could hardly be call

dispute, since Magdalen be in the habit of yield the field at once

to Michael in the matter of argument, merely tell he that no doubt

he be quite right, and simply refuse to change her way because she

do not choose to do so.



‘it be too bad of you,’ say he, ‘when there be so much work cry out

to be do. I could find you plenty of employment in Bridge Street, and

one or two other slum.’



‘I haven’t a doubt of it. I feel not the slight vocation for anything

of the kind.’



‘it be bad to sit aloft in meaningless exclusiveness.’



‘I daresay it be. it be the only kind of thing I care for, here. I hate

district visiting, and people who make themselves common, too.’



‘you could not make yourself common if you try, and it would put more

interest into your life.’



‘no, it would disgust I; that would be all. every night I should think

of all the horrid scene and horrid people I have see in the day. I

should be always see you mix up with they, and I should get to

think you as horrid as they be—you need not look at I in that way.

it’s my nature.... oh, I daresay you be quite right, Michael—indeed,

you always be—but I don’t take any interest in those thing, and I

don’t want to. I prefer to remain as I be.’



‘as I be,’ be exquisite enough in its refinement, hauteur, and beauty.

have any one else so speak, Michael would clearly have discern, and

probably point out, an odious spirit of pride and exclusiveness. as it

be Magdalen, he think, certainly, that she be unreasonable, but he

find the unreasonableness agreeable; he like the shape which it

take—that of fastidiousness—and be not dispose to quarrel with it. the

rare and wonderful creature be his own; he have never even yet feel as

if he fairly understand that fact, or could think enough of it. he

suggest, with a smile light up the dark gravity of his face, that

he should drive her round some day in his dog-cart, when he have not many

place to call at. she slightly lift her eyebrow, and draw out the

silk with which she be embroider.



‘no, sir. when you have a half holiday, and wish to devote it to I, I

will drive you out, or ride with you. at other time—I know it be not

intellectual, or humane, but it be so—I prefer the wood-walk in the

park; I will remain at home.’



she do remain at home, and take her monotonous stroll along the

woodland path, or might now and then be see, alone, in an open

carriage, pale and tranquil and indifferent-look, envelop in her

dark fur and feather, with a huge light gray fur rug fill up the

rest of the carriage, and this even on day when the wind be keen and

the frost bite. she be well aware that not one woman in twenty could

have drive about Bradstane in winter, in an open carriage, without her

countenance assume rainbow hue. she could drive thus, and return home

without a red nose or blue cheek; and it give she a negative, cynical

pleasure to do it, and watch other people on foot, or seal up in

stuffy brougham with both window shut.



the evening before the luncheon already speak of, Michael be with she,

and she ask he if he be go to be present at it.



‘no,’ say he; ‘I’m engage at the time, but Gilbert be go.’



‘Gilbert! why, he surely do not generally go.’



‘no, but he seem to take more interest in sport since he become such a

chum of Askam’s; and, of course, _he_ will be there.’



‘ah, of course,’ say Magdalen.



‘of all the queer partnership I ever know, that one be the queer,’

add Michael, reflectively.



‘do you think so? it seem to I the most natural thing in the world.

would you have have Gilbert take up with a nobody?’



‘my dear Magdalen! I be go to say, a nobody would be well than

Otho Askam; but as he’s a friend of your, too, I suppose there be

excellence in he which my base vision cann’t perceive. and I know what

you mean by “nobody.” that’s poor Roger Camm. well, I’ll leave you your

friend, if you’ll leave I mine.’



‘I have not the slight desire to know anything about Mr. Roger

Camm—certainly not to interfere with he,’ reply Magdalen coldly.

Michael merely smile the sweet smile which Magdalen, in her heart of

heart, consider insipid, and the discussion end.



at two o’clock on the following afternoon, some forty man, young and

old, sit down at the long table, in the great room at the ‘King’s arm.’

the so-call ‘lunch’ be, in fact, a very substantial dinner, as such

luncheon be willnot to be. Sir Thomas Winthrop sit at one end of the

table, and at the other be a very young man, call Lord Charles

Startforth, represent his father. every family of stand in all the

country-side have send a representative, and every man present be more

or less acquainted with every other member of the company.



at Sir Thomas’s right hand sit Otho Askam, with a cross look in his

eye, and a more sullen expression than usual on his brow and mouth. Sir

Thomas be a very worthy, honourable gentleman, ready to take a paternal

interest in any young man of promise; but he be not a student of

character, nor acute in read the silent language of expression, as

see on a human face. from Otho’s quietness, and his monosyllabic

answer to the remark make to he, Sir Thomas augur a milder mood

than usual, and resolve that now or never be the time for he to say

his say; for he have on his mind ‘a few word’ intend for Otho’s

ear—word which he have succeed in convince himself it be his duty

to say. on the opposite side, a little low down, sit Gilbert

Langstroth, and next to he be Byrom Winthrop.



as the wine go round, the talk grow fast and free. man see each

other to-day who have, perhaps, not meet for some time past, and these

meeting call up recollection, bring out question, to which the

expansiveness of the moment produce confidential answer.



at the end of the table over which Lord Charles Startforth preside, a

discussion suddenly begin about some of those who sit near Sir Thomas

Winthrop.



‘what a lowering face that Askam have!’ be the observation which begin

the conversation.



‘his face cann’t be more lower than his temper,’ reply some one

else.



‘hah! I see there’s his inseparable chum, not so far off he.’



‘Gilbert Langstroth, do you mean? oh yes! he’s never so far away from

he. I have hear that that young man have a very long head, and would

not object to go into partnership with Otho Askam—Askam to supply the

money, and Langstroth the brain.’



the other laugh. ‘what, on Arthur Orton’s plan, do you mean? “some

people plenty money and no brain, and other people plenty brain and

no——”’



‘oh, come! that’s too bad. Langstroth be a gentleman.’



‘I never say he ben’t, that I know of. gentleman have get to live,

like other people, though these radical’ (with a growl) ‘seem to grudge

we our very existence.’



‘oh, hang all radical! you say Langstroth be a gentleman—Gilbert, I

mean. his brother be, at any rate. I don’t know a well fellow

anywhere.’



‘no, nor I.’ the assent be general. then some one else say—



‘he don’t seem to get marry.’



‘No; that engagement have be hang on far too long.’



a slight pause, and then, lean confidentially forward, the first

speaker say—



‘somebody else don’t seem to get marry, either.’



a sort of smile go round. then Lord Charles, with the rash candour of

youth, make the remark aloud which every one else have be make in his

own mind.



‘I wonder if Michael Langstroth know that every one say Otho Askam be

sweet upon his intended?’



one man shrug his shoulder.



‘if he do know, what could he do, or say?’



‘I don’t know what he could say, I’m sure; but what he could do would be

to get married to she at once.’



here a rather timid-looking young man, who have hitherto take no part in

the conversation, join in, with a slight stammer—



‘if he have to marry she like that, it would be all spoil for he. he

t-trust she.’



‘ah, yes; very beautiful of he,’ say a sceptical spirit; and then a

feeling seem to prevail that the talk have go far enough in that

direction. indeed, the conversation at the other end of the table have

become somewhat loud, and the speaker with whom we have be concern

begin to look and listen what it be all about.



what have take place be this. Sir Thomas Winthrop, good gentleman,

feel his heart warm within he, take advantage of much loud talk

around he to address a few word to Otho Askam.



‘I’m glad to see you with we to-day, Mr. Askam.’



‘thank you,’ say Otho, with his wooden bow.



‘I hope you find the sport to your liking,’ add the baronet. ‘we

consider it have be rather a good season, on the whole.’



‘it have be nothing to complain of,’ be the gracious reply.



encourage by this admission, Sir Thomas fill his glass, and say—



‘I hope this will not be the last time, by many, that we shall meet on

an occasion like this.’



again Otho bow stiffly, drink his glass of wine, and give ear to Sir

Thomas, as he proceed—



‘I’m glad to see you and Gilbert Langstroth so thick. he’s a very

intelligent young man.’



‘rather!’ observe Otho emphatically, with a nod, and what would have

be a wink, only he remember in time that such a testimony to

Gilbert’s intelligence might be think rather compromise than

otherwise by his interlocutor. in his own mind, and speak to Gilbert

in his own language, Otho call Sir Thomas ‘a rum old party, and green

as grass, you know,’ and this private opinion, which he hold very

strongly, render it a little difficult to he to meet the other now on

equal term.



Sir Thomas go on—



‘he have behave in a very admirable way to his father, and I always like

to see that in a youth. he have almost retrieve their affair, which

be in a deplorable condition.’



his voice take a confidentially funereal tone, and he shake his head.



‘yes, I know,’ say Otho, vaguely, and at this juncture he catch

‘of course,’ resume Sir Thomas, for whom the subject appear to have a

fatal fascination, ‘a young man, who have have to do battle with reverse,

as he have, be apt to think his affair be the centre of the universe,

and that every one else be as much concerned in they as he be himself.’



to this Otho say nothing, but he regard Sir Thomas with a curious,

bull-dog expression.



‘I’m afraid he be just a little rash in some thing,’ Sir Thomas go

on. ‘for instance, there’s that property by the river—those Townend

and as Otho make no reply ‘do you know if he have any project of that

kind?’



in the fulness of his heart and head he have not moderate his tone

the governor! he’s perfectly infatuate about those factory of Gilbert

Langstroth’s, and he’ll go and say something he ought not to.’



Otho’s answer come quite distinctly too, in bluff, curt tone.



‘I cann’t inform you on that topic. all I know of it be tell I in

confidence.’



‘quite right, quite right!’ say Sir Thomas, with the fatuity of an

elderly gentleman, in whom a solid meal, judiciously mingle with sound

wine, have develop the sense of benevolence to an abnormal degree.

‘that’s only just and honourable. but listen to I. your father be a

friend of mine; therefore, I may be allow to say a word to you. don’t

any one else, let you in for something you don’t know the end of. take

my word for it, Bradstane be not the site for a manufacturing town; and

gentleman have well keep clear of factory. the good thing to do with

those mill would be to pull they down, and build cottage where they

stand; and if you sink any money in the concern, stick to that, stick to

that!’



he lean back in his chair with a smile, a fatuous smile, upon his

visage. it be perfectly evident to the mean observer, that Sir

Thomas Winthrop have become—cheerful, and that he have just say a very

uncomfortable kind of thing; not that there might not be plenty of truth

in the thing, but to have say it aloud be truly unfortunate.



Gilbert Langstroth have start up, his face pale, and be lean

forward, with compress lip, apparently about to speak. Byrom Winthrop

say in his ear—



‘don’t make a row, Gilbert. you know the word “manufacture” always set

he off. it mean nothing.’



then a thing happen which no one be prepare for. Otho Askam, look

round, observe—



‘I see a lot of you have hear what Sir Thomas have be say. all I

can say be, I do not bring on the discussion; but now that it be on,

I’d have every man here know that Gilbert Langstroth be my friend; and

whoever say a word against he, say it against I. the business that

Sir Thomas speak of, have be mention between we. I want to help

he with it, and he wouldn’t let I—if you call that ‘let I in for

something that I cann’t see the end of.’ he say it be a risky thing for

my money. I say, d—n the risk! he’s welcome to half of all that I’ve

get, and if he do not choose to take it, why, I say he do not know

what friendship be. shake hand, Gilbert.’



Gilbert have be listen, white and breathless. Sir Thomas, in feeble

despair, be protest, in the futile way common to people who have

stir up a riot without have the least idea how to quell it, that

really, it be most unfortunate. he never mean—he have no idea; and so

forth.



Gilbert suddenly turn upon he, with his blue-gray eye flash from

his pale face.



‘I do not know what idea you may have have, sir, nor what you mean, but

it be not the first time you have attack I, and say ill thing of I

behind my back. you try to set my own brother against I on this very

subject. you will pardon my presumption in say it, but upon my word I

and your father’s friend malign I to you. I think I will say good

afternoon,’ he add, as a stinging parting shaft to Sir Thomas. ‘it

would be embarrassing for we both to remain, and it be fitter that I

should leave than you.’



with which, and with a slight and perfectly self-possess bow to Sir

Thomas and the assemble company, he depart, and Otho Askam with he.



this scene, of course, make a great sensation, and that night be

report far and wide, throughout many mile of Yorkshire and Durham.

every man agree in say that Sir Thomas Winthrop be apt to become

too expansive on these occasion, and that they hope it would be a

lesson to he. as to Gilbert and Otho, and their behaviour, opinion

differ. man speak of their part in the fray accord to their own

feeling and disposition, some say that it be a touching example of

faith and friendship, other lean on the opinion that Otho Askam, in

to-morrow’s stingy fit, would repent he of his reckless generosity

to-day; while one observer say—



‘I suppose there be something real in it. I’m sure there be on Askam’s

side, at any rate; but that Gilbert Langstroth be a queer fellow. I’m

certain, if you could see to the bottom of his heart, you would find

gratitude to Sir Thomas for having give he such a chance. it be very

telling, that slightly tremble voice, and that little side stroke

about having fight their battle alone, and without ask Sir Thomas’s

help. it make Sir Thomas look confoundedly foolish, and as if he have

be do a very mean thing.’



‘and don’t you think he have?’



‘certainly not. he have be do what he think be the very good for

everybody, and in the most disinterested way. only, you know, he hate

what he call tradespeople like poison; and the idea of knock

Gilbert’s factory on the head be just too much for he.’



‘well, I’m much mistaken if he have not give they a good push towards a

fresh start.’



‘I quite agree with you.’ and there be a laugh at the expense of Sir

Thomas.



the poor gentleman hide his diminished head that night, and it be not

till the follow morning that he have so far revive as to be able to

take a tone of dignified bitterness, and grave satire on his own

good-nature. ‘selfishness,’ he inform Lady Winthrop, ‘be the only

policy that pay, and never again would he commit the mistake of

offer disintereste advice to young man, even though they might be

the son of his old friend.’



it never transpire what pass between Gilbert and Otho at any private

interview after this scene, but it be not very long afterwards, that

Gilbert, with a tranquil smile on his face, sit down to his desk and

write the following letter:—



‘DEAR ROGER,



        ‘do you remember, when you be stay with we, my take you

down to the end of the town, to look at those two factory? and I ask

you if you would come and manage they, suppose I ever get they to work

again. you say you would, if you be not tie down to something else,

or in a much well position. Michael tell I you be still in the same

place, and not too well satisfied with it. I be go to claim your

promise. my friend, Otho Askam, have buy the mill; at least, an

arrangement have be make by which they will most likely become his in

and counsel as soon as ever you can give they to I. I hope you have not

change your mind, and that you will not think the salary too small. we

find that we cannot offer you more than £120 to begin with, but it would

be advance on the first possible opportunity; and with the increase

prosperity of the concern the manager would get more, and eventually

I intend that it shall so turn out. I need not say that we will

guarantee that you lose nothing in a pecuniary point of view, if you be

willing to help we to start a new thing. I cannot say fair than that;

and in the hope that I may very soon receive your assent to this

proposition, to be follow by your speedy arrival, I remain,



                                        ‘your faithfully,

                                               ‘gilbert LANGSTROTH.’



the result of this letter be, that within six week of its having be

‘here I be,’ be Roger Camm’s laconical greeting.

                              CHAPTER VII



                        MICHAEL, ROGER, gilbert

                              CHAPTER VIII



               the first-fruit of the wisdom of gilbert

easy-go, so slow in judge other, so full of sweet-temper

charity. he do not look very much at peace with himself or the world

just now. he be the first to speak.



‘I send for you, Roger,’ he begin, and his voice be very quiet, and

very incisive. Roger hardly recognise it. ‘I want you to hear something

I have to say. you be my friend; and a friend, as we all know, sticketh

close than a brother.’



‘can this be Michael?’ Roger think, in his bewilderment. ‘I fancy no

one but Gilbert could sneer in that way.’



Roger have yet to learn that there be no sneer so bitter as that which be

call forth by intense suffering, or a very keen sense of injustice. he

think all sneer be the product of a cynical frame of mind, or,

with some person, constitutional. but, think that such a tone be

most cruel mental perturbation, and look in a helpless way from one

‘yes, Michael,’ say Roger, at last. ‘I be ready, either to do or to let

alone, as you wish. what be it?’



‘boy!’ exclaim the little doctor, unable to contain himself any

before you dispute, for Heaven’s sake consider! you may say thing,

or cheat love and trust.

Hark to I, Roger!’ he turn now to Roger; and to the latter it seem

wish you to know how he have dispose of his property, and to hear what

course I intend to pursue, in consequence of that disposition. here be

the will. I willn’t trouble you with much of it, but I must ask you to

listen to this passage.’



from the will, it appear that the Langstroth estate be now free of

encumbrance. the income derive from what remain of they be all

require, and would be for some few year, to pay off the remain

interest on some debt, of which the capital be already clear away.

over and above, there be a clear sum of six thousand pound, gain

about a year ago by the advantageous sale of two farm and some wood,

mention in the will. of this, four thousand be leave to Gilbert, at

his absolute disposal; three thousand, as the will state, as his just

half of the property, and another thousand as a sort of payment or

indemnity for his service in retrieve the estate, which, without his

care and diligence, would probably have be rather a debt than an

inheritance. the other two thousand be leave in trust to Gilbert, to be

invest and dispose of for Michael’s benefit, and the income derive

therefrom be to be pay to Michael by his brother; the testator

declare himself to have the great faith and confidence in the

business ability of his son Gilbert. the Townend factory would pay

nothing for a long time to any one but Otho Askam, whose money have find

the mean of start they again. when they should, or if they ever

should begin to pay, their profit be to be equally divide between

Michael and Gilbert, or, fail they, their heir. that is, in plain

term, there be a probability that some eight or ten year hence,

Michael might begin to receive an income from ‘Langstroth’s Folly.’ the

house call the Red Gables, situate within the township of

bradstane-on-Tees, and all the furniture, plate, picture, china,

ornament, and all other household appendage whatsoever, save such as

might be personal possession of Gilbert, be to go to Michael

absolutely, as the eld son.



such be the tenor of the testament, to which Roger listen

breathlessly, as Michael read it in a low, quick, clear voice. when he

have finish, he lay the will on the table again, and Roger, look

on his shoulder, ask in a low voice—



‘Michael, what do it mean?’



‘it mean that my brother be very clever, and I be a great blockhead. I

be fain to doff before his superior wisdom.’



Gilbert, his arm still fold across his chest, be look at they,

pale, calm, and seemingly self-possess.



‘take heed of what you say, Michael,’ he say, quietly. ‘abuse, even

from——’



‘I be go to do nothing but praise and congratulate you on your great

unless you take pity on it, it will be useless, for I shall never touch

‘now, Michael, Michael, madman! beware what you say!’ cry the little

lightly speak of.



Michael, having patiently wait till this apostrophe have be

‘_two thousand_, Gilbert; it be an odd coincidence. do you remember my

say to you long ago, that if I’d two thousand to start with I would

be married to-morrow—eh?’



Gilbert neither move nor raise his head.



‘I know you think that a very imprudent way of spend two thousand

pound. it seem my father must have hold the same opinion, and between

you, you have arrange that I should do nothing mischievous.’



here he raise himself up again, and, turn to the other, go on—



‘I want you all to understand this. that which I be not trust to

handle for myself; that which be confide by my father to my young

hear what I say?’



there be a low murmur from the doctor and Roger. Michael go on—



‘that be the case, it seem that what I have leave, to call my own, be

my father’s house—the house in which we be both bear and bring up,

where we live as brother, without an unbrotherly thought—on my part at

least; and the house where, when I go out into the world to relieve

the burden which have fall on our affair, I leave you in my place, to

tend my father; to watch over all our interest; to deal justly by I as

well as by yourself——’



there be a very long pause. it seem as if Michael, steady though his

voice have remain, be unable to finish the utterance of the thought

doggedly downwards.



‘that house, as I say, be now all I have, but it be my own, and you have

just give in the account of your stewardship,’ go on Michael, his

‘there it be!’ he lay his hand upon the will. ‘to I, it have be a

fatal stewardship. it have rob I not only of my inheritance, but of

the latter rise; perhaps he know what be to come. neither of the other

dare to speak. Gilbert once lift his head and look at his brother,

but instantly his face sink again. he be voiceless, powerless,

defenceless. Michael step aside and throw the door open wide.



‘be my house,’ he say, ‘I order you to leave it now, this instant.

go!’



another pause. silence still. Michael stand wait. Gilbert look

around he, as if he struggle to speak, but could not. he see nothing

to cheer he. Dr. Rowntree with his hand clasp, his kind face look

the picture of woe; Roger Camm frown and silent. Gilbert take two

step towards the door.



‘Michael,’ he say.



‘go!’ repeat Michael, in a stony voice.



Gilbert walk slowly out at the door, into the hall, take his hat, and

with two of they, at least, as if the sound strike they like an actual

resistless determination and strength of will which none of they have

which he be make, good or bad, be begin to show itself.



after a moment’s silence, he turn again to the other and say—



‘I willn’t detain you any long. I wish I could have spare you such a

scene, but as my two near friend, I wish you to be under no

mistake as to what I be go to do. and now I should like to be alone

for awhile.’



Roger heave a deep sigh, and say nothing, but move towards the door.

the doctor, who have a tender heart, and down whose cheek the tear be

run, fall back into old Quaker phraseology, as he almost sob out—



‘Michael, my poor, poor lad, thou’ll come and sleep in thy own bed

to-night, at my house, willn’t thou?’



‘yes, I will, doctor,’ reply Michael slowly; and they leave he alone.









                               CHAPTER ix



                     the goddess of the tender foot





‘the goddess calamity be delicate, and ... her foot be tender. her foot

be soft, he say, for she tread not upon the ground, but make she

path upon the head of man.’



these word, or something like they, be float dimly in Roger Camm’s

mind, as he walk with Dr. Rowntree across the square to the house on

the opposite side. his heart be full to burst. love Michael as he

do, well than any one in the world, he feel to the full the meaning

of the summon he have receive, to hear his friend’s decision. it be not

for a light thing that a man turn out of door the brother in whom he

have all his life feel unqualified trust and confidence; it be not a

casual acquaintance whom he summon to witness the deed, and so Roger

feel. but while he quite appreciate this accident of the thing, the

thing itself bewilder he even yet. it be one of those bizarre,

jarring circumstance which come upon one like a clap of thunder from a

cloudless sky, which one fail to take in properly on the first blush of

they. even yet, Roger could not feel at home with the recollection of

Michael stand erect and stiff, the spirit of anger flame from his

eye, deaf to every remonstrance, and cast scornful eye upon

Gilbert’s pitiful condition.



neither he nor the doctor speak till they be in the house again. it

seem that they have be but a short time away, for there be

this bald reality and commonplace of everyday life do not seem to put

thing into any more comprehensible shape; if possible, they heighten

the strangeness and sadness of the situation. but stand together

there, they (to use the vernacular) ‘find their tongue.’ Dr. Rowntree

‘who would ha’ think it, Roger? who would ha’ think it?’



‘well,’ say Roger, prop his broad back against the mantelpiece, and

stare down at his boot, ‘not I, for one, and I think there will be

precious few to jerk their head and say, “I tell you so,” this time.

and yet I don’t feel half so much surprised as enrage, now that it be

all out.’



‘he should not have fling away what be leave he in that way,’

complain Dr. Rowntree. ‘he should have be cool.’



‘cool, doctor! now, come! would you have be cool? be you cool, as it

be?’



‘no, no, I know. but he ought to have keep cool. he should have carry

it before a court of justice. they do set aside will sometimes, that

be flagrantly unjust; and I think they would, at any rate, have hand

it stand to reason. an eld son, with not a penny of cash leave he,

except, as you may say, at the discretion of his young

brother—monstrous, monstrous! as if he have be a spendthrift, or a

ne’er-do-weel!’



‘if it be twenty thousand, it would make no difference,’ say Roger

slowly, for he have be work the thing out in his mind. ‘I can see

where it be. do you suppose Michael could have get beside himself in

again.’



‘I’m afraid not—I’m afraid not.’



‘he would not be what he be if he could,’ say Roger.



‘how can one console he?’



‘nohow. it ben’t to be do.’



‘what can I say to he, my poor lad?’



‘nothing, if you’ll believe I. I can tell you I shall not speak of it.

there be thing no one ought to meddle with, unless they be open out

‘I will try,’ say Dr. Rowntree; ‘but if I see he look very

miserable, I don’t think I can keep quiet.’



‘you willn’t see Michael look miserable, I can tell you that. my time

be up,’ add Roger, look at his watch. ‘I must go back to my work.’



he leave the house, with the thought just come into his mind, ‘after all,

I shall have to speak to he. I don’t see how how I can stay in this

he turn into the office, but it be with difficulty that he succeed

in give any attention to his work; for in his mind’s eye he have the

where the wretched scene of the morning have take place. it seem to

could overtake he—which idea serve sweetly to illustrate his own

misfortune can assume; also of the marvellously elastic nature of the

human creature, and of that part of it, be it brain, or heart, or soul,

or whatsoever it may in reality be, which suffer.



Roger Camm, repeat to himself the half-forget Greek of his

quotation about the goddess Calamity, never dream for a moment but

that she have stay her course. surely her foot have press with

sufficient weight upon the head which she have select as her

standpoint! could his spiritual eye have pierce that veil, filmy, and

yet dense, which envelop we as we move to and fro on this earth, and

see the guide power about Michael, he would have perceive still

hover amongst they a dark form with a woebegone countenance—she of

the tender foot yet.



he return to Dr. Rowntree’s from his work, and, have no heart to

a divided attention, wonder the while whether Michael would go to

Magdalen that night, or wait till the morrow; and wonder likewise

whether she would be of any use to he in the crisis.



‘Gilbert would be a well spec. for she now,’ say Roger bitterly,

within himself. ‘only, not to blacken he more than be absolutely

necessary, he never have the faint fancy for she. in fact, I don’t

believe he would take she with fifty thousand down.’



towards ten o’clock Michael come in, greet they both with great

this dry-eyed composure, this something indescribable in voice and

they separate at their usual hour, and no one complain the next

than perfect repose.



Roger get a few word alone with he before breakfast.



‘Michael, I want to speak to you. after what have happen, I don’t see

Michael pause a moment. ‘I understand what you mean,’ say he. ‘_you_

stay—unless they sack I.’



at that moment a note be bring to Michael. he open and read it very

quickly, and then toss it across to Roger.



‘read it,’ say he. ‘you must see I through each stage of this, so that

we need never have to mention it to each other again.’



Roger read it. it be from Gilbert, and the paper on which it be

write be stamp, ‘Thorsgarth, bradstane-upon-Tees.’



‘there be two precious rascal together under the same roof,’ be

Roger’s unspoken comment before he begin to read.



but his face change as he peruse the line. the note be short, but

strong in its very baldness and simplicity; as unlike Gilbert’s ordinary

Gilbert ask Michael for an interview. ‘though you have treat I like

do, I shall never ask again. I can make it right, and the whole future

of both of we may depend upon your answer.’



Roger read this twice over to himself, and look at Michael, who have

go to his desk and be write quickly. as soon as he have finish, he

  ‘I have receive your note, and decline to see you or hold any

  communication with you. your possession be, I believe, at the Red

  Gables. I shall not be there to-morrow, and you will be at liberty to

  fetch away what you choose of your belonging. after that you cfannot

  be admit there.



                                                MICHAEL LANGSTROTH.’



‘Michael,’ say Roger, hold both these document in his hand, and

speak very earnestly, ‘forgive I for even seem to meddle in

your affair. Gilbert have a meaning under that note of his. willn’t

you think twice before you send that answer to he?’



‘I do all the thinking about he that I shall ever give to he

again, yesterday,’ say Michael, trenchantly. ‘do you suppose I

Roger have be far more struck than he would have care to confess,

‘Michael,’ he say, in a low voice, ‘have you the right to do it?’



‘yes, I have,’ reply Michael, his face grow terribly hard and

Roger pause, look at his friend. in his mind be the word,

‘until seventy time seven,’ but he have not the courage to utter

‘I know you would, but it never can and never will,’ say Michael,

be on its way to Thorsgarth.



‘be you go far to-day?’ the doctor ask Michael at breakfast.

he would have give a good deal if the young man would have

opening for sympathy and condolence. but the young man do nothing

of the kind.



‘yes,’ he answer at once; ‘a good way. I shall not be back to

lunch. I shall get that at the Brydges. then I have to go on to

Cotherstone, but I shall be back to dinner; and then,’ he add, ‘I

must really try to get to Balder Hall. it be age since I be

there.’









                               CHAPTER X



                        the process of ANNEALING





soon after breakfast they separate as be their wonot. Roger and the

doctor come and go as usual, but the November afternoon have grow

to darkness before Michael return, look pale and fag from

his long ride and hard day’s work. take as a whole, the patient in

and around Bradstane be not a very profitable set. for one rich

old lady like Miss Strangforth, say Dr. Rowntree, linger on as a

chronic invalid for year—always want attention, and always

profoundly grateful for all that her physician either do or fail

to do for she, and pay her bill with a cheque by return of

post—for one treasure like this there be a dozen farmer’ wife

and daughter, or sordid, unlovely poor in Bridge Street, call

upon the doctor with a frequency and persistency which they would

never have dream of if they have possess either the mean or the

intention of pay he. other there be, cottager, labourer,

live at immense distance over bad road, and expect a great

deal of attention in return for very small fee—anything but a

profitable _clientèle_—and some of these Michael have be visit

to-day.



he come in, pick up a note which lie on the hall table wait for

he, which he look for as if he expect it—his dark face light

for a moment as he take it, for the handwriting be that of Magdalen

Wynter—put his head in at the library door, remark, ‘I’m wet

through—change my thing—down directly,’ and run upstairs, shut

his bedroom door after he.



‘what a spirit!’ cry the doctor, enthusiastically. ‘what a spirit

he have! he’ll get over it yet.’



‘well than his brother will, I think,’ say Roger, half to

himself; and then, gaze into the fire, he wonder what Gilbert

be do, and wish, as he have catch himself wish more than

once that day, that Michael could have see his way to answer that

note of Gilbert’s differently.



by and by the gong sound. Roger and the doctor go into the

dining-room. Michael be still upstairs. the soup have be serve,

and he come not.



‘go to Mr. Langstroth’s door and say everything will be cold, and we

be wait for he,’ say Dr. Rowntree to the serve-maid, who do

as she be tell, and presently return, speedily follow by

Michael.



Roger give a sharp glance at he, and think he carry his head

very high—high than usual.



‘sorry to keep you wait,’ he say, with an affected, jaunty air,

not in the least like his usual manner. ‘I quite forget how time be

go on.’



he laugh as he speak, and say he be ravenously hungry, but

offend the doctor greatly by scarcely touch what be set before

‘what do you mean by say you be ravenous, and then not eat

anything?’ he ask, crossly.



Michael laugh a nervous, forced laugh, and reply—



‘oh, I must have think I be hungry than I really be. I cann’t

eat anything now. these long ride take it out of a fellow in such a

way.’



‘do you have lunch at the Brydges?’



‘yes. they have quite a lot of people stay there. that be one

reason why I be so late. after lunch I go with Tom to the stable

to see his new hunter. it be a beauty, too.’



Roger sit silent, mislike the unusual volubility of Michael’s

speech and excuse. Michael himself, in the meantime, have go off

on a new tack, and be describe his adventure at a farmhouse on

the moor, and the extraordinary symptom enumerate by the mistress

anything force or unnatural in his manner. Roger’s foreboding grow

it be wet.



‘why, man!’ exclaim he, ‘you say you be wet through, and you

have get on the identical tog that you come in with. what an ass

you be, Michael!’ he add, gently. ‘go upstairs and change, right

away, or you willn’t get to Balder Hall to-night.’



‘I’m not go to Balder Hall—I think not,’ say Michael, wearily,

as he let Roger push he towards the stair, up which he begin

‘there’s something wrong—something wrong—something wrong,’ keep

Michael’s room be over the study. Roger, listen intently, hear

when he have try once or twice again, he open the door and go
