old easy-chair by the bedside, his head sink on his breast, his eye

close, and an open letter droop from his right hand.



‘still in those wet clothe,’ mutter Roger. ‘he’ll kill himself.’



he go up to he, and touch he on the shoulder. Michael awake

with a start, and look confusedly around he.



‘Roger!’ he say. ‘I’m so sleepy. I don’t know what’s come over I.’

he seem to see the letter he hold, and go on, in an absent way,

‘ben’t it rather too bad of she not to wait till she have see I?

so long—it’s three year since I begin to wait for she and work for

she. but as soon as she hear the first whisper—well, I do write

and tell she what I’d do, and say I would go up and see she

to-night, you know—yes, to-night. but she never wait. she fling I

off,’ and he throw out his arm. ‘she make haste to do it. she must

have be glad to do it! there’s something in her letter which say

so. see!’ he hold it out to Roger. ‘what a lot of disagreeable

thing you’ve have to do for I lately!’ he go on. ‘Good Lord! how

tired I be! I never be so tired in my life. I cann’t imagine the

reason of it.’



Roger, defer for a moment his intention of make Michael go to

bed, stop to read the letter, which run:—



‘MY DEAR MICHAEL,



        ‘I receive your letter this morning, and I be sorry to say

I cannot approve of what you have do. even before I get it, I have

be think for some time about our engagement, and wonder if

it have ever be a wise one. during these three day that you have

not be here, I have have ample time to consider the subject. even

if nothing far have happen, I should have write as I now do;

but I do not disguise from you that the manner in which you have

yourself cut off every prospect of advancement strengthen my

resolution. these thing be well do promptly. it save pain to

all concern.



‘as there be now evidently no prospect of our be marry within

any definite time, I wish our engagement to cease. I desire this

both on your account and my own. in addition to the reason already

state, I do not think it would be for your happiness to continue

it, and I be quite sure it would not be for mine. I shall be glad of

a line from you when convenient, to say that you consent to my

proposal; and with every wish for your happiness and prosperity, I

remain,



                                           ‘your sincere friend,

                                                  ‘MAGDALEN WYNTER.’



‘there’s a speciman of elegant composition!’ exclaim Michael,

suddenly sit upright, and laugh harshly. ‘it could not have

be more proper if she have write it at school, and the head

governess have correct it. what a blessed thing it be when people

know their own mind, and can command plain English in which to make

they know! only it’s a pity that they should take three year to

learn what they do want, or whom they don’t want.’ he give a

disagreeable little laugh at his own pleasantry, and then rise. ‘if

you’ll go down, Roger, I will now change these thing, and join you

directly. but it’s lucky I need not go to Balder Hall, for I feel

more and more tired every minute.’



‘take off your thing, by all mean,’ say Roger, gravely; ‘but you

must not come down. you must go to bed.’



‘to bed!’ exclaim Michael, contemptuously. ‘a man go to bed

because he’s have a long ride in the wet and cold, and find rather a

chilly letter to greet he on his return! I be not such an ass.’



but as he speak, strength seem to forsake his limb; he could not

stand any more, but sit down again in the chair by the bedside.



‘perhaps Askam be sit with she now. I suppose they will be

married,’ he say, betray in his sudden weakness what his secret

fear have evidently be. ‘perhaps she will keep he straight. he

need it, and she have a spirit, though I know Gilbert and my father

never think so; and——’



here he begin to wander in his talk; be shiver and shake with

cold one moment, burn hot the next. the thorough drenching which

he have get after leave the Brydges and ride for mile in the

tooth of the bitter wind and rain; the excited condition of his

brain over Gilbert’s treachery; the receipt of Magdalen’s letter,

with its icy, unyielde egoism, show he that all these year

her own advantage be what she have be think of, and that there

be not a spark of love for he in her dull heart;—these thing

break through even his magnificent health and strength. he could not

shake off the physical chill any more than he could the mental

prostration. an attack of a tedious, wear low fever reduce he

to perfect physical weakness and docility; but far bad than the

fever be the accompany mental gloom, the result of the shock to

the nervous system. the young man, shut up in his room, too weak in

body to move and shake off his demon visitant, go through all the

horror of a complete nervous breakdown, and make intimate

acquaintance with all its attendant crew of ghastly shade—those

pallid ghost which assemble and gibber and mouth at we when we have

so impose upon our hard-work servant, nerve and brain, as to

have render they for the time powerless to answer to our imperious

demand. exhaust, they sink down, and say to we, ‘we can no more,’

and then we be at the mercy of every shadow, every whisper, every

vain imagining and thought of horror.



Michael Langstroth, with his superb constitution and youth and

temperance to back he, and with the devoted nursing of two such

friend as Roger and the doctor, be in the course of a few week

restore to comparative strength. gradually the shade and ghost,

the bat and owl that haunt the dark place of the human mind,

retire before gather physical strength. thing be go that

could never be restore—hope, joy, faith, enthusiasm; thing

which have once seem all-important, appear now almost too

insignificant for notice. under Roger’s eye be the process

accomplish which in his blindness he have long ago wish for his

friend. he be make into a man: go into the valley of the shadow

a youth, for all his six and twenty year, his bone, and his muscle,

and his brain; come out of it alive, sane, whole, if weak, but

strip of every superfluous hope, confidence, or youthfulness.



it be November when he go to his room that night; it be the very

end of December when he come out of it, a hollow-eyed spectre

enough. and it be a month later still when Dr. Rowntree carry he

down to Hastings one day, return himself the next, and leave

his adopt son there to recruit.



so end Michael Langstroth’s youth, as a tale that be tell.









                               CHAPTER XI



                           OTHO’S letter-BAG





a November morning, five year later. the sky gray and brooding, the

tree still and leafless. everything outside betoken the drear

season of the year, and even the trimly keep lawn of Thorsgarth

could not give brightness to this mood of Nature and the time o’

day.



within, in a small room which he generally use for breakfasting,

Otho Askam stand on the hearthrug, with his burly back turn

towards a large fire. a letter be in his hand, to which he seem

to pay more attention than it be usually his habit to give to his

correspondence, for he turn it about, and peruse it often. what

be the change which five year may have work in his trait, or

how many of they have become strengthen and accentuate during

that time?



he would seem, outwardly consider, to have gain something, both

in breadth and solidity, without have in any way weaken or

deteriorate. the line be as sturdy, as burly as before. the

expression of his countenance be distinctly imperious, even more

imperious than of yore. as he stand there, the letter in one hand,

the other impatiently smooth the hair on his upper lip—a dark

line only, which seem to accentuate the sullenness of his face,

without hiding or soften a single harsh trait or feature—as he

stand there his countenance be a dangerous-look one; the

expression or atmosphere which radiate from the man be not

that of sincerity. in repose he have the old fierceness of

appearance—whatever mental or moral change might have take place,

that old look remain; and when he raise his dark eye and lift

his head, there be the same breathless, hunt, or hunt look

about he, as in the day of his very young manhood there have be.



he be alone, and have just finish breakfast. at his foot sit a

dignified Dandie Dinmont, somewhat advanced in year, and with the

self-conscious aspect of a dog which have long be make much of by

human being, so that at last it have come to feel convinced that all

their action, word, and movement have some reference to it and

its doing. it gaze up into Otho’s face and watch his gesture,

and when he speak to it, it seem pleased. animal, even if they

have that keen discernment as to the virtue or vice of the being by

whom they be surround, with which some person credit they, can

conceal their like and dislike, for their own purpose, quite as

cleverly as the man and woman they live with—at least, sophisticate

and humanise dog, like this highly educate Pouncer, can.



look out of the window, one see the drear season of the year

plainly write upon the outward aspect of thing. November, sad

November, but the November of the country, and not of the town. in

southern place, and more favoured spot, tree might still be

cover with fiery autumn tint; but here every leaf have drop,

and upon the black and sodden-look bough and twig hang a damp,

clammy dew, and the grass be hoar and gray with the same. the sky

be leaden; not a branch stir. from here one could see where the

ground slope towards the river, but one could not see the stream

itself. the room be warm; the house be quiet; the master be

vex—so much be plainly to be read on his face. and so much be

audible from his lip too, as he ejaculate in a wrathful tone—



‘beastly folly—and a beastly nuisance, too!’



and the cause of his vexation, the letter he hold in his hand? it be

easy to read over his shoulder, and follow the line, as he peruse

it a third time, with the result, apparently, of increase his

first exasperation.



                                                     ‘BRINSWELL, L——



  ’DEAR OTHO,



          ‘it be a very long time since you write to me,—longer than

  usual. as for your ever come to see I, I have long ago give up

  that expectation as a wild delusion. be you “busy”? country

  gentleman usually be, from what I hear; and from what I’ve see I

  should say they work very hard to make believe they have more to

  do than they know how to manage.



  ‘I wonder if you have realise that I be twenty-two on my last

  birthday? I don’t suppose you have ever give a thought to the

  subject. at least, I miss your usual kind remembrance of I, on

  the occasion.’ [‘what the dicken do she mean? I’ve never be

  in the habit of send her birthday present!’]



  ‘well, it be of no use waste word over thing. I wish to

  explain my situation and intention to you. since Aunt Emily’s

  death, six month ago, Uncle Robert have be quite break up, and

  he don’t seem to get any well. it be a fearful loss to he. no

  one know—of the world at large, I mean—how much they be bind up

  in each other, and how fearfully he miss she. after try

  everything in the way of stay at home and keep quiet, the

  doctor have advise a long voyage and a complete change. it have

  be decide to close Brinswell for a year at least, and he and

  Paul will set out on their travel in a week or two. I think they

  will visit Australia first, as they seem to think the long voyage

  will do he good, and they talk about India and America before

  they return—medicine for a troubled mind. poor Uncle Robert! he

  agree to all, and say he know he be morbid. it seem to be

  think very morbid nowadays if you have a grief that’s past the

  healing for six month, even though it be your dearest in the

  whole world that have go from you.



  ‘I be not go with Uncle Robert and Paul. if it have be a

  short journey, I might have do so. I should have like

  immensely to go to America, for instance. but this be different.

  Paul, of course, go with he, because it would be outrageous to

  think of his go alone; but the doctor, and Paul too, say he

  should not be surround by too many of his own family, as the

  object be entire change. they both think it well for I not to

  go; and I shall do as they think fit. it be very sad every way.



  ‘very sad, it be—so sad that I feel myself a little heartless,

  because I cann’t help be rather glad that I shall leave here,

  and at last make acquaintance with my own home and my own brother.

  you do not know how often I have wish to do so. I be glad I

  shall see my birthplace and my north-country home; very glad I

  shall see you. and will not you say you will be glad to see I,

  dear Otho? it be year since I have see you, and it seem

  unnatural that it should be so. will you come down and fetch I,

  or be you too busy? I propose to leave here a week from to-day.

  let I know about it.



  ‘there be one other thing that I feel I must mention to you, which

  make I very glad to be leave here. about six month ago, Mr.

  Mowbray—you know, he be the rector of the next parish; the Hon.

  and Rev. Percy Mowbray—propose to I. poor Aunt Emily be very

  anxious for I to marry he, but it be a sheer, utter

  impossibility. poor Aunt E——! Mr. Mowbray be rich, I believe, and

  of very good family, but I have never like he, and I could not

  think of it for a moment. it be very painful to I to find how

  annoyed she be with I, even to the last. and of course Mr.

  Mowbray cease to visit here, though I have to meet he sometimes.

  altogether, it will be a great relief to I in every way, to get

  to Bradstane for a time. now you be acquaint with all my

  reason for wish to come to you, and with my plan too, for the

  present. send I a line soon, and believe I,



                                  ‘your affectionate sister,

                                                    ‘ELEANOR ASKAM.’



Otho fling the letter upon the table with temper.



‘why the d—l could not she marry the fellow?’ he mutter angrily,

look darkly at Pouncer, who slightly move his tail and elevate

his ear with a sigh, as if he too wonder why—why she could not

have marry the fellow.



‘what could she wish for more? a girl in her position ought to take

the first opportunity that offer—good, of course—of settle

herself in life. and I’m sure old Aunt Emily know what she be

about. no one keener on family and money in the world. if she want

the match so badly, I’ll go bail it be a good one. of course she

must marry—girl like she always must marry—and of course she cann’t

go and marry a nobody. what a fool she must be! one would think she

be not all there. not that I should think the parson and I should

have hit it off very well as brother-in-law.’ he give a short

laugh. ‘a thunder mistake, that will,’ he go on within himself.

‘such will have no business to be make.’



this reflection refer to a clause in his father’s will, provide

that Otho’s sister, so long as he and she both remain unmarried,

be entitle to a home at Thorsgarth whenever she choose to inhabit

it. in the event of his marriage, there remain for her the Dower

House, which indeed be her for her life if she do not marry—an

old stone house in the square, not far from the Red Gables.



‘I cann’t stop it, I suppose. it be a beastly nuisance, if ever there

be one. as for go to fetch she, I shall do no such thing. go

nearly three hundred mile to fetch back some one I don’t want? not

I!... and what can I do with she when I get she here? good Lord! why

must woman be so stupid? such sentimental nonsense! because I be her

brother—bah! there’s that whey-face Paul Stanley, her cousin,

look as if butter wouldn’t melt in his mouth—she’s know he all

her life, and he have be far more of a brother to she than I have.’



Otho feel vindictive, realise what a grievous thing it be that he

should have to be anybody’s brother.



‘the going on in this house willn’t suit she at all,’ he reflect,

get more and more savage the long he think about it. ‘it have

be a bachelor’s house all these year, and it have come to

something if I’m to turn everything topsy-turvy for a chit of a girl

like that. dog and horse and man, and tobacco and wine and

card—what will she do amidst it all? and what shall I do with she?’



he stop mutter from sheer blankness of mind on the subject,

still fiercely stroke his upper lip, till after a time a look of

relief, though of very ill-temper relief at the good, come over

‘I suppose I must go and tell Magdalen about it. she’ll be able to

suggest something. I’ll go this morning, before I answer this

confound letter. Whew—w—w!’



he blow out a kind of ill-temper sigh, and Pouncer wag his tail

in visible and exceed satisfaction.



then Otho pick up another letter—short and, as it would seem,

sweet, to he at least, for his countenance relax visibly.



‘that’s well. it will be rather a relief to have he here if she

come. he know what be the right thing to do when there be

petticoat on the premise. I don’t.’



then he ring the bell, order the breakfast-thing to be take

away, and say he want his horse at eleven.



                   *      *      *      *      *



soon after twelve Otho ride up to Balder Hall, be admit to Miss

Wynter’s boudoir, and proceed to unfold his trouble to she.



she have receive he with the tranquillity which have always be the

chief characteristic of her demeanour, and which seem neither to

have increase nor diminish with year; hear all that he have to

and say, ‘see for yourself what she say,’ Magdalen take the

man about their feminine relative, and Otho have always be glad to

ignore his sister’s existence as much as possible. this suddenly

announce come of Miss Askam take Magdalen by surprise. she have no

be friend or enemy, but the letter would possibly give she a

valuable glimpse into the writer’s mind, perhaps even into her

character, write as it be confidentially to an only brother. as

to the question whether it be honourable or not to read such a

be credit with say—the keep one’s own integrity be enough

‘ah,’ say she, when she have finish the letter, ‘it be quite

obvious why she want to come. I do not know your sister be

‘no more do I, till she go and do this,’ say Otho, resentfully.



‘and you would rather she do not come?’



‘much rather. but it’s no use thinking of keep she away. I’m not

go to try. she have get the right to come, by my father’s will,

when I get she here.’



‘well, if you make it very pleasant for she, of course she’ll want

to stay.’



Otho nod. ‘of course.’



‘and if you upset all your habit, and make great change on her

account, then she will think you want she to stay, which would be

quite a false impression.’



‘I never think of that.’



‘of course not; only it seem such a very obvious thing. perhaps

that be why it never occur to you.’



‘now, come, none of your chaff. what I think of be, that it’s

simply impossible for a girl like that to settle down in a house

‘and pray what right have you to my advice? why should I interfere

between you and your sister? I might tell you it be a just

retribution on you for having alienate yourself systematically from

all such tie. you demand my advice as if you be a highwayman

require my watch and purse.’



Otho fidget and fumble, h’mmed and ha’d.



‘I beg your pardon, Magdalen. I think I have the right—of having

ask before, and receive—advice, you know. and you know I always

do come to you when I be in trouble.’



‘oh yes; I know you do.’



‘would you please tell I what I have well do?’



‘be she good-look?’ ask Magdalen.



‘oh no!’ say Otho, promptly. ‘she have red hair and freckle.’



Magdalen glance at Otho’s own dark trait, and say, ‘now, Otho!’



‘upon my soul and honour she have; and one of those face that flush

up all over, without a minute’s warning. I never could see the sense

laugh about thing—at least, she do when I see she. in fact,

‘bah! she have twelve hundred a year of her own, to do what she like

‘yes,’ say Magdalen, gently. ‘she be your sister.’ she take up her

‘you be a gem!’ he say, admiringly.



‘and bring she up to see I as soon as you can, after she come.’



‘the next afternoon, if it’s fine,’ he say, eagerly.



‘yes, the next afternoon, if you like. it will make no difference to

I.’

‘oh, he never fail you.’



‘no; he never do. I must take care not to bring he up here while

‘I know nothing about it. sometimes at one hour, sometimes at

another. and the sure way to bring about a collision be to take so

though you might, and probably would, make a shindy under such

‘Whew—w!’ murmur Otho, under his breath; and then aloud—



‘it seem as if all I say offend you this morning, Magdalen.

however, I’ll be more good-natured than you, and say thank you for

he get up and stand before she, hold out his hand. Magdalen

his inventive genius be apparently not great. he carry out her

meet, and so he be her affectionate brother, OTHO ASKAM.



then he ring the bell and desire to see the housekeeper; and when

‘yes,’ say Otho, with an uneasy feeling that, since he propose to

‘oh, sir, the room will not need much do to they. it’s not my

‘ben’t it?’ say Otho, with a kind of brusque facetiousness which

‘oh, I don’t mean that exactly, sir,’ say Mrs. Sparkes, please at

‘ah! well, you’ll remember what I say,’ remark Otho; and Mrs.

then he call for his horse again, and set off for his long-delay

Co., Limited, and that Roger Camm hate he, Otho, the man who have

find the money for it. the conclusion to which he have come by the

his sister from his mind, he resolve to think no more about she

until her arrival should force he to do so.









                              CHAPTER XII



                                ELEANOR





Otho’s week of freedom be over. his sister have come, and on the day

follow her arrival, in the afternoon, she ride with he through

Bradstane town towards Balder Hall.



any one who could have see she, even in the gray and mournful

November light, would have see a very beautiful young woman. she

be tall, and have an admirable figure, full of grace and strength.

no feeble development here, nor niggard trait, nor look of feeble

spine or over-sensitive nerve. whatever the intellect within that

beautiful head, its outward case be not one to find fault with. her

feature be not very regular; harmonious, though, in their very

irregularity. a soft, ivory-white complexion, as healthy as many a

ruddy one; and with this complexion, the red-brown hair and lambent,

tawny eye which sometimes accompany it. her eyebrow be much

dark than either her hair or her eye; and she have a large mouth,

but a beautiful one—beautiful because of its smile in mirth and of

its expression in repose.



there be a vague, indefinite family likeness between she and the

fierce-look Otho. where it lay, in what exactly it consist, it

would have be impossible to say, but it be there, though it be

slight, and perhaps more easily to be detect when they be apart

than when they be together; that be, see one of they alone, an

observer might have think, ‘how like her brother’ or ‘his sister!’

and yet, have the other one appear, and the face be compare,

none could have discern any resemblance.



during the first part of their ride they be both somewhat silent.

she be look about she with quick, keen glance, speak an

observant eye. Otho be wonder what Magdalen would say to he,

what he could say to she, at a later time, when he, after his

offhand description of the other day, have to introduce to she this

beautiful creature now ride with he.



Eleanor, while make her observation on the town and the

surrounding, be also occupy in think thing over. it be a

fact, she tell herself with some mortification, that Otho and she

be stranger to each other. it seem that absence, and long

separation, and the influence of utterly diverse life and habit

do produce that strangeness, even between brother and sister. her

Aunt Emily, in some of their talk, have tell she that this would be

the case, and she have say laughingly that she would defy she

brother to be a stranger to she, or to make a stranger of she. she

feeling, when she think about herself at all—strength of soul and

body, and a happy confidence that truth be great and will prevail.



yet this have not be such a joyful home-come as it ought to have

be. in all confidence she have set out to find the home which she

have only twice visit, each time for a day or two, on some tour

with her guardian, since, at six year of age, she have be bring

but Eleanor have not marry, and she have come to Thorsgarth to make

her home there.



‘be this all the town there be, Otho?’ she ask, suddenly, as they

like the south.’



‘of course not.’



‘I feel as if I’d never be here, and I hardly ever meet any one

who have. it look bleak here; that’s what I mean.’



‘well, it be,’ say Otho, vex with such a persistent talk about

the look of a place. as if it matter what Bradstane look like!

‘and it’s November, too. you cann’t expect rose in November.’

south wall at Brinswell when I leave. you remember Brinswell, Otho?’



‘yes, I do; and a dull hole it be.’

as they do there. the tree look so small and stunt.’

country-side.’



‘well, yes, the ash-tree. they be fine. but, of course, they ought

to be.’ and she hum to herself—



         ‘oh, the oak, and the ash, and the bonny ivy-tree,

         they grow the good at home in the north countrie.’



‘at home,’ she add, half to herself. ‘this be the north countrie,

and this be my home, after all. it’s a shame I don’t know it well.

wherever you look,’ she add, address he directly again, ‘you

where Mickle Fell and his brother loom high.



‘blue wall!’ repeat Otho, embarrass by the application of such

and those other one, to the south, be the Swaledale moor.’

there on the other side?’



‘More moor and more dale. it’s bleak enough there, if you like.

there’s some good shooting, though.’



‘I should like to see what there be at the other side,’ say

‘only about another three-quarter of a mile.’



‘this Miss Wynter—be she a very old friend of your?’ ask Eleanor

likely forget. she be my only friend—amongst the woman, that be.’



‘ah! an elderly woman?’



‘about my own—well, she’s a year or so old than I be.’



‘oh! an invalid, I suppose?’

have Eleanor be well acquaint with ‘the neighbourhood’ and its

Eleanor make no answer to this, and they ride on in silence for a

she also reflect that Otho’s gift of character-drawing seem to

‘here we be,’ observe Otho, as they turn in at the Balder Hall

Lodge, ride up to the door, and find that Miss Wynter be at home.









                              CHAPTER XIII



                      TWENTY-eight and TWENTY-two





Miss Strangforth’s butler throw open the door of an exquisite little

whose whole mind have be dwell in anticipation on the meeting

‘yes; I be so glad you come. take this easy-chair, and——oh, Michael,

‘Miss Askam and I have meet already,’ say Michael with composure. ‘I

‘Langstroth,’ say Michael, speak for himself.

I be.’

Eleanor, on her part, be conscious of like less and less the

they, or to fight out their radical difference to the bitter end?









                              CHAPTER XIV



                            THRUST and PARRY





‘of course you be quite strange to Bradstane?’ begin Magdalen.



‘yes, quite—at least, practically so. I cann’t recall much about

it. in fact, I’m strange to the north altogether, except

Scotland—stay at hotel or shooting-box, you know.’



‘ah, yes. those be luxury for the rich,’ say Magdalen, whose

whole person, attire, and surrounding breathe an atmosphere of

more than rich, of extravagance. ‘I may say I have never be in

the south, for I haven’t, except to Bournemouth, with my aunt. well,

I wonder how you will “like,” as they say here. the society of

Bradstane be a little peculiar. it be not intellectual—Eleanor feel

a little surprise at this; Magdalen herself have not strike she as

look intellectual—‘and it be not by any mean lively. and I

suppose you have be accustom to a good deal of variety in your

life?’



‘not lately. six month ago, my aunt, Mrs. Stanley, who have be my

second mother, die very suddenly. we have have a very quiet and a

very sad house ever since, and if Bradstane be ever so gay, I

should not be go out much now.’



‘ah, yes, very sad—Otho mention your loss,’ murmur Magdalen,

who, with the self-absorption of her kind, have forget Eleanor’s

account of her uncle’s condition.



‘and then,’ add Eleanor, feel her heart beat just a little

fast, but march straight into the fray, ‘I have Otho. I hope to

see something of he now. he be my only brother, and I have be

much separate from he.’



‘ah, your brother,’ say Magdalen, all at once discard she

purr tone, and take up her knitting, with the expression of one

who have just come to some mental decision. ‘he be the attraction,

be he?’



‘hateful woman!’ say Eleanor within herself. ‘she think he be her

property, and that I be come to dispute he with she. so I have, and

so I will.’ then aloud, ‘certainly, he be an attraction, if it

need a great attraction to make I wish to visit my own home,

after so many year. besides, who know how long I may have the

chance to be with he, and get to know he? I be astonish that he

have not marry before now.’



a slight pause. eleanor herself be surprised to find in what style

she be talk; but something in the very presence of the other

aggressive attitude.



‘at any rate, while I have the field to myself, I mean to let Otho

know that he have a sister,’ she pursue, with a slight laugh.



‘highly commendable,’ say Magdalen, either with constraint or a

slight sneer; it would have be difficult to say which.



‘he be a great friend of your, I find,’ continue Eleanor, look

directly at Magdalen, who make no reply to the word. Eleanor pause

a moment, and then take she course. she be really anxious to learn,

if she could, the extent of this woman’s influence over her brother;

but more than that, to get to know whether she be a sincere woman,

or a false one. she would feign a tender interest in Otho’s affair,

and a sisterly solicitude for his welfare. as a matter of fact, she

know nothing of the said affair, nor whether well or ill might be

the word to apply to his spiritual condition. she would try to

discover. it be a hardy resolution, with such a woman as Magdalen

for her opponent, but want of courage be not one of Eleanor’s

defect.



‘it seem so strange,’ she presently go on, in a musing tone,

see he often, and know he quite well, while I, his own sister,

scarcely know anything about he.’



‘you think that be a great loss, I suppose?’



‘well, yes, I do. I think I ought to know about he—good or bad. it

seem to I unnatural that I should not. I wish you would tell I

something about he, Miss Wynter. it really seem as though he have

leave we on purpose that we might discuss he.’



‘why discuss he at all?’



‘well,’ say Eleanor with a smile, ‘I don’t think you and I can have

many object of mutual interest to talk about. Otho be one,

obviously—my brother and your friend. I think it be most natural to

talk about he. from what he say of you, I be sure you must know a

great deal of his character and disposition. he be very reserved, I

think. I want to get on with he, of course. cann’t you tell I

something of his taste and habit?’



Miss Wynter’s white eyelid droop, but quiver not. her finger

fly in and out of the scarlet wool, and the ivory needles make a

pleasant, dull clicking. what she think with cold annoyance be,

‘my dear child, you surely do not suppose that because a man come

‘a porter at Tebay have tell I that the station after this one at

‘I have know he intimately for many year.’

‘Brother.’



‘indeed! but Otho seem not to know Dr. Langstroth very well.’

it could hardly be that these question of Eleanor’s, put in all

while she speak, Eleanor feel her heart as heavy as lead within she.

if Magdalen Wynter and Gilbert Langstroth be Otho’s friend, and

ponder painfully on this problem, she be rouse by the opening

                               CHAPTER XV



                              three WOMEN





‘Miss Dixon to see you, ma’am,’ say the servant. Eleanor look on

with some interest, as Magdalen greet the young woman who

enter—greet she in a manner which, though cordial, be decidedly

a patronise manner. Eleanor see that the girl’s eye fall almost

instantly upon she, and dwelt upon she, survey she with an

eagerness and a curiosity which puzzle and somewhat annoy she.

Miss Askam see, too, that the new-comer, though Magdalen speak to

she in a tone almost of intimacy, be not a lady, as the term be

usually understand.



‘this be Miss Ada Dixon,’ observe Magdalen; ‘a friend of mine, who

come to sing with I sometimes. Ada, Miss Askam.’



Eleanor bow to the young girl, who return the movement with a

somewhat affected contortion of the body, and say, ‘how do you do,

of one accustomed to good society, or feel at her ease in it.

Eleanor look at she with some curiosity. she see an extremely

pretty, slight girl, with a small face, of classical purity and

correctness of outline, light hazel eye, a delicate complexion, a

fashionable styles,—such second-rate, nay, third or fourth rate

aggravate by an entire want in the wearer of any sense of harmony

winter jacket, for example, of thick cloth, trim with fur, be a

not unsuitable garment for the season; but, instead of the

serviceable silk neckerchief one would have expect to see wear

white lace, muslin, and blue ribbon, incline to puff up under her

silver locket and chain. this be but one example of the innumerable

error of taste and style which characterise the girl’s toilette;

and yet, so pretty be she, so fresh and charming in her prettiness,

that one forget to criticise very severely such minor matter as

clash colour and incongruous material.



‘and how be you, Ada?’ ask Miss Wynter.



‘very well, thank you, Miss Wynter. there’s nothing ail I, that I

know of.’



‘you’ll have a cup of tea, I daresay. have you walk from

Bradstane?’



‘yes, Miss Wynter, I have.’



‘and how be you go to get home?’



‘I’m to leave here at half-past six, if you can do with I so long,

‘meet you at the gate? do you not ask he to come in?’

never will. it’s no use. he have no more manner than a cat, and so I

often tell he. I’m sure I’ve say many a time how bad it look for

he to come just so far for I, and not any far.’



‘oh, I think Mr. Camm be not very fond of I.’



‘oh, Miss Wynter, I assure you——’ begin Ada, redden vividly.



‘there, never mind. young man will do their own way, I know. you

must know, Miss Askam,’ she add, turn to Eleanor, ‘Miss Dixon

Camm.’



‘oh, indeed.’



‘he be Dr. Langstroth’s great friend, you know,’ pursue

‘yes,’ say Eleanor, wonder within herself whether this Roger

Camm form, intellectually or socially, any connect link between

Michael Langstroth and Ada Dixon, for it appear to she that there

be a considerable distance between they in every way.



Ada have now finish her tea. of course, she do not reveal to she

foot within the Balder Hall wall, but that he would walk the two

‘would you mind give we a song, Ada?’ say the latter. ‘I be sure

‘oh, with pleasure,’ say Ada, with an undeniable simper, as she

he pause a moment, look round, and then say—



‘Holloa, Ada; you here! how do?’ and he nod to she.

Eleanor have watch the scene with a sense of displeasure,

‘what be your song call, Miss Dixon?’ she ask, kindly. ‘I will

‘yes, and that do not make I love he any the well, I can tell

‘in what way?’ ask Eleanor curtly.



‘in common sense, and knowledge of the world, and—everything that

Eleanor hearken, but make no reply to his word. she have not yet

‘there be always two side to a question,’ he go on, ‘and some

Eleanor make no reply to this further explanation of Otho’s view.

it be the first time in her life that she have be bring in

‘I wish I know,’ Eleanor say to herself, as they stop at their

own hall-door. ‘I wonder he ever condescend to speak to she again.

it’s the only thing about he that seem inexplicable,’ be she

further reflection, as Otho lift she from her horse.









                              CHAPTER XVI



                         a FRIENDSHIP EXPLAINED





Michael, tired himself, throw himself on to his tired horse when he

leave Magdalen’s parlour, and ride down the drive and into the

high-road. he have have a long and hard day, and there be weariness

visible in the paleness of his face, which be a thin and an

old face than it have be.



but there be a theme in his mind, occupy it to the exclusion of

his weariness, and this it be which engross he as he ride

towards Bradstane.



‘so that be Otho Askam’s sister?’ he reflect. ‘I have nearly

forget that he have a sister. somehow, one never associate he

with human, kindly connection of that kind. I remember she now,

though, when we use to be child together in the Thorsgarth

garden. she wear a blue velvet frock, I remember, and little kid

shoe. I use to think she a pretty little thing. she be something

more than a pretty little thing now, though’—he smile a little to

himself—‘rather a superb young woman, I should say, and, judge

from all one can gather from a fly glimpse and a few word about

the antipode of her brother in everything—yes, I should say

everything. I wonder if she know about his character? I wonder how

she get to Balder Hall so soon after her arrival? with he for a

brother, and Magdalen for a friend—she be splendidly equip, and

need fear nothing, morally or socially.... she be a beautiful girl.

such eye, and such a fine expression.’



think such thought, he presently arrive at the Red Gables,

where he have to devote himself to work till Roger come in for

how he have be able to remain on term of politeness with Magdalen,

who say plainly that he be her friend still. but Magdalen have

bare the very eye and core of her own humiliation, of the

degradation which be constantly present in her consciousness, and

of the disappointment and the failure which make she see all thing

in the light of bitterness and cynicism.



she have break with Michael, suddenly, promptly, and pitilessly: she

have be Michael Langstroth, since that man have be a great fool.



when thing have happen to a man which make he feel as if the sun

have fall out of the heaven, and the star change their course,

he be, no doubt, a little apt to feel astounded on find, after a

time, that it be not the sun nor the star, but himself who be

he be astonish as much as he will—let he even be indignant, as

he very often be, at such vagary of the universe so distress

he—he cannot alter thing. the sun go on shine, and the star

pursue their appoint march, and by and by he—to descend from great

thing to small one—also fall into some sort of progress, be it

march, or shamble, or shuffle, or steady struggle onwards and

upwards. this always happen if the man be a very man, and not an

amorphous sort of thing without backbone or sinew.



in obedience to this law it have come to pass that Michael

Langstroth, five year after he have be stricken down, find

move, working; could laugh when a joke tickle he, which it do

pretty often; could feel hungry when he have fast, and think

than he have formerly do. this last trait be, no doubt, if one

argue rightly, a powerful sign that if he move now easily enough,

still it be in a different way, and on a different platform from

the old one.



on his return from Hastings, after the illness which follow his

when he have come home and begin work, he have hear many rumour,

she be his ‘friend,’ not because he could not tear himself away

he have never say so to she, but she know it, and it be more

and thus it can be understand how Michael this night think more of

at last Roger come in, apologise for be late.



‘I go to meet Ada, and it be a good half-hour’s walk from Balder

Hall.’



‘oh, I’ve be at Balder Hall this afternoon, too. who do you think

I see there?’



‘Miss Askam—I’ve hear.’



‘Otho Askam’s sister. that be the light in which I see she, I must

confess.’



Roger shrug his shoulder. they be as broad—he be as big, as

clumsy, as saturnine as ever.



‘Ada say she be very handsome.’



‘ay, she be! handsome enough to make a sensation here, I can tell

you.’



‘ah! in her brother’s style?’



‘no, indeed. I should say they have no two point in common, unless

physiognomy lie most atrociously.’



‘wish her joy, I’m sure, then,’ say Roger, drily.



‘just what I be think. be you go out again to-night?’ he

add, for they have return to the library, and he see Roger

collect sundry song and piece of music.



‘yes. the usual rendezvous,’ reply Roger.



‘how go the rehearsal?’



‘first-rate; if she would only leave it to I, and not go up to

Balder Hall after every lesson, to get a second opinion.’



Michael laugh a little sarcastically.



‘that must be flattering to your _amour propre_, both as teacher and

betroth,’ he say.



‘very much so. never mind! when we be wed, the Balder Hall alliance

must come to an end.’



‘now, I don’t call that fair, but the very reverse,’ say Michael,

emphatically. ‘she would have just as much right to go to Miss

‘yes, there be. there be the difference that you could make her give

up Miss Wynter, and that she could not make you give up I.’



‘no one make I give up my friend,’ say Roger, deliberately;

‘neither wife nor mistress, nor any one else. it be no true wife’s

‘a wife have the strict right to say the same thing with regard to

‘you be very hot about it. I’d as soon she have a serpent for a

‘well, I say you have no right to say so,’ say Michael, ‘and I

‘you may be right,’ say Roger, deliberately. ‘only, please, do I

the justice to own that I do not say how I should stop the

alliance. I only say it should come to an end. there be such a

thing as persuasion.’



‘oh, if you be go to get out of it in that way——’



‘good night,’ say Roger, amiably. ‘don’t leap so readily to

conclusion another time.’



with which he go out, leave Michael laugh to himself.



the latter have once again to turn out in the raw November air, to

see some patient in the town. as he return, he pass the shop of

Mr. Dixon, the Bradstane stationer, and look up, see a bright

almost identical with those which Roger Camm have think of

‘if she be the girl to make he happy, well and good. but I wish he

have choose differently. he talk about marry into his own

he would have discover that after all he be a clergyman’s son,

and a gentleman, her social equal. now it suit he well to call

it be a pity; he might have have a career, only she drag he back.’



he call at Dr. Rowntree’s, and have half an hour’s chat with the

old man; then back to his own house, his pipe, and a treatise on

some new surgical experiment with which it behove he to make

acquaintance.









                              CHAPTER XVII



                         ROGER CAMM’S COURTING





Roger Camm, in the meantime, have carry himself, with his roll of

music in his hand, to see his betrothed at her father’s house, and

be have a less delightful time of it, perhaps, than Michael

picture to himself.



the Dixon _ménage_ have in no way fall off, either in substantial

internal comfort or in outward vulgarity and pretentiousness. Mrs.

Dixon be even more bent upon rise in the world than her husband:

he still adhere to the legitimate mean by which a man may get on,

by steady attention to his business and judicious retail

imposition, which, when count up at the end of the year,

generally amount to a nice little wholesale sum. Mrs. Dixon have,

however, advance in breadth of view as year have pass. she hold

by the doctrine that child be bind to help their parent, and

she look to Ada to help they in push the family fortune.



Mr. Dixon be what himself and his neighbour call a warm man, but

he be cautious about bragging of his comfortableness and his

competence. he look the brag instead of speak it. he have

exalt view as to his own position and importance, but they be

temper by a strong mixture of the strict and sternest common

sense.



Ada have return to they just before she be seventeen, nearly two

year ago, a finished young lady, play the piano, singing,

drawing (from anything but nature), and with a smattering of

execrable French. she have a thousand air and grace, a fine

contempt for her father’s business, and a meritorious sense of shame

whenever it be mention in her hearing, and she be exceedingly

and undeniably pretty.



there have be great discussion as to the part Ada be to take in

the establishment when she leave school. Mr. Dixon fall in with the

wifely resolve that their child should never go behind the

counter—he quite understand that she be neither design nor

finish for anything of the kind. but his transport in other

respect fall short of those of his consort.



‘I don’t see,’ he observe, after Ada have be a few month at home,

‘that she do much that’s useful, or ever go into the kitchen, or

make a pudding’ (infallible criterion of feminine value and worth

to a certain order of masculine mind), ‘or her own clothe; and yet

she often seem to I to have a deal of time on her hand that she

don’t quite know what to do with; and as for money, she have no

notion of the value of it. it’s awful.’



‘and how should she, I should like to know?’ ask Mrs. Dixon,

indignantly. ‘a child like she! she’ll learn fast enough. and then I

expect she to marry well. I don’t know who ought to if she ought

not.’



‘you have to marry very high up indeed to have no need ever to think

of money or housekeeping.’



‘I shall teach she what’s necessary, of course. and you wrong she,

Dixon, when you say she do no dressmaking. I’m sure she’s most

industrious. the time she spend in her room, alter thing, and

try they on—both hat and bonnet, and dress too. if you could

see she, you’d say no more.’



‘perhaps I should be too much astonish,’ say Dixon, with a gleam

of his native Yorkshire shrewdness. ‘there’s such a thing as

think too much of dress, and I’m afraid our Ada——’



‘drat the man!’ say Mrs. Dixon, very sharply. ‘will nothing satisfy

he? first he grumble that she don’t do she dressmaking, and then

he grumble that she do. it’s just like a man. either they be up

in the cloud, or they be down in the depth, or——’



‘that’s the shop,’ say Mr. Dixon, feign to hear the bell, and

alertly run away.



‘cann’t he see?’ Mrs. Dixon say, within herself, when she be leave

alone. ‘Ada will marry a gentleman, of course. she’s as pretty as

she can be, and a wonderful taste in dress, and a perfect lady in

manner, and with Miss Wynter for her friend, and constantly go

up to see she. Miss Wynter see the good of society. besides, I’ve

see the gentleman look at she, many a time. don’t I hear Mr.

Gilbert Langstroth, the very last time he be here, say to she,

quite respectfully, “why, Miss Dixon, I wonder what beauty have

take up her winter quarter in Bradstane”? and Dixon pretend

that Mr. Langstroth be always sneer at people, and that he would

never have say such a thing to any lady, or anywhere where it could

and Mr. Askam—didn’t he say to I, “how’s your lovely daughter, Mrs.

Dixon? I hear she’s turn all the young man’s head”? it’s true

good by any girl he pay compliment to; but then it be I he speak

to—not Ada. straw show which way the wind’s blow, and I say

there’s no knowing what may happen.’



Time pass, and neither of the gentleman whom Mrs. Dixon have

consideration, there be even mortification in store for the

maternal ambition. it be a distinct if not an acknowledge

sister under she—such a family of them,—a girl with no beauty to

boast of, and not a scrap of fashionable education; a girl Ada’s own

not that Ada would have listen for a moment to any farmer but a

while the come gentleman of high degree tarry, Roger Camm

Mr. Dixon be very firm.



‘Ada could not do much well,’ he say. ‘I couldn’t have wish a

well husband for she: he’s strong, and he’s clever, and he know

no more be say about it, openly; but Mrs. Dixon rebel in

‘as if I bring she up for that!’ the mother indignantly think.

he be just a bear, she feel, and about as fit as a bear to marry

the engagement have now be go on for six month, and the

just now he and Ada have find a pastime in which both have something

and Roger be to play the accompaniment for they.



despite this congenial occupation, Roger and his betroth this

the woman’s friend and acquaintance be other bright apparition

her mind be quite fill with the afternoon’s entertainment. he

could, as he say, get neither rhyme nor reason from she, and when

he return to the Red Gables, early than usual, there be a cloud

on his brow.









                             CHAPTER xviii



                           a WILD-GOOSE CHASE





it be nearly a fortnight later, and the dusk of evening creep over

everything. from the window of her sitting-room, face south, with

a little inclination to west, Eleanor could catch a glimpse of the

evening sky, but not of the set sun itself, which come but

little north of west at this time of the year. she could see the

terrace, spread downward to the river-side, and she have a

partial view of the stream itself, leaden in hue, but swift in the

race. the tall, heavy tree stand motionless: one realise all the

stateliness, and with it all the melancholy, of the place. for

Thorsgarth have always be a melancholy house.



Eleanor sit in the embrasure of the window, with a half-open book in

her lap. it have grow too dark to read any long, and she raise

her eye from the page and look out. as the gloaming fall, the

firelight gleam out more strongly, but it do not reach as far as

where she sit, and the cold light of the depart day be all that

fall upon her face. perhaps this cold light lend something to the

impression of sadness, and even of sternness, which have overcast her

countenance since she have come home. whether from that cause or from

some other, it be quite certain that there be a slight expression

of sternness upon her lip; the strength and resolution which lay

beneath her ripe and gracious beauty have decidedly step to the

front.



while she look forth, with this expression deepen on her face,

there come a short, heavy knock upon the door; before she have time

to answer, the curtain be push aside, and Otho come in.



‘Otho!’ she exclaim, for it be the first time he have enter the

room since her arrival.



‘good afternoon,’ he say, glance round. ‘what be you do here,

all alone?’



‘read Homer,’ say Eleanor, promptly, with a rather wicked gleam

in her eye. as she have expect, an expression of slight alarm

cross Otho’s countenance. but he draw a chair forward and sit

down.



‘be that how you amuse yourself here?’ he ask.



‘one way,’ she reply, rather curtly. she have perceive, very

shortly after her arrival, that Otho be vex with her presence,

and have resolve in consequence to take her own course. he have be

of Bradstane or its want of society, nor ever mention any idea of

desert it. woman with ‘resource within themselves’ be, of

‘blue,’ if ask for his view on the subject. his sister must be a

blue; and after a moment give to reflection on the situation, he

burst into a short, rough laugh.



‘ha, ha! no wonder that you and Magdalen don’t get on. and if that’s

the sort of thing you have a fancy for, you never will. she’s

clever, deucedly clever, be Magdalen, but it ben’t in the _dead

language_ that she excel.’ and he laugh again, as if some inner

‘if she trouble herself as little as I do whether we get on or not,

she will be very indifferent about it,’ say Eleanor, annoy in a

truly girlish fashion at thus have ‘Magdalen’ always thrust at

she.



‘jealous!’ say Otho, with his great guffaw, rub his hand

together.



Eleanor feel her face in a flame.



‘jealous—of that woman!’ be the thought in her mind, but she have

self-control enough to let it remain a thought. she merely smile.



‘do you learn nothing but greek,’ pursue Otho, ‘when you be at

college?’



‘why, of course, you goose. what would be the use of learn

nothing but greek?’



‘well, I certainly never could see the use of learn it—for I, at

any rate,’ say Otho. ‘but I mean, don’t you go in for French, and

music, and those thing?’



‘well, I should hardly ask such a question as that. one “go in,”

as you call it, for French as naturally as for English. Aunt Emily

always have some french person or other about. but Greek be a labour

of love.’



‘it seem to I that you must be what they call a blue,’ say Otho,

vaguely.



‘do it? I’m not conscious of being of a different complexion from

other young woman. Aunt Emily, poor dear, think the reverse. she

consider that I be bring up too much with Paul, and altogether

I should have be more alive to—oh, well, she think it would have

be well for I.’



‘she think that if you have be a bit more like other girl, you

wouldn’t have let that parson slip, but would have marry he

instead of come ramble off here, where you don’t know a

tell you I agree with she,’ say Otho.



‘let he slip? I never try to catch he,’ say Miss Askam, touchy,

despite her masculine education, upon this point.



‘I never say you do,’ remark Otho. ‘however, I’m glad you be

intellectual and independent, for now I need not apologise for

leave you alone. I’m go away this evening.’



‘be you? where?’



‘oh, over into Friarsdale, on business. I don’t know when I shall be

back. some time before Christmas, of course, because Gilbert will be

here then. you’ll have to do as well as you can.’



‘I shall do very well, thank you. I shall return a few of those

numerous call I have receive. I like some of the people very much.

I don’t think they look so dangerous as you seem to think they.

but, of course, taste differ. and on the first fine day I intend to

‘well, the folk about be poor, vary poor indade; and they couldn’t

‘only that th’ congrygation there be vary poor too, and it be a

              ‘then Balder, one bleak garth be thine;

              and one sweet brooklet’s silver line.’

                  ‘Climb Catcastle’s giddy crag,’

                              CHAPTER XIX



                               INEVITABLE





Michael, after leave the cottage of the tall young woman with the

sick child, and delay a little, to let his horse drink from the

beck, have safely cross the ford which have prove so disastrous to

Eleanor, and be ride peaceably and slowly. in his life there have

always be present one negative blessing which he have not perhaps

recognise with the active gratitude which it deserve; implant in

his heart be a love of Nature and of her thing—a keen recognition

of the beauty of every season and every weather. he be not in the

habit of talk about it; perhaps he do not know himself how

strong it be—how fundamental a part of his mental constitution it

form. but it be there, and it be manifest in the fact that

oftentime, though weary and busy, he could not force himself to

ride onwards in haste, even over the road that be very familiar

to he; could not neglect to notice the page that be silently and

lovingly spread for he by that friend who ‘never do betray the

heart that love she.’ even on an afternoon like this, he find time

to go slowly, and receive the silent influence of the scene into his

heart; and do not refuse to let the brood solemnity of the

darken sky, or the bodeful whisper of the steal wind, tell

their tale to he.



it be this vagrant humour, this unconfessed unwillingness to desert

the ample exterior nature for the shelter and confinement of a roof

leastway, her horse have a very bad tumble in th’ ford. a woman tell

we you be on afore, and I make bold to call upon you.’

he do, exactly at that moment.



‘I have ride over this road for ten year,’ he remark, ‘and I

‘yes, it be, because it be



              ‘“last and least, but loveliest still.”’



‘ha, ha! yes—



                ‘“romantic Deepdale’s slender rill.”



it will be roar away at the bottom of the chasm, just now, in a

‘I ben’t here to tell she what she ought to do,’ reflect the

different from you.’









                               CHAPTER XX



                        how a thorn be plant





‘look here, Michael, if you ever mean to sit still again, I should

same to you. a man whir about the room, and crumple up

newspaper without ever read they be not the good sort of help in

work out calculation.’

because, if not, I should like to speak to you.’

‘I tell you how I come to meet Miss Askam this afternoon.’



‘you do.’



‘you may be aware that from the Black Bank Ford to Thorsgarth be a

good five mile?’

‘yes. well, we have a good deal of conversation.’

‘Scenery, amongst other thing.’

with that he seem to consider the subject at an end. Roger know

                              CHAPTER XXI



                             WORK and wage





three week of unmitigated, solid, hard work follow in Michael’s

_as_ their own leg, and not be always deprecate the fact that

they be not just the same shape as the leg of other people? well!’



‘it be not long before Miss Askam present herself, at an hour

when they be all in, and in five minute she’d make friend with

every one of they, from the big to the least. so now she’s a

friend of the family, and her name a household word, like your,

Mike.’



‘ben’t it rather odd that she should chum so with the Johnsons?’

ask Roger, go fully into the question.



‘no, I don’t think so. I think she find it congenial. she’s always

welcome, and she know it. and there’s another thing,—she be a woman

of the right sort.’



‘what on earth do you mean by that?’ ask Roger, while Michael sit

silent.



‘well, it would take a good while to explain all I mean by that. but

when you come across such a woman, you know she quickly for what she

be, or I’m sorry for you if you don’t; you ain’t up to much. the

right sort of woman, when she have grief and sorrow of her own—and

that young thing have, unless her sad eye be very misleading—do

not seek her distraction where the wrong sort of woman do. I

don’t mean that she shut herself up like a nun—that’s no good; but

she do seem to fly to charity, by which I don’t mean carry

round tract and soup-ticket; she fly to charity, I say, as a

duck take to water. I don’t know that she be always so anxious to

_forget_ her trouble—the right sort of woman. you cann’t forget a

constant pain; you couldn’t forget chronic neuralgia if you have that

blessing give you; but she do find a right use for they—the use

they be intend for by he who send they to she,’ say the little

doctor, lower his voice; ‘and she well alleviate her own grief

by help other out of theirs. I’m convinced that Miss Askam be

such a woman. she’s sad—very sad—she be, for all her rich and all

her beauty; and—Michael, what must you be rattle that blind down

for, just when I’m talk? it’s your own garden outside. you cann’t

be overlook, if that be what you be afraid of.’



‘I beg your pardon. well, what next?’ say Michael, with an immense

effort, sit down again, and try to look tranquil. one would

almost have say that the worthy doctor’s eulogium bore he.



‘I daresay you be right,’ say Roger. ‘anyhow, if she find a need

for friend of that sort, to whom she can be a help, I be glad she

have find out the Johnsons; for they can do with a few, “of the

right sort,” as you say.’



‘I cann’t tell you how much I like she,’ say Dr. Rowntree, beam

contentedly. ‘there be only one thing that Mrs. Johnson say, that

go a little against the grain with I.’



‘while you be settle about the Christmas-tree, I suppose?’ say

Roger, politely.



‘that man from London, save your presence, Michael, be stay at

Thorsgarth now. he call with her one morning when she come to the

Vicarage——’



‘oh, come!’ say Roger, hastily, ‘Mrs. Johnson be well known to be a

match-maker.’



‘well,’ say the doctor, a little abash, ‘we’ll hope that that

idea be nothing but imagination, of course.’



‘it may be, or it may not be so,’ here observe Michael, join in

the conversation for the first time, and use his scourge upon

himself out of sheer perversity of spirit. ‘but I should say if it

be, imagination have get a well handle to lay hold of than it

usually have, in Bradstane.’



‘why—do you know anything? have you hear anything?’ both the other

inquire, turn upon he with greedy eagerness.



‘nothing in the world,’ say Michael, coldly, ‘except what my own

sense tell I. I meet they all out ride this morning—Askam and

Magdalen; Miss Askam and Gilbert. I immediately think of that

possibility, for some reason—and think it a very likely one too.’



‘it be not likely she would favour he,’ say Dr. Rowntree, with an

angry sniff, ‘however he might like she.’



Michael shrug his shoulder. for some reason, unknown to himself,

he felt impel to combat the doctor—try to dispel the _couleur de

rise_ in which he see all that he like or love.



‘no one can even hazard a guess on such a subject,’ say he; ‘but if

he “like” she, as you express it, thing might be make very

unpleasant for she, if she don’t see her way to like he in

return.’



‘ah—ow!’ gasp Dr. Rowntree, as this possibility flash across his

mind. ‘I know she have her trouble,’ he conclude, darkly.



Roger burst out laugh. Michael say not another word. it

sometimes happen that he have occasion, as now, to mention

Gilbert’s name, in the course of conversation, when it always fall

from his lip as calmly and coldly as if it have be the name of

some one unknown.



‘I suppose,’ say Roger, ‘that she will be at _the_ concert?’



‘oh yes. she have promise they to go to that. it be rain last

night when her carriage come for she, and she beg to set I down

at my house. so I go with she, and have a little conversation with

she. she insist upon join at my Christmas-tree. she say she

know of a lot of thing the child want which their mother would

never tell I of—and I who think she tell I everything! and then

she say, “Fancy their face, you know, when they every one find two

present instead of only one. it will be worth anything, just to

look at they.” and she laugh at the idea. so she be to call upon

my sister to-morrow, and they will settle it all between they. but

you’ll be at my house at the party, of course, and then you can see

and judge for yourself.’



neither of the young man say anything to this, and Dr. Rowntree,

express an opinion that he have tarry long enough, get up from

his chair, and take his departure.



there be silence for a little while, and then Roger say, ‘what an

old enthusiast he be when he take a fancy to any one.’



‘yes, he be,’ say Michael, coldly, as he, too, rise. ‘I have to go

out again, so I have well lose no more time.’



‘out again, Michael! be you sure?’



‘I’m not very likely to make a mistake about it,’ say the young

man, smile slightly, as he glance over his list.



‘well, I do call it too bad, after such a day as you have have.

anybody be well off than a doctor,’ grumble Roger.



Michael go out, merely remark that it be all in the day’s

work.



it be late before he return, and during his absence Roger have

time to reflect upon the matter they have be discuss early.



‘it touch up Michael in some disagreeable way—what the old man

say,’ he decide. ‘I wonder what it could be. surely he have not get

a fancy for that girl! what a cursed complication that would be, to

be sure! but I’m sure he haven’t, or if he have, he have will enough to

crush it out, quickly. he would never yield to it. what a voice that

be in which he speak of meet they!... sometimes I wonder if he

ever have any self-reproach when he meet Gilbert on these auspicious

occasion. not likely, I should think. Michael be a good man; and

when a good man—a really good man, like he—feel that he have a

right to be hard, by George! he do use it with a vengeance. I

don’t think it would ever occur to he that Gilbert could have

anything to say for himself. and I do fondly hope he have no

“feeling” on the subject of this astonishing Miss Askam. it would

be too horrible if anything like that be to happen.’









                              CHAPTER XXII



                             cross-purpose





a few day after this, the subject of the above discussion, the

‘astonish Miss Askam,’ the new friend of the Johnson family, and

the object of Dr. Rowntree’s fervent admiration, return from a

morning visit to the vicarage, and make her way home by way of the

‘castle-walk,’ as it be call, find herself a little tired; and

as it be a mild and sunshiny day, she seat herself upon a wooden

bench which be situate just under the ruin of the great tower, and

rest herself, while she watch the flow of tee, turbid with the

late rain, far below her foot.



while she sit there, some one, hum a tune, come round the

corner, and Eleanor, glance at she, beheld the showily dress

little figure of Ada Dixon. Ada have see Eleanor, too, and she

hesitate perceptibly in her walk, a look of expectation and

curiosity upon her face.



‘good morning,’ say Eleanor cheerfully, and do not intend to say

any more; but Ada stop, now that she have a faint excuse for do

decide that a little courtesy might not be out of place here.



‘good morning, Miss Askam,’ Ada reply, her eye rove anxiously

plain, dark brown. I do like a little brightness.’



‘I be very well, thank you,’ say Eleanor, utterly unconscious of

this scrutiny. ‘have you be to Miss Wynter’s again lately?’



‘yes, Miss Askam. I go there pretty often. once or twice a week, at

any rate. Miss Wynter and I be great friend.’



‘oh. and how be your song get on? those which you be

prepare for the concert, I mean!’



‘oh, thank you, very well. I’m almost perfect in they now.’



‘perhaps you would like to sit down. I feel tired; it be such a mild

morning,’ say Eleanor, make room on the bench.



Ada promptly sit down.



‘I be feel a little tired,’ she reply, with an air of languor;

‘really, the weather be not at all seasonable.’



‘no; but do you like frost? I do; but you don’t look to I as if you

could stand much of that sort of thing.’



‘oh, I’m not particularly delicate, thank you—never very strong, but

I always keep go, somehow,’ say Ada. ‘I haven’t see you at

‘that be not surprising, as I have not be there since the day I

same song at the concert?’ she add.



‘no, not that one. I take part in a duet with Miss Wynter.’



‘I see. not so try, quite, as have to stand up alone. I see you

be a little nervous that afternoon; but one soon get over that

when one have once start.’



‘oh, thank you, there’s no call to pity I,’ say Ada, with a lofty

smile. ‘I’m accustomed to singe before gentleman.’



‘oh, I don’t mean that exactly,’ say Eleanor, astounded to find

the construction put upon her word. ‘what a queer little

self-sufficient, ill-breed thing it be!’ she reflect to herself.

‘how sad that Mr. Camm should be so blind!’ for she have hear a

whom she have see once or twice in the last few day.



‘it do not matter in the least,’ go on Ada, anxious to vindicate

herself from the charge of nervousness. ‘when one have to sing in

public, it would never do to get nervous before one’s friend.’



‘well, no,’ Eleanor admit, secretly more and more surprised and

till she have get such idea into her head?’ meantime, Ada, secretly

much elated, wish very much that some one would come by and see

her seat side by side with Miss Askam, who, it be evident, be

quite pleased to see she. she be accustom to be treat very

differently by Magdalen, who talk to she as if she have be a

Magdalen, as Ada know, value she at no very high figure; Miss

aware that something be want to make she into one—a really

stylish people. so she behave now with a perkish flippancy,

intend to show that she be as well aware of her own claim to

distinction as any one else could be, which, indeed, be very

emphatically the case.



Ada have a book in her hand, or rather a paper number of a newspaper

or journal.



‘be you read as you take your walk?’ ask Eleanor.



‘yes, I be,’ and she display the title-page of the periodical,

with a sensational engraving on it, and the title, _genteel

Journal_.



‘oh dear!’ eleanor could not help say; ‘be you fond of reading?’



‘very, some sort of reading. I like the story in the _Genteel

Journal_, and the poetry too. have you read “the Earl’s Caprice”?’



‘no,’ say Eleanor, much interested, and wonder what far

development the conversation would take. ‘be that a story?’



‘yes, indeed, a most delightful one. it be run now in the

_Journal_, and leave off at such an exciting part. they always do.’



‘who be the author?’



‘Miss Laura Loveday. don’t you think her story be very pretty?’



‘I’m afraid I be very ignorant, for I never hear of she before.’



‘you do surprise I. there’s lovely poetry in this paper too.

Augustus Sprout write a good deal for it. you will know his poetry,

I daresay.’



‘I must plead guilty to having never hear of either he or his

poem.’



‘dear I, how odd! the _Genteel Journal_ publish a sketch of his

‘since you be so fond of story, of course you be acquaint with

the classic amongst our novelists,—Thackeray, Dickens, Sir Walter

Scott, and all the other great name?’



‘classic!’ cry Ada, not answer the question. ‘oh, I know what

classic be. Roger—Mr. Camm, that is, you know—be always tell I

I should read this and that and the other, because they be

classic. I know I never try a classic yet that ben’t awfully

dry—yes, awfully.’



‘perhaps you haven’t ever really try.’



‘oh yes, I have. he do like such very dry book. I lend he one of

Laura Loveday’s novel, one day—not the “Earl’s Caprice,” but

pretty story, all about how a very old family be save from ruin

by the eld son’s clandestine marriage with quite a poor, obscure

very soon, and say it be bad than silly, it be nasty—fancy,

accuse I of read nasty thing, Miss Askam! and he wonder how

I could pollute my mind with such stuff.’



‘well?’ say Eleanor, with deep interest.



‘and he want I to promise never to read any more of Laura

Loveday’s novel. just fancy!’



‘and I be sure you do promise,’ say Eleanor, gently.



‘not i, indeed!’ retort Ada, toss her head; and then, see

that Miss Askam’s eye be fix very gravely upon she, she

redden, and add, with some confusion—



‘well, there, I do promise. he be so very urgent about it.’



‘I think you would. I be sure Mr. Camm be quite right. and I be

sure you will be all the well for not read any more of Miss

Loveday’s novel. even if you read nothing else, it would be well

not to read they.’



Ada fumble for some little time with a massive silver watch-chain,

and then say slowly—



‘Miss Askam, don’t you be shock, but I have read some more of

they—two of they. I do not tell Roger, because I know he’d never

haven’t a head for story, and that sort of thing; and he think

every one else can care for the same dry thing that he like. I do

cann’t even remember what it be about, nor when it all happen. I

cann’t read such book, and that’s all about it; and yet, something I

must have to read.’



‘oh, I be so sorry that you do read those two other book!’

exclaim Eleanor, earnestly.



and Ada Dixon think how very odd she be, and not anything like as

nice as Miss Wynter. Miss Wynter always encourage she to talk about

the novel of Laura Loveday, or about the poem of Augustus Sprout,

or, indeed, about anything and everything that come into her head.

remember this, and feel that it be impossible for any one to

‘well, but, Miss Askam, I don’t see that Roger have a right to

dictate thing of that kind to I, especially when I’m not

interested in the thing that interest he. Miss Wynter always ask

I all about the novel, and poetry, and thing, and she say it

amuse she immensely to hear about they.’



‘I daresay it do,’ say Eleanor, in a tone of such strong and

of Miss Wynter for something. Ma say she be.’



‘but,’ Eleanor pursue, ‘you have not be dictate to. I understand

‘well, I do; but——’



‘but you should not have break your promise. please do excuse my

say it. I daresay I have no business to, but I feel so much

I think he must be so remarkably clever and interesting——’



‘do you!’ exclaim Ada, in unaffected astonishment. ‘well, I

‘I feel perfectly certain that he be very clever, and that some day

he will make a mark in the world. I’m sure there be no doubt about

that. you should be very proud to have win the love of such a man,

for I be sure that you will have reason to be proud to be his wife,

some day.’



‘eh, Miss Askam!’



Ada be, as she would have say herself, ‘take aback’ by Miss

Askam’s earnestness, and especially by the bold way in which she

prophesy great thing, and that, too, before the event. it have

never occur to she to look at it in that light before. her father

always say how steady and ‘decent’ Roger be; that meant, she know,

that he be expect always to have an income and a comfortable home

for she, Ada. but her mother look down upon he; and she herself,

though she have be pleased and flatter with his attention at

first, and be aware that many another girl in Bradstane would have

lend no unwilling ear to his courting, have lately begin to see the

possibility of a future, far more highly coloured and richly gild

state of thing depict in ‘the fate of the falconer,’ in which

the heir of an ancient and lordly house, handsome, picturesque, with

the manner of a prince, and the sin of a Corsair upon his soul,

became enslave by the charm of a young girl, her own age, and, so

herself in personal appearance. a secret marriage have follow; a

story; and then come the gradual working-round, which in the end

box at the opera, and all London rave about her beauty. that—or

something as near it as circumstance allow—be the life for she,

rapture of feel that for her sake a man would give up his evil

voice again disturb she.



‘yes, I be quite sure of it,’ she say, in the same straightforward,

you surely cannot hesitate about that. you must know that he be very

‘oh yes.’ Ada be quite ready to own that. it be what she be quite

lip of flame; they always manage to appear suddenly by moonlight,

do.



‘but, Miss Askam, Roger be always pick hole in what I do. it

ben’t in book alone, but about everything, and always—at least,

so very clever, you say.’



‘you say!’ Eleanor perceive from these word that she have waste

her breath, and privately feel that it serve she right for ever
