‘indeed, I wish you would.’



‘well, in a way, I think you ought to be proud to be even find

fault with by he. he would not do it if he do not care very much

for you, and also feel sure that you have it in you to grow into

something high and well.’



‘well, I don’t know. Roger be satisfied enough when I say I would

have he,’ say Ada, discontentedly.



‘but,’ say Eleanor, the slow, deep blush come over her face, and

hesitating as she speak, ‘if you love he very much, as of course

you do——?’



‘I—oh yes, I’m very fond of he, of course,’ say Ada, unwillingly.



‘then you willn’t be satisfied with yourself, I should think, but will

want to rise higher and become well and well, so as to be more

worthy of he.’



‘Worthy of he!’ echo Ada, offend. ‘I’m as good as he be. he’s

not the only one I could have have. no, and I needn’t be sit in

the dust to keep he. if it be all off to-morrow I could have

another next week.’



‘could you, indeed?’ say Eleanor, coldly. ‘I think we probably

don’t agree upon the matter, and have well not say any more about

it. you ask my opinion, or I certainly should not have speak to

you on such a subject.’



she rise, and see Ada’s flushed and discomfited look, could not

continue vex with she.



‘I be sorry if I have annoy you,’ she say, frankly, and smile

her bright smile. she offer Ada her hand, add, still with a

smile, ‘you must forgive I, and don’t let Mr. Camm know that you

have be get lecture from some one else. I expect he prefer

to keep a monopoly of they.’



Ada could not rise to the occasion. she shake hand rather

sheepishly, mutter something about it ‘not matter,’ and the two

separate.



after lunch, late in the afternoon, and when it be grow dusk,

Eleanor be sit in the library. she have find that when she do

this, Otho, especially now that he have a friend with he, would

sometimes stroll in towards evening and sit for an hour. this

afternoon he do so, follow by Gilbert. they have be shoot,

say they be thirsty, and crave for tea, which she give they.



if Eleanor look grave in these day, the gravity be partly cause

by the fact that she could not reconcile the Gilbert Langstroth of

whom she have hear so much, and from so many person, with the

Gilbert Langstroth who be Otho’s guest, and her own frequent

companion. she naturally abhor what she have hear of he; she have

receive he with cold civility, and be in every way dispose to

keep he at a distance and cherish exalt thought of his brother.

but she have find it impossible. strongly bias though she be

against Gilbert, and for Michael, she could not succeed in find

Gilbert detestable. reason as she would, she could not make herself

find he personally disagreeable, or be bore, vex, or harass by

his company. he have great power, she have have to confess—power to

make himself welcome, look for, agreeable, his opinion valuable,

and his influence desire; while his marvellous command over Otho

call forth her gratitude, and force she into an attitude of

half-cordial, half-reluctant civility to he and respect for he. it

be the effort to reconcile this Gilbert Langstroth who have suddenly

appear in her life, with the Gilbert Langstroth of year ago, of

whom and of whose treachery one uniform story be everywhere tell,

that help to make her grave, and give a shade of embarrassment to

her manner towards he.









                             CHAPTER XXIII



                                QUARREL





it be two day before Christmas that this great event usually take

place,—an event speak of by Mrs. Johnson as though it have be a

solemn feast, with an appoint date in the Church’s year; and it

form almost the most trying of the many try occasion which

chequer her earthly career. this season, thank to the valuable

assistance of Miss Askam in the dreary business know as

‘decorate,’ Mrs. Johnson feel her difficulty much lightened, and

look forward to the evening’s entertainment with a kind of

‘rest-and-be-thankful’ feeling, rare indeed in her experience.



Eleanor feel less comfort than Mrs. Johnson, in the anticipation of

the evening. she hardly know how it be, or with whom the invitation

have originate, whether with Otho, herself, or Magdalen’s self—but

an invitation have certainly be give to the latter to dine at

Thorsgarth, and go with the party from there to the entertainment.

‘the party’ mean all of them!—Eleanor, Magdalen, Gilbert, and Otho.

Eleanor have be unaffectedly astonish when Otho have say he be

go. she have promise Mrs. Johnson to be there herself, and do

not intend to fail she; but she have expect to go alone, call for

the doctor’s sister, Mrs. Parker, on the way, and under the decent,

if not highly distinguished chaperonage of that lady, sit through

the concert, and derive from it what enjoyment she might. she have

resolve to know nothing about Gilbert and his arrangement, and to

ignore Magdalen, except by a bow, and a few word. this new scheme

have completely change the aspect of thing. she have have to send a

note to Mrs. Parker explain that, so far from be alone, she

would have a party with she, and must remain with they. and then

there be the prospect of the concert itself, and of the company of

her brother and Gilbert Langstroth, and of Magdalen Wynter, who

would, of course, join they as soon as her part in the performance

should be over. it be a thoroughly painful prospect to she; not the

less so, in that there be absolutely no excuse for she shirk the

ceremony.



she return to Thorsgarth from the vicarage, in the afternoon, and

presently go upstairs to dress for the evening’s entertainment.

the most competent authority have assure she that at these

concert it be the custom for the lady of the vicinity to go in

full dress, or, at any rate, in unmistakable evening dress, in order

to do honour to their town and townspeople, and to show that they

do not labour under the idea that ‘anything would do’ for a

Bradstane concert. follow out this tradition, Miss Askam have

cause she maid to array she in a quaint-look but handsome gown

of velvet and brocade. she know that dress of this kind, more

splendid than airy, suit she, and wear they by preference to any

other.



she stand now before the toilet glass, while her Abigail put the

finishing touch to the dress, right glad to see some of the finery

wear once again. it appear to she that her mistress’s beauty, of

which she be proud, and her accomplishment, of which she have hear

people speak, be utterly and entirely throw away in a place like

this. Eleanor stand a little undecided what ornament to wear, when

her maid be summon away, and presently return, bear in she

hand a bouquet, with a card dangle from it.



‘if you please, ma’am, I be to give you this, with Mr. Langstroth’s

compliment.’



‘Mr. Langstroth!’ suddenly exclaim Eleanor, a quick change come

over her face; and then, take the flower, she lift the card and

read: ‘with Mr. Gilbert Langstroth’s compliment;’ and just below,

‘Miss Askam wish for some double violet for this evening, which

G. L. hope do not arrive too late to be of use.’



Eleanor have hold her breath as she peruse these word; she now

breathe again, quickly, and say, as she give his flower again

into her maid’s hand—



‘put they down, and just twist these pearl through my hair. I shall

wear they, I think.’



‘yes, ma’am,’ say Louisa, and add as she survey the

flowers—‘They’re really lovely, ma’am. could Mr. Langstroth have get

they in England?’

                              CHAPTER XXIV



                             OTHO’S revenge

                              CHAPTER XXV



                            in the ANTE-ROOM

for more than five year; and within the last year, she have begin to

confess that her pain have be in vain—that he have never intend

to proceed to anything more than friendship, and be not likely now

to change his mind. and then her own deed have avenge themselves

upon she, for in confess this, she have suffer torture, and in

try to act upon it, and to shake off her intimacy with he, she

have find that she could not. he have make himself master of what

heart she possess. her resistance this evening to a demand of his

have cost she a pang, and this conduct of his, in consequence,

bewilder she. she be throw off her guard; she feel that she be

grope in a fog, and she know not how to battle with he. she

repent she now, in her soul, of having thwart he just to-night,

since this be the way in which he choose to revenge himself. she

could see nothing except to maintain an unruffled personal dignity,

which she know come easily to she, and, if necessary, to retire

altogether from the arena. she be calculate altogether without

her host in the matter, as Otho soon prove to she.



she take the chair he offer she, and sit down; and then Otho,

take no far present notice of she, turn to Ada, and, under

sense, take fire at his attention, and construe they into proof

of the most flattering regard.



Magdalen sit quite passive under this behaviour until the choir have

go to the concert-room, to sing their glee about ‘the oak and the

deliberately—



‘Ada, I want to speak to you.’



‘oh, never mind she!’ say Otho, carelessly, almost contemptuously.

‘a sermon should not come before a song, especially such a song as

Miss Dixon be go to give we.’



but old habit be yet strong in Ada. never before have Miss Wynter

address she without receive instant and profound attention, and

she receive it now. Ada give it almost instinctively.



‘what be it, Miss Wynter?’ she ask.



‘only this, that I don’t know what Mr. Askam mean by behave as he

certain,—that Mr. Roger Camm will be here directly, and I would

advise you to moderate your transport, and behave a little more

like a reasonable being before he come.’



‘Roger Camm, indeed!’ exclaim Ada, nettle. ‘he’s not my master

yet, nor ever will be, Miss Wynter. he may come twenty time for

aught I care.’



never before have she address Magdalen in such a tone. it would

appear that the latter be in earnest in her remonstrance, for she

‘right!’ exclaim Otho, with a laugh. ‘I never ask about right. I

do what I have a fancy for.’



‘pray what harm can Roger say of I?’ say Ada pettishly.



‘I would rather ask, what good he can say of you, if you let he see

you make yourself ridiculous in this fashion. in any case, you

belong to he, and——’



‘not yet!’ exclaim both Otho and Ada in one voice. Magdalen look

at they both, and show what be with she a rare sign, betoken

‘Otho,’ she say, slowly and deliberately, and with a glitter in her

he have not be too angrily determined to do his own way to heed

‘to the last day of your life,’ she repeat. ‘but I have speak,

and I leave it between you. I wash my hand of you both.’



she get up, and go to another chair at the extreme end of the

be laugh in a nervously pleased manner at his word, the

suddenly open door let in a loud burst of music from the front.

it be close again.



‘oh, I’m not so late, after all,’ begin Roger Camm’s voice, and then

he come to a dead stop, look from one to the other, speechless.

Otho, who be lean over the back of Ada’s chair, raise his head

as Roger enter, and look at he with the disagreeable smile

which show his white tooth and his frown brow.



‘good evening, Camm,’ he say, carelessly, and in so condescend a

tone that Magdalen look up.



Roger advance a step.



‘what do this mean?’ he ask, and his hand have clench

themselves, and his face have grow pale.



‘what do what mean?’ ask Ada, laugh flippantly, to conceal

‘what be you do here?’ ask Roger, stand directly in front

‘what I please,’ reply Otho, insolently, and not raise himself

‘that be an odd answer to give I,’ observe Roger, incisively,

‘Roger!’ exclaim Ada, flush fiercely, and speak in a choke

voice.



‘ha, ha!’ laugh Otho.



at this juncture Magdalen again rise, and come forward once more.

she be pale even to her lip, and she walk up to Roger Camm, lay

‘Mr. Camm, you must listen to I. I believe I be the cause of this

he wish I to promise he, early in the evening, when I be at

seem pitiful to I. if I have know, nothing would have induce I

to come here. I can only say he be beside himself, and——’



‘that will scarcely do,’ say Roger, turn away. ‘he be not

acknowledge as a lunatic yet, nor shut up, whatever he ought to be,

and I will thank he——’



here the door again open, and all the performer come into the

room. Magdalen say imploringly to Roger—



‘please go and play the prelude. I will _make_ he behave himself.’



there be no time to be lose, and Roger, after hesitate a second

or two, follow her direction. Magdalen turn to Otho. for once

she find he deaf and senseless to her word. she bid he go to

the concert-room. he flatly refuse to do so, with a bow and a

in low tone, the pantomime being eagerly watch by those who have

come in, and who could see the gesture of the speaker and their

face, without hear their word.



the man’s vindictive determination prevail. if he be mad there

be method in his madness. he be prepared to throw all appearance

to the wind, and to say or do whatever come uppermost. Magdalen be

not; and she have little time in which to decide. Otho offer his

arm to Ada, and they go on to the stage in the order before speak

of.



when they all return to the ante-room, Ada be more uneasy and

less triumphant than she have be; and greatly embarrassed too, by

Otho’s mark attention in the face of the other performer, who,

so far from be awestruck at the distinction confer upon she,

seem to be titter amongst themselves at the absurdity of the

Roger walk up to Ada, and ask she gravely and quietly if she have

to sing again.



‘no, Roger,’ say she, in a subdued voice.



‘then I think you have well let I take you home,’ he say gently,

and offer she his arm. Ada take it instantly.



‘oh, nonsense!’ begin Otho. ‘we cann’t do with that. she——’



‘be good enough to stand out of the way,’ say Roger, fix his

eye upon he with a steady look that bode anything but peace

between they in the future. ‘I will take Miss Dixon home now. there

have be foolery enough to-night. I will settle with you to-morrow.’



this promise be give heartily enough, if in a low voice. Otho,

with a sneer laugh, let he pass, and then turn to Magdalen.



‘I suppose you be not too overcome to go into the other room,’ he

say. ‘shall I take you there?’



‘I shall go when I be ready,’ reply Magdalen, coldly. ‘you be at

liberty to go as soon as ever you please.’



‘not I!’ say he, throw himself into a chair near to she. ‘I’ve

work hard enough to get your society. I’m not go to quit it the

instant I have secure it.’



here the choir be again summon to the front, and they be leave

alone. Otho have speak of having work hard to obtain Magdalen’s

company, but he sit in silence till towards the end of the chorus,

when, as it be the last thing in the first part of the concert,

Magdalen rise, and begin to gather up her shawl.



‘now I shall go,’ she remark.



‘all right!’ say he. ‘but listen to I, Magdalen; you must let I

see you home, and I’ll tell you the meaning of this.’



‘as if I require to know the meaning of it!’ she say, bitterly.

‘it be pure malice and viciousness on your part, Otho. meaning,

indeed!’



‘you know nothing in the world about it.’



‘I cannot talk about it now. I be not go to enter into an

argument with you. you have make I feel ill already.’



‘then settle matter by promise that I shall go home with you; or

I vow you shall hear I in this very room. I intend to have it out

with you to-night, do you hear?’



‘very well—as we go home,’ say Magdalen, very coldly.



and, as the door open to admit the return performer, and the

interval have begin, they take their way to the concert-room, and

join their party.









                              CHAPTER XXVI



                           her HEART’S DESIRE





when the Thorsgarth carriage have drive away, and the Balder Hall

one come up, Otho hand Magdalen in, follow she, shut the

window, and turn to she. after the bright light around the

concert-room door, they seem suddenly to plunge into utter and

outer darkness, and Magdalen be glad of it, for she would not

have have Otho see her face now for a great deal of money,—perhaps

not even if his see it would have secure to she the object for

which she have toil so long and so unsuccessfully—the position of

his wife. she do not know what he be go to say to she, but

she believe she could guess. she believe that the new line she

have lately take towards he—to-night, and on one or two other

occasion recently—have so anger his imperious and exact

at an end, unless she would submit to take a low position with

herself about Otho and his ‘intentions’—she know that she have no

‘dishonourable’ proposal to fear from he. she have maintain

always a footing distinctly forbid such possibility; but she

dread and fear that he have shake off what influence she have

have over he—that he find he could exist without the counsel and

have often be of service to he. she believe he be angry that

she have dare to thwart he in a whim which he consider to be

harmless, and a kind of amusing joke, and that after make the

bizarre and humiliating exhibition of himself, she, and Ada, which

he have accomplish this evening, he be now go to let she

scarcely what might have be expect. neither anger, contempt,

nor indignation, but grief, sorrow, soreness; a yearning

see he; an almost passionate speculation as to whether she could

not concede something—keep the man at her side, somehow.



thus she be glad when the darkness hide her face. he could not see

‘pray be quick. I do not know why I have grant you this favour at

all. it be far beyond your desert.’



‘as for that, there may be two opinion. if you’d hear I out

to-night, instead of pounce upon I as you do——’



‘do not allude to that. it be over, and I have not repent my

refusal to you. it be quite obvious what people think when you

appear with that girl on your arm. not for world would I have put

myself into such a position.’



‘position—position! you spit out that word just as woman do when

they want to make out that their dear friend be do something

they be true or not, but I know you have often talk a lot about

friendship, and the duty of stick to your friend when you have

get one. I wonder whether you dare show every one that you be

my friend to-night.’



‘Absurd! to show myself your friend in that fashion mean one of two

thing—either that I be engaged to be married to you, or else that I

show myself a bold, vulgar woman, whom any other man might well be

be be loud and fast, and all to no purpose. such a proceeding

could serve no possible end.’

she, who have so long contrive to have the rein in her own hand,

they’ll tell you that I drink, and I dice, and I bet. so I do, and

like they all; and, of course, they’ll tell you I’m no fitting

make you happy, for I know I should make you miserable, but whatever

I seem—I cann’t always answer for myself—whatever I seem, I’d

love you to the end of my life, ten time well than I do now. dare

Silence. the carriage roll softly on over the snowy road. Otho have

seize hold of her two hand. his face she could not see, but she

he do not urge she when she do not answer; his passion seem to

‘Otho!’—her face almost touch his as she spoke—‘I know what you

be. I have be try to tear you out of my heart. I do not want

you there. I cannot kill the love I have for you. I dare do anything

for you.’



as she cease to speak, their lip meet in a clinging kiss—a kiss

which bind their two fate together from henceforth, for evermore,

and which make her heart beat chokingly with terror and passion, but

which be utterly devoid of the joy and spring rapture it might

and human and gentle, and a fitting husband for a civilised lady,

but that she accept his evil way along with himself, and endure

they as well she might.



they sit silent for a while after this, till at last he say—



‘Magdalen!’



‘well?’



‘you be not a good young woman, you know. you have not always stick

you jilt Michael Langstroth,—did not keep your promise to he,

I do jilt he. he be not make for I, nor I for he. I daresay he

‘he take his dismissal,’ pursue Otho, with a sneer, ‘and never

raise his hand. but let I advise you not to try that game with I,

‘I will,’ say Otho, with generous promptitude.



‘you cann’t. here we be, and it be snow, actually,’ she add, as

‘I’ll walk, of course,’ say Otho, jump out, and hold out his

expostulate with him—‘walk over three mile in this drive snow—and

brougham, and you could put it up at your place till to-morrow.’

flake of snow to I, now? haven’t I be in a fever all night? I

tell you, I want to work it off, so let I alone.’

‘stop a minute!’ say Otho. ‘Heugh! what a wind!’ as a screaming

blast from the north-west whistle past the vestibule.



‘Otho, you must not walk home——’



‘be quiet, I tell you; let I alone! if I’ve a fancy, I’ll sleep in

‘and that when the time come—we shall both know when it do—you’ll

‘and that whatever happen, you be mine—you don’t cut yourself

‘there be no need for I to swear that, for I could not, if I

would.’

Magdalen put she two hand on his shoulder, and say—



‘I have swear a good many thing to you; I want you to swear nothing

indirectly, from this time forward, you do to your _wife_, for you

be mine now, as much as I be yours. good night!’

‘you have be very good to I, and very patient with I, Magdalen.

you’ll get your reward, I hope.’



then he turn on his heel, ram his cap on to his head, and

he have choose to walk—persist in walk, perhaps with some idea

we’ve be drive together, if ever two lose soul be, and——’



here he be oblige to give his undivided attention to keep the

it be nearly two when at last Gilbert let he in at the side door

to stand, cover all over with snow, and shade his blinded eye

‘good heaven, man! where have you be; and what have you be

do? I be just think of rouse the house, and send relay

of man after you, with lantern.’



‘I’ve be do my courting,’ say Otho, pull off his overcoat,

and shake himself; ‘and since win the lady, I’ve have to do

battle with the storm. have you get a good fire in there, and

something to drink? it’s not weather for a dog to be out in.’



‘which lady have you be honour with your proposal?’ inquire

Gilbert drily.



‘which? why, there be only one, and that’s Magdalen.’



‘oh! it be a pity you do not manage to let other people understand

that as clearly as you seem to do yourself.’



‘come, don’t be crusty, Gilbert,’ say he, suddenly, and without his

usual sullenness. ‘you know I have be wonder for a long time if

I should do this; and now that it’s do, by Jove, you don’t seem to

think it make any difference to a fellow! I think you would shake

hand, and wish I joy at any rate.’



Gilbert be a little time silent before he answer. then he say—



‘I can do that, if you like, and do it honestly. I’ve no objection

to shake hand with you, and I would rather you meet with joy than

sorrow; but’—with a sudden change of tone—‘why do you spoil

everything by make that hideous exhibition of yourself to-night?

why could you not tame Magdalen, if she want taming, without

embroil yourself with half a dozen other people? it be too

stupid!’



it be not by reproach like these that Gilbert have get and

maintain his power over Otho, but something to-night seem to

drive the word out of he, whether he wish to utter they or not.

Otho do not seem inclined to quarrel.



‘what do it matter?’ he say, tolerantly. ‘let I alone. it will

all blow over.’



‘it will not blow over!’ say Gilbert, almost passionately. ‘do you

suppose that Roger Camm will put up with such treatment? he be

perfectly frantic, and you will have to reckon with he yet——’



‘I’m quite ready,’ say Otho, scowl suddenly. ‘he have well mind

what he be about, in call I to account, that’s all.’



‘not only that, but you make yourself ridiculous; and, above all,

Otho, you put a public insult upon your sister by behave as you

do in her presence, with a little vulgar fool like that Dixon girl.

it be——’



‘my sister choose to come poke her nose into my house, and mix

herself with my affair,’ say Otho, sullenly. ‘she may take the

consequence. let she go home again to her genteel friend, if she

object to what go on here.’



‘bah!’ say Gilbert, with indignation. ‘have you no sense of

decency?’



‘you be cross, Gilbert; and it’s very late. I’m go to bed, and I

advise you to do the same. the lecture to-morrow, when you’ve have

time to think thing over. good-night, old fellow.’



he take a candle, nod to Gilbert, and leave the room. his friend

slowly follow he, look altogether more limp and less

self-assure than he have do six hour early.









                             CHAPTER XXVII



                             RECRIMINATION





Roger Camm, lead Ada away, go out into the passage, and stand

at the door of the cloak-room, while she put on her shawl and hat,

and then come out to he. her face be still flushed, and more

sullen than downcast. she do not look at he, as she say, ‘I be

ready.’



he give she his arm, and they go out, to walk the few hundred

yard between the schoolroom and Ada’s home. the cold night air blow

upon their heated face, for Roger, though he look so pale, be in

a fever, and Ada’s heart be hot with anger and disappointment.

nothing be say till they arrive at the side door which lead to the

house part of Mr. Dixon’s premise. then Ada observe—



‘well, I suppose after all this, you willn’t want to come in.’



‘oh, Ada!’ exclaim the young man in a voice of reproach, ‘that be

cruel. I want to speak to you, of course. I would not go away and

leave you alone—the idea!’



‘oh, come in, pray! now that you have spoil my whole pleasure, and

make I a laugh-stock; take I away, like a baby in disgrace,’

say Ada, in a voice that tremble violently.



they go into the house. the astonished servant come out of the

kitchen, on hear the unexpected noise, but retire when she see

who the intruder be. Mr. and Mrs. Dixon be both at the concert.

Ada lead the way upstairs, to the family sitting-room, and turn up

the gas, and give Roger the prosaic order to poke the fire.



he do so, and then turn to she. for a little time he stand with

his back against the mantelpiece, and his hand clasp behind he.

she draw out her handkerchief and hold it before her eye, to

conceal the tear which rage and temper prevent from flow;

while she tap the floor with her foot. it be a cruelly painful

how, how be he to reproach she? for reproach she he must. he have

a man whose character and station make it impossible for such

she to be so mislead. all that be necessary be for he to tell she

what a mistake she have make. but how to begin—what word be soft

enough, gentle enough? while he be thus inwardly debate, Ada

‘have you bring I here to play at a quaker’s meeting?’ she ask,

angrily. ‘I think you say you have something to say; and I wish

you’d be quick with it, as I’m sure it’s nothing agreeable.’



‘Ada, be that my fault?’ he ask, turn to she with a wistful

look.



‘of course it be your fault,’ be the indignant reply. ‘why must you

fly into a passion about nothing at all, and speak to I as if I’d

be do something bad than any one else ever do, and insult

Wynter sit there? be I not to be allow to speak to a

gentleman?’



‘Ada!’ he exclaim, all the gentle phrase scatter to the wind

at the picture which her word conjure into his mind, and speak

solemnly, and even sternly,—‘for heaven’s sake be silent, or you

will drive I to speak to you in a way that I shall repent.

Gentleman! no gentleman would behave as that blackguard behave

to-night. when I go in and see he lean over your chair, I

wonder I do not rush at he and knock he down, without a word. let

I tell you that no girl’s character would benefit by its being

know that Otho Askam be on friendly term with she. he be a

thorough-pace cad, without honour, or honesty, or principle.

child, child! how could you let he lead you on, in the face——’



‘you’re never jealous!’ cry Ada, her anger turn into something

like a smile.



‘jealous!’ echo Roger, with unspeakable contempt in his tone.

‘when I have to be jealous of he, it will be all up between you and

I. I boil with rage at the pollution you suffer from his

familiarity. Ada—you do love I, my darling, don’t you?’



‘why, yes, of course,’ say Ada, slowly.



‘then you must promise I to have no more to do with he. see to

what you have drive I already. I shall have to have a reckoning

with he to-morrow. I shall tell he what I think of he, and

promise he a horsewhipping, if ever he venture upon such

impertinence again——’



‘Roger!’ the presumption, the audacity of his word, cause Ada to

turn pale.



‘and of course,’ Roger go on, calmly, ‘I shall have to shake the

dust of this place from my foot. I have no fear of not be able to

get another situation; but it may be some little time first; it may

be a long way from here when I do get it; it cannot be here, of

course; there be no other place here. and that will separate we. you

do not know or foresee all this; you could not, but it be so, you

see. and I cannot speak quite calmly about it.’



Ada be silent. Roger think she be thunderstruck on be show

the consequence of what have doubtless seem to she a few

flattering attention.



‘I don’t see the good of make all that fuss about it,’ she say at

last. ‘there be no wrong in Mr. Askam’s say a few word to I;

but people will say there be, if you go on in that way.’ (a feminine

view of the case which have not before occur to Roger.) ‘I have

always be a friend of Miss Wynter’s——’



‘yes, indeed; and precious little good it have do you,’ be Roger’s

ill-advise retort.



‘and Miss Askam sit talk with I for nearly an hour the other

with the gentry about here, and there’s proof positive that it ben’t

only the gentleman who notice I——’



‘notice you—notice you!’ he say, sting intensely by her word. ‘who

want you to be notice? you have no need of any one’s notice. no

honest girl have——’



‘honest girl! well, I declare!’



‘your line be not cast among such people. they only amuse

themselves with you, whatever you may think. if you keep they at a

distance they respect you. as for Miss Wynter, I have always

dislike she——’



‘yes, you have; and without a scrap of reason. you think she’s bad

than poison, and say all sort of thing about her,—false, I’ve

hear you call she. and there be she before you come in, talk to

‘oh dear!’ exclaim Ada, mentally anathematise her maladroit

only mean to show that she be not the false woman you call she.’



‘I judge she by her action. as for Miss Askam, she be different.

Dr. Rowntree know she, and he say so; and Michael Langstroth——’



‘oh, I know he think so. and of course he’s always right!’ retort

‘not a word; you be quite right,’ say Roger, constrainedly. ‘I

should have know there be some reason for what he do. but that’s

ever sing. and as for Miss Askam, of course he say she be an angel,

and an angel that’s pretty thick with he, by all account.’

indignant at her tone.



‘Goodness! I never say it be against they. I see no harm in it;

but then I’m not so strait-laced as some people. they’ve be see

ride together, not so long ago, and he call there when Mr. Askam

be away.’



‘their meeting be a pure accident.’



‘oh, of course! such thing be always accident,’ say Ada, with a

‘do you mean that you doubt I, Ada?’ he ask, very seriously.



‘doubt _you_?—not at all. I suppose Dr. Langstroth say it be an

and you do what he tell you, and believe what he say.’



this shaft fall quite ineffectually.



‘it do not matter who circulate the report,’ say Roger. ‘every

‘if he speak to I, I suppose I’m to shut my mouth and not say a

‘that’s my own dearest Ada!’ exclaim Roger, too much pleased with

but Ada be by no mean to be so easily pacify. she decline the

and she be thoroughly divide in their opinion upon the

occurrence of the evening.



but she be so young, so innocent, he say to himself. what could

she possibly know of the real character of a man like Otho Askam,

or of the sinister and compromise nature of any attention from

come right. when she be married to he, and he could speak

plainly to she, soul to soul, there would be no more of these

misunderstanding, these cloud and dispute. she would be

innocent still, but not ignorant any more.



and, perhaps, so far as he be himself concern, it be well for

his soul’s health to be free from all connection with a man like

Askam.



thus he reflect, as he take his homeward way, and on arrive at

the Red Gables, find the room dark, and Michael still absent.



about half-past ten he arrive, and find Roger alone, in an

easy-chair by the fireside.



‘Halloa! back again!’ exclaim Michael.



‘ay!’



‘you take she away altogether, do you?’



‘yes.’



‘quite right, too. brute like he want show that they cann’t ride

rough-shod over every one and everything.’



‘Michael, do you think he be a little cracked?’



‘not a particle, unless be bear bad, and a bully, be be

crack. it be a somewhat debatable question, you know, now. we be

so very liberal and tolerant in these day. it seem to be the

theory that if by chance you behave decently, you ought at once to

have a statue put up to you; whereas, if you conduct yourself like a

savage, or a blackleg, or as if you have be bring up amongst

professional thief, and the low riff-raff, the thing be, that

you be not quite all there, poor fellow!—that’s all, and ought not

publicly. that’s about the tune of it.’



‘bully be usually coward,’ observe Roger, reflectively.



‘and so be he. wait till the time come when the shoe begin to

the remark of creditor; then you’ll see where his brag end.’



‘nothing would give I great pleasure than to look on at such a

say good-bye to he, and leave he to his own resource. I’m

not a “gentleman bear,” like he be——’



‘now come, Roger. you’ll be say next that all “gentleman bear”

be like Otho Askam, and all “work man,” as you be pleased to

call yourself, be just like you—on the same level, and with the

same feeling. keep within bound.’



‘not a gentleman by birth, like he be,’ Roger go on softly; ‘but I

be a human being, with susceptibility, and with coarse desire and

betrothed, which I consider to have be want in respect. the

latter inspire I to tell he he be a cad, threaten he with a

horsewhipping, and cry quit with he. don’t say anything against

it, because I be go to do it, and it’s no use your worry about

‘I—worry. Nay, you may choke he by knock his own impudence down

his throat, if you like; I have nothing against it. I be sorry for

his sister, I must say. do you see she to-night?’



‘yes,’ say Roger, tranquilly. ‘so do you. she look superb.’



‘and miserable, poor thing! who would guess they to be brother and

sister?’



‘who, indeed?’



‘I cannot imagine that she can be very happy in that house.’



‘I cann’t imagine that any decent person would.’



then Roger light a pipe, and smoke it, before go to bed.

Michael pull out a book, and say he have some reading to do. how

soon the one sleep, how much the other read—these thing have not

be ascertain.









                             CHAPTER XXVIII



                              AT the mill





when Michael come down on the follow morning, he find Roger

gaze out of the window, at the snowy prospect, and drum his

finger on the pane.



‘a jolly day for you to turn out, Michael. have you to go far

afield?’



‘not very; but I have a good deal to do. Balder Hall be the farth

place I have to go to. I must see Miss Strangforth.’



‘ah, well, it be not a very good road.’



he turn to the breakfast-table, and they have both make some

‘Roger, you say you be go to have it out with Otho Askam

‘so I be.’



‘do you mean to give it he hot?’



‘I mean to tell he that I have do with he, and to promise he a

‘do you know, I don’t think it be the good thing you could do.’



‘well make he a speech, thank he for his politeness and

‘oh, nonsense! you know that be not what I mean.’



the thought in Michael’s mind, of which he could not, of course,

look at it from his point of view, and feel a sorrowful surprise

at Roger’s denseness.



‘what I mean be this,’ he go on. ‘Otho Askam be not exactly like

other man; he’s a great blackguard than most. you might as well

creature that he be. and if you quit he at a moment’s notice,

people will be quite ready to say that there be more in it than meet

the eye. I think that, for her sake, you should be careful.’



Roger move uneasily in his chair, and a deep flush of anger be on

his face.



‘curse he!’ he exclaim, at length, with emphasis. ‘it would be a

good deed to choke he!’



‘oh yes! but we have to put up with human vermin where we should

scotch they if they be snake.’ Michael speak more lightly, for he

see that his word have take the effect he wish they to have,

without his having be force to say what he think; that though

conduct herself like a fool, and a vulgar one, have put no

impediment in the way of his so behave.



‘you know, he can sue you for breach of contract if you

inconvenience he, and that would be confoundedly expensive, and

the reason why you leave he at a moment’s notice.’

‘and make every one think that he could say more about you than he

have do, if he choose to. no; you have to deal with man as they be,

you know, and not as they should be; and you cannot treat a

‘and Ada!’ say Roger, in a constrained voice. ‘while I be

there be a pause, during which Roger look dark and angry, but at

‘yes, yes; you be perfectly right. but oh, Lord!’ he add, almost

could he have see clearly into his friend’s mind, he might have

‘there’s a high thing though, than a fighting animal,’ pursue

Roger make no answer to this oracular utterance, and they presently

separate and go their several way.



Roger, in the office, ponder upon Michael’s word, and know they

gnaw at his heart, and turn to his work.



the morning, which despite the snow, have break bright, clear, and

sharp, cloud over, till everything look very sad and gray;—the

street where the tramping work-people have pound the snow into a

dirty slush; the mill-yard itself; the river flow sullenly past,

deep and flood.



none of they all could be grayer than the spirit of Roger Camm. he

begin to wonder how it be that he have so little luck, and try

hard to see his way, even for a yard before he, but not with much

success. by degree, to his trenchant mood succeed one of

Otho would not come down; so far from desire to give he a

this be not to be. about half-past eleven he see two horseman enter

‘what business?’ he ask.



‘there be the letter,’ reply Camm, as he push they across to

Otho take they and stand near the fire. Gilbert turn to Roger.



‘I have be talk to Mr. Askam,’ he say; ‘and I find that he have

not insure that new machinery that come the other day. I think it

ought to be do as soon as possible.’



Otho look up.



‘what’s that? oh, insurance! you be at it again.’



‘I should imagine that Camm would agree with I,’ say Gilbert.



‘why, of course,’ reply Roger. ‘it be a thing that ought to be

do at once, and I have mention it several time.’



‘do you hear, Otho? now do be reasonable, and get Camm to write

about it at once, and have it settle now.’



‘not I!’ say Otho, lay down the letter. ‘we’ve spend far too

much money already in insurance. insurance be all bosh. the mill

be insure; and where’s the use of a thing, or the amusement, if

you go and arrange against all accident beforehand?’



at this novel view of the merit and use of insurance, Gilbert give

‘oh, you must not gamble with everything; and even if you do, it’s

wise to calculate your chance a bit, unless you be clean mad.’

‘those,’ reply Roger, point to some envelope that lie on the

this extreme brevity, which for the life of he Roger could not have

‘come, Otho, haven’t you nearly do? it be time we be move,’ he

Roger do; his read the letter be a form to be go through,

‘well,’ say Gilbert, once again, ‘willn’t you think about the

‘no,’ retort Otho, impatiently. ‘I’ve no money to spare for

‘turn economical with advance year,’ observe Gilbert, with

‘how you preach!’ almost snarl Otho. ‘tell you I don’t mean to

‘I should like to speak to you before you go,’ observe Roger,

Otho, hear this, turn sharp upon he, grasp his whip in his

‘what be it?’ ask Otho, his hand on the door-handle.



‘merely that I be think of leave Bradstane. to-day be the

‘what the devil be the meaning of all this?’ demand Otho, loose

the door-handle, but hold the whip fast, and turn upon Roger

‘how do I ever bear with he for this length of time, the brute!’



but he answer civilly and tranquilly—



‘that be scarcely the way in which to speak to I. I say that I wish

to leave your employment this day three month. ben’t that simple

‘I’ll be hang if it be!’ say Otho, savagely. ‘it’s usual to give

‘I would advise you not to ask for it,’ be Roger’s answer, his face

‘what! may a man not ask his servant’s reason for leave he?’

‘don’t be a fool, Otho!’ here observe Gilbert. ‘Roger Camm have as

‘I say,’ observe Otho, suddenly change his angry demeanour into

I can tell you——’



‘if you mention her name, I’ll give you the hiding you deserve!’

thunder Roger, spring up, and walk very close up to Otho,

whose laugh now change to a look of furious anger.



‘you be threaten I!’ he demand, in a voice of suppress

rage.



‘I shall not confine myself to threat very long,’ be the

breathless reply.



Otho’s eye look dangerous still, but he seem also amuse, in a

curious manner.



‘then it be about the little girl that you have cut up rough. Lord

bless you, she ben’t worth think about twice!’ he say, burst

‘you blackguard!’ say Camm, between his clench tooth. ‘I’ll——’



his hand be raise, and there be fury in his eye. the word

collar the result might have be unpleasant, but he feel Gilbert’s

‘don’t you see he be just lead you on? you be not a

prize-fighter, if he be. let he go!’



Roger’s hand drop. Otho be watch he with a look of hatred in

his face which be far strong than the sneer which his lip try

‘I say I would go in three month,’ say Roger, constrainedly,

clench his hand down, to keep himself under control; ‘but you

have make that impossible. you can look out for yourself from this

moment. I will not darken your door again, if I can help it.’



with which, pick up his hat, he push Otho unceremoniously to

one side, and walk out, leave the other to make the good of the

his heart be sick as he walk away. such a scene his very soul

abhor. all the tingling desire to chastise Otho seem to

evaporate as he leave his presence. he feel again nothing but

loathing, aversion, and a wish to keep as clear of he as possible.

but reptile can sting, and Otho have sting. as Roger pass through

‘she’s not worth think twice about. which be the bad, eh?—she

or i?’



his heart, wring with shame and anguish, call upon her name. no.

he must not go in now. he must wait until hour have pass, and

reflection have come to his aid.



he go on to the Red Gables, and find Michael just in from his

first round. to he Roger relate what have happen, and what he have

do.



‘I could not help it,’ he say. ‘I begin civilly enough, and

prudently enough; but when that cur give tongue I lose my head. he

have never happen to do it before, about anything in which I have

any concern; but as soon as he begin, it be all up with I. I leave

he and your excellent brother to settle it as they well could; I

walk off.’



‘well, I cannot blame you,’ say Michael, when he have hear he out.

should’ have do the same, or more. but it be an odious business.’



‘it be a vile business,’ reply Roger, gloomily; ‘and until after

Christmas, I shall be at a loose end, for it be useless try to

see after anything before then.’









                              CHAPTER XXIX



                       a false step in good faith





the day after that unfortunate fracas at the mill be Christmas

Day. it will easily be understand that to Roger it do not this year

form the most cheerful occasion imaginable. he have see Ada on the

evening of the twenty-fourth, and some kind of a reconciliation have

then be patch up between they, but one which set Roger thinking,

and make he feel that many difference of opinion might be less

disastrous than such a making up of a quarrel. it have not be

spontaneous; it have be largely due to the intervention of Mr.

Dixon, who be very indignant with his daughter for what he call

‘make such an exhibition of herself.’ he condemn Otho Askam in

no measured term, but his blame of Ada and her ‘want of sense’ be

almost as strong. he want to know where she mean to draw the line

in her folly. he add that she be do her character no good by

such ‘carrying on,’ and utter a dark hint as to the implacable

nature of his wrath should she ever in the future disgrace, or as he

express it, ‘lower herself’ in any way whatsoever.



during the paternal admonition Mrs. Dixon maintain an ominous

silence. as have be before say, she do not favour Roger’s

pretension, and have always look to her daughter to marry

well;—not what Mr. Dixon consider to be well, but what she, his

consort, understand by the term. on return from the concert, and

find that Ada have go to her room, her mother have repair

thither, and have extract from the girl an account of every word

utter throughout the evening, by herself, Otho, Miss Wynter, and

Roger. she have not say much, save some strong expression

condemnatory of Roger’s behaviour, which she characterise as

‘tyrannical,’ ‘impudent,’ ‘masterful,’ and ‘odious,’ and express

indignation that her daughter should be force to do the bidding of

such a man. but at the recital of Otho’s attention there be an

expression in her face which Ada do not interpret as one of

displeasure.



by Mr. Dixon’s order the young woman have receive she betroth

with outward friendliness, though she decline with quiet,

persistent obstinacy, to say she be sorry for what have happen.

reconciliation make to order be apt to carry about they a very

strong flavour of their artificial nature and origin, and this

particular reconciliation bear the stamp of unreality very plainly

to be read.



on go in on Christmas Eve, Roger be, for once, not at all sorry

to find that the Dixons have friend with they. Mr. Dixon receive

he have promise Mr. Johnson to take all the organist’s duty on the

that he would not be able to call and see she before service. she

agitate his mind. how be he to treat she? what be he to do with

though in his tenderness he be ready to condone that, and would

have reality in it, yet the sense of duty and of the fitness of

the man be to beg forgiveness for all misunderstanding, whether

cause by himself or not, be simple madness. yet how he be to

most glimmering consciousness seem to have be afford she.



with this trouble in his mind he go to church on Christmas

that alone be a help to counsel. it be not the first time that

music have come to he with healing on her wing; most likely it

would not be the last.



seat up in the organ-loft, and look into the mirror in front of

pretty still in the framework of a smart and become new bonnet.

it be a fresh, sweet face, see thus in repose, and at a distance,

and his heart yearn towards its owner, and he try to put out of

whether the music inspire he, or the sight of Ada, he know not,

but there flash suddenly into his mind the recollection that he

the good thing that could possibly happen to they both. separation

for a season would, he argue, teach they both to look upon thing

would learn to be less indignant at what have happen between they.



as he come to this conclusion, which he hug as a conviction,

because it present to he a way out of his difficulty with regard

again Roger’s hat come off, and he could not find it in his heart to

from Dr. Rowntree, and from my friend, Mrs. Johnson.’

‘she would repair the wrong, if she could. poor thing! she might as

well try to sweep back the ocean with a besom.’



then Gilbert say to he, ‘I have no opportunity of speak to you

well that we should have a little talk.’



‘thank you. I shall be punctual,’ say Gilbert, with the air of a

they part, and Roger take his way to Mr. Dixon’s, where he have

on the follow day, at noon, punctual to his appointment, Roger

‘he know I wouldn’t set my foot inside Thorsgarth, and he know,

too, that he need never again darken the door of his brother; so we

be an odious state of thing, and I shall be glad to be out of it.’



he have not long to wait. Gilbert arrive directly afterwards, and

‘I be much oblige to you for come,’ begin Gilbert. ‘it give I

‘of course that will be necessary, but it be not what I come about

‘I don’t know about the value of my service, but I feel as if I have

‘of course. and you will naturally be unwilling to remain without a

‘naturally. I have do nothing about it yet, because nobody want

to hear about such thing at Christmas-time; but I think of

advertising, or perhaps write to my former employer directly.’



‘yes, of course you could do that. but I have it on my conscience

former employer, and it must be my business not to let you suffer

‘you be very kind,’ say Roger, perfectly appreciate the unusual

any one could hold you responsible for what have happen, or

‘you have do a great deal more than that. you have stick to the

any common foreman might have stay in his place and take his

the proposition take Roger by surprise, and embarrass he at the

‘I think it be well not,’ say he. ‘I have consider my whole

there be a little pause, during which Roger think some rather

suddenly Gilbert observe—



‘I be present at the concert in the schoolroom the other night, and

I see what happen there.’

‘the wicked always gain their end,’ say Roger, unguardedly. ‘I do

begin to scheme for it, and with it she have get a lot of other

say it, but how be it that you too cling to that man? that be a

Gilbert’s face change a little.

in his way, long ago, when I have other hope and other ambition

seem to I that the good thing to do with they be to carry they

we have get so far below the surface in our talk, there be one thing

‘yes.’



‘he give it to I to read at the time.’



‘yes?’

‘I be glad you have tell I. his heart be so broken, you say,’ say

‘I be afraid he would think so. he do not even know I be meet

‘I don’t suppose he do. that do not make the blow at the time

‘no, he have not.’



‘do you think he have ever care to?’



‘he never have, up to a little while ago.’



‘and now you think he do?’



‘no, I don’t. I think he have be so well discipline by what he have

‘no. of course I look upon all this as say in confidence; and I

think that for the present we have settle all we have to do.’



‘yes, quite, I think. and I assure you I be much oblige to you.’



‘not at all. I be glad to have have the talk. you have my London

address, I think.’



‘yes. how long do you remain here?’



‘only a few day more.’



they exchange good morning, and separate. Roger, go home, be

very thoughtful. he know he have take a momentous step in refuse

to remain in Bradstane. he believe it be the good step that be

open to he, and he take it. it be what man have to do on their way

through life. step of some kind we have to take, though each one

may be fraught with consequence which we cannot foresee, and which

we can only appreciate after we have lose all power in the matter.

we can look on, in these after day, at the result of our action;

it be permit to we to rejoice in the fruit of our conduct, or,

as often as not, to repine over the same, or to beat our breast and

wish we have never be born,—but not to alter by so much as a

hair’s-breadth, the direction of the road open out long ago by our

own deed.









                              CHAPTER XXX



                          SERMON, BY a sinner





Gilbert have say to Roger that he be only remain a few day

long at Thorsgarth; but as a matter of fact, he stay till over

the new Year,—being able, seemingly, to put off the business which,

he every now and then remark in a casual way, call aloud to he

from London. he could hardly have enjoy himself much, during the

latter portion of his visit—at least, that be Eleanor’s feeling, as

she uneasily watch the course of event after the concert. for a

few day she be quite in the dark as to the exact state of thing.

of course she lie awake a long time on that particular night,

feel uneasy about every subject to which her thought

turned,—Otho, Gilbert, Magdalen, Ada; she feel no sense of security

or comprehension with regard to any one of they. why do Magdalen,

after behave so well at first under the insult which Otho have put

upon she, fall off so lamentably afterwards—tamely submit to his

behest, and allow he to drive home with she?



and Gilbert—in whom, to a certain extent, she have put her trust—be

no more than a broken reed. he have promise to see that all should

go right, and, on the contrary, everything have go wrong—just as

wrong as it could possibly go; and he seem neither vex nor

uneasy about it, but allow thing to take their course.



when she meet Otho at lunch, after his quarrel with Roger, and see

his sullen look, and hear his sulky, curt remark and reply, she

feel miserable, in spite of tell herself that it be no affair of

her; and she do not venture to inquire what have anger he. she

vaguely dread to hear his reply. the Christmas Day, which happen

also to be a Sunday, come; and the doctor’s Christmas-tree be to be

on the twenty-sixth. she have not see Mrs. Johnson since the

concert, and be therefore in ignorance as to what have happen at

the mill, and it suit Gilbert for a day or two to say nothing to

she. so she live on in uneasiness, and sometimes catch herself

thinking of her former life, which she have leave six week ago, as if

it be a hundred year away from she; and of her uncle and cousin

Paul on their travel, as if they be inhabitant of another world,

journey on sea and in land unheard of.



thing be in this condition on Monday afternoon, and she be

sit alone in her parlour in the wane daylight, when Barlow

come in with a message from Gilbert, to know if she would see he.

her thought, which have stray away from the painful present, be

suddenly pull back again to their post. instinctively brace

herself to meet something disagreeable, she bid Barlow show Mr.

Langstroth up, and then sit and wait for he.



in a minute, however, he be with she; and, as usual, his presence,

unwished for, and even dread in anticipation, prove in reality

soothe, almost agreeable. Eleanor struggle against this power of

Gilbert to make himself agreeable to she; resent it deeply in her

heart, as a sort of disloyalty to his brother, to whom she have in

her soul give irrevocably and for ever, the place of master of her

heart and destiny. this last be as strong a feeling as anything

which could be experience; but, nevertheless, Gilbert possess

this power of be agreeable to she when he come, and the fact

puzzle and annoy she more than she would have care to own.



‘you be very kind to let I see you,’ he say at once, as he take

the chair she point to. ‘I have be want to speak to you for a

day or two—about Otho.’



‘ah, I know it be about Otho. say on, and let we have do with

it.’



‘perhaps that will not be so easy, either. however, I will say on,

as you suggest. before I could speak to you, I wish to accomplish

a certain piece of business. I have now do so, and be free to say

what I like. I suppose you have be notice how angry Otho look,

without be able in your own mind to assign a cause for it?’



‘unless the cause be that he be unhappy because he have be do

wrong.’



Gilbert repress a smile.



‘I be afraid I cannot comfort you by confirm that theory of

your. by “do wrong,” I suppose you mean his little escapade at

the concert the other night. yes, I see. well, I imagine he have

forget all about that by now. he be angry, or “unhappy,” if you

like, because he have be, and be be, put to great inconvenience,

and he don’t like it; it make he uncomfortable.’



then he tell she about the quarrel between Otho and Roger, with a

sort of amused carelessness, as if he have be divert by the

combat, and somewhat contemptuous of the combatant, which tone

puzzle and do not reassure his hearer.



‘Otho do not like office work,’ he go on, smile openly. ‘he

have not have much of it yet; but the factory reopen to-morrow,

after the holiday, and then he will have to try a little of it. I

have telegraph to a man whom I know to send down some one

suitable, and I have promise Otho to wait until the some one come,

and just to put he in the way of business; but it may be a week or

so before my friend can hit upon the right kind of man. that make

he very angry——’



‘you don’t seem to think anything of the way in which _he_ have

behave,’ burst forth Eleanor, indignantly, the colour high in her

cheek. ‘I think it be the most abominable thing I ever hear of—his

treat Mr. Camm in that way. it be—it be——’



word fail she. she feel as if she would choke with anger and

disgust. Gilbert’s eye be fix upon her face; the slight smile

be still hover about his lip.

deserve to be put to inconvenience. he do not deserve to be

help out of it. what become of Mr. Camm?’



‘oh, I have see Roger. we understand each other. but don’t you want

to hear all that I have to tell you? I have another piece of news.’



‘what be it?’ she ask, feel from the way in which he speak that

it must be news of some importance, and stay her anger to hear

his anger, one would think. I tell he he ought to tell you about

it, but he say he willn’t; it be all between he and she. he do not

feel inclined to talk about it, and, in short, I see you half guess

                              CHAPTER XXXI



                   BRASS POTS and EARTHENWARE pipkin





the bad of winter have storm itself away, and it be March—the

latter end of March. the leonine portion of his reign have endure a

long time this year, and though it be now over, the warm gale

have yet some north-east to blow back, and the dominion of the lamb

have not fairly set in. and yet, there be the caress of spring in

the air—that caress which be unmistakable, and which may be feel, if

it be there, through the bleakest wind and the cold rain. this

caress be in the air, and the hue of spring be in the sky. here

and there her finger have sweep aside the wither leave, and

allow a violet to push its way up; and in some very sheltered

southern corner appear a tuft or two of primrose. in the garden

border at Thorsgarth, the crocus be begin to make a gallant

profound,—steady and to be rely upon. in the shady corner of the

garden, under the bud tree, the clump of daffodil be

and laugh through the April shower. and the grass, too, be

fade and brown. everything be full of promise. nature step

forward, erect and laugh, jocund, cast the burden of her

sadness behind she; not as in autumn, advance droopingly towards

it.



so much for the garden, the cultivate. outside, the road be

heavy and soft with mud; but it be a mud to make glad the heart of

man, especially farming man. the plough field, stretch their

great shoulder towards the upland, look rich in their

great pasture near Rookswood, on the Durham side of tee, the giant

ash-tree stand yet in their winter bareness, give no sign, save

by the hard, burnish black bud, which for month to come be

mean to hold fast their secret wealth of bud and leaf, their

treasure of summer glory. there be every promise that this year the

oak would be out before the ash, with, it be to be hope, the

proverbial result.



it be on such an afternoon as this, when the breeze blow from the

‘how full the river be just now; and so brown and strong! Dr.

individuality, which none in his vicinity can afford to despise.
