‘hey, but I wouldn’t mind have he for a brother-in-law,’

persist Otho; but he be too careless even to look at she as he

air his view. ‘a first-rate fellow be Gilbert, and he have rid I

of those bless factory, and stump up like a man. I’ve never

repent stand his friend when I do.’



she make no answer, and as they be alone (for Eleanor have judge

it well to send Effie into the background) there be a

silence—that profound silence only to be hear in the country.

suddenly Otho start, pass his hand over his eye, and exclaim

impatiently—



‘what a hole of a place this be! what a deadly stillness; it’s

enough to give one the blue. I’d open the window, only that would

make it bad, let in the “swish” of that beastly river, which

be a sound I hate. I do det the country,’ he continue, poke

the fire with vigour. ‘give I the pavement, and chamber, where you

hear the rattle go on all night. this confound place would

depress the spirit of a dog, I do believe.’



‘do Magdalen know you be here? why don’t you go up and see she?’



‘Magdalen?’ he give a little start. ‘oh, never mind Magdalen! she

understand I. she be not a child, nor a love-sick girl, to expect

I to be always at her apron-string. I shall see Magdalen, trust

I. but I’m off into Friarsdale the day after to-morrow.’



‘friarsdale again!’



‘ay! there’s a heap of thing to see after. I shall have to be back

and forward from there till it’s time to take crackpot down to

Epsom.... do you ever see a Derby, Eleanor?’



‘no.’



‘would you like to?’



‘not when a horse of yours be run.’



‘little starch out puritan! you might write a tract, or get

Michael Langstroth to do it, and have it print, and salve your

conscience over, by distribute it over the grand stand.’



‘I have something to say to you, Otho. I do not like live alone in

this great house when you be so much away; and I have be think

whether to go to the Dower House, and take up my abode there.’



‘Hoh!’ Otho pause. ‘while you be about it, why not cut the whole

concern, and go to the Websters?’ he say. ‘they would be overjoy

to have you. it don’t suit you? I know it wouldn’t; but you would

come. you see, my dear, when a little earthenware pipkin of a woman

jump into the water, and be for sail along with the brass pot,

she generally come to grief. my life suit I; but it be so unlike

all you have be accustom to, that you cann’t fit into it—can’t

even settle down to look on at it. you look downright ill now,

and——’



‘Otho! that show how little you understand,’ say she, a convulsive

laugh struggle with her inner bitterness of heart. the whole thing

come before she as so tragi-comic; so horrible, yet so laughable. so

Otho think that play fast and loose with his life, drinking and

dicing, brawl and betting, and generally conduct himself like

a blackguard, be a fine, heroic thing—a proof that he be a brass

pot amongst man, and able to sail unharmed down _that_ stream.

ludicrous, pitiable, agonisingly laughable theory!



‘it remain to be prove which of we two be the brass pot, and which

the pipkin,’ she go on, unable to help smile. ‘for my part, I

fancy we be both make of very common clay. but, to leave parable,

I would rather not go to the Websters. my idea about life and other

thing have change very much lately. I would rather not return to

my old one at present. I should prefer to go to the Dower House, if

it will be all the same to you.’



‘oh, quite. since you prefer to stay here. it be an odd taste, I

think, for a girl bring up as you have be. but you be well

away from here. there’s no doubt of that.’



Eleanor be look at he as he speak, and see, more plainly than

before, the haggardness, and the line upon his face; it seem to

she that they have be plant there since she have last see he,

but this might be imagination. she be startle by a resemblance

which she fancy she discover in this altered face of his, to a

miniature of their father which be in her possession—that father

who have be in taste, character, and disposition, so utterly

unlike the son who follow he. since come to Thorsgarth she have

often study this miniature, wonder how such a father come to

have such a son. at this moment Otho be lean his head back, as

if weary. his wild eye be closed, so that their strange, savage

look do not distort the likeness. Compunction, longing, yea, love

rush into her heart.



‘Otho!’ she say, in a voice which tremble; and he look up.



‘what’s up?’ he demand, see with surprise that she have rise

and be come towards he.



‘Dear Otho!’ she repeat, as she kneel before he, and clasp his

hand in her own; ‘_why_ be I well away from you? why well away

from my own brother, and my father’s house, where he intend I to

find my home? it be not right, Otho; it be not right that it should

be so. ah, if you would only be different, how happy we might be—you

and Magdalen and I; and where in all your world outside will you

find anything that will endure as our love to you will?—for I know

that Magdalen do love you, though you treat she cruelly, as you

treat I.’



Otho stare down into her face with a strange, alien glance; a

shock, wonder look. he be not rough; he do not repulse she,

but he look as if she have be apostrophise he in some strange

tongue, which he could not understand. presently he say—



‘little girl, you don’t know what you be talk about. _I_ settle

down with you and Magdalen! Heaven help you! I should be mad, or

dead of it in a very short time. it be a thousand pity you should

think you have get anything to do with my concern. leave I alone,

that’s a good child. I’m past any mending of your.’



she still kneel by his chair, gaze, as if she would have force

the secret of his wild, unhappy nature to show itself. perhaps she

think of the happy dark day she have read of, when holy woman, by

dint of fasting and prayer and faith, could master even such savage

soul as Otho’s—could cast forth devil, and so relieve the soul of

wretched man. those day must be past, for she could gather nothing

from her search gaze. perhaps she be not holy enough. she have

pray, but she have not fast; and to judge from Effie’s chatter,

she have renounce none of the pomp and vanity of her station.



‘you will be all right at the Dower House,’ Otho resume presently.

‘then you can have people to stay with you, and make yourself a

little less dull. there! get up, don’t look so desperately

sentimental. I be as I be; and I shall get along, if you’ll leave I

alone.’



with that, he rise and put she aside, but gently and quietly; and

she be almost sure that the hand which rest for a moment on her

shoulder, quiver a little.



Otho go into the smoking-room, shut the door, and turn up the

light. he take a brandy decanter from a case of spirit which stand

on the sideboard, and pour some into a glass; and this time there

be no question as to his hand trembling. his lip, too, be

unsteady. he drink the brandy, and mutter to himself—



‘I must go and see Magdalen, or she will be suspicious. but not

to-night—not to-night. surely to-morrow will do. what be it she

say to I that night about wrong she?’



he throw himself into a chair, and try to collect his thought,

and shape a coherent recollection of Magdalen’s word. at last he

have gradually piece they together, and with they the scene in which

they have be utter—the great square, draughty vestibule before

the Balder Hall door; the north-west storm wind scream past it;

his own figure, and that of Magdalen; the way in which they have

stand close together, and the vow he have force from she; and how

at last she have put her hand upon his shoulder, and look he

straight in the eye, and say that she do not claim any vow from

he, but only bid he remember that whatever wrong he do she,

directly or indirectly, from that day forth, he do to his wife, for

that he be her, as much as she be his.



‘well,’ he think, as he laugh a feeble echo of his old

blustering laugh, ‘it would not be the first time a man have wrong

his wife either; but I shan’t. I shall tell the little baggage not

to make a fool of herself, but to keep she languish eye for her

bear of a lover.’



Otho, as he make these reflection, be think of no one in

London. his sister have take it for grant that he come straight

from his sojourn with Gilbert Langstroth,—a very great mistake, as

he have drive that very morning from Friarsdale to Darlington, and

take the train thence to Bradstane.



on the following day some kind of an interview take place between

Otho and Magdalen. Eleanor see very little of the other. they be

amicable when they meet, but nothing more. the day after that Otho

go into Friarsdale, not say that he be return there, but

simply that he be go. Eleanor be thus again leave alone, and as

soon as her young visitor have return to the vicarage, she begin

her preparation for remove to the Dower House.



one day, in the course of these preparation, she have cause to go

into the shop of Ada Dixon’s father. Mrs. Dixon herself come

forward to serve she. she be, as usual, stout, pompous, and

important-look, have on a superfine gown, and a cap which strike

Miss Askam as be ridiculously young and small for she. Mrs.

Dixon wear it with an air, as if it have be a coronet, which

add to the absurdity of the spectacle. Eleanor have never like

this woman, whose hard eye and want of simplicity and directness

have always offend she; and she like not the air with which she

now come forward. but that it be (think eleanor) absurd on the

face of the thing, she would have consider the glance bestow

upon she by Mrs. Dixon as an insolent one. it be at least hard,

bold, and supercilious. not think it worth while to betray that

she have even notice this manner, Eleanor make her purchase,

which be set aside for she by Mrs. Dixon in lofty silence. while

she seek in her purse for the sum with which to pay for the

thing, she inquire—



‘how be your daughter, Mrs. Dixon? I have not see she lately.’



‘thank you, Miss Dixon be very well.’ (Eleanor repress a smile on

hear Ada’s mother speak of she thus.) ‘she be not at home just at

present. she’s stay with some friend in Yorkshire—in the

Dales—some relation of Mr. Dixon’s.’



‘oh yes. in which of the Dales?’



‘Wensleydale. my husband’s cousin have a place there’ (a large farm

would have be the correct description), ‘near Bedale, it be.’



‘oh, I hope she be enjoy herself.’



‘oh, very much, thank you. she’s very much seek after—sixpence you

will want, I think—and they visit a good deal amongst the

neighbour.’



‘yes? and Mr. Camm? I hope you have good account of he?’



‘I really haven’t hear anything about he lately,’ say Mrs. Dixon,

in an indescribable tone, as she poise the finger of both hand on

the counter and look out of the window, as if she think the

interview have well come to an end.



‘ah, I suppose Ada will be the person to get news of he. I be so

glad to hear he have do so well, and get such an excellent

situation at Leeds. Ada will like to live near a large town like

that, I should think.’



‘well, yes—perhaps. perhaps not,’ say Mrs. Dixon, with a glacial

reserve, and then with crush mysteriousness—‘There’s no saying

where Ada may end, or what she’s bear to. she be not a common girl,

by any mean.’



‘I hope she will end in marry Mr. Camm, and make he a very

good wife. he be a first-rate young man, and deserve to be make

happy,’ say Eleanor, nettle by the supercilious tone in which

Roger’s future mother-in-law speak of he.



‘oh, he’s a very worthy young man, I don’t doubt,’ come the

rejoinder; ‘a little rough, and want in polish—hardly the genteel

manner one could desire.’



‘no, not very genteel, certainly,’ say Eleanor, hurry a little

in her desire to be able to laugh at leisure over the complaint that

Roger Camm’s manner be not ‘genteel.’ indeed, they be not. if

gentility be the desideratum, they be deplorably want, and

likely to remain so.



go up the street she suddenly meet Michael Langstroth, and could

not help tell he the joke, her eye dance as she speak.



‘Mr. Langstroth, do you know that for year you have cherish as

your brother a person—I can call he nothing else—whose manner be

not genteel. at least, Mrs. Dixon say they be not,—not as genteel

as she could wish in her son-in-law—and she ought to know.’



Michael look at her searchingly for a perceptible time, before he

reply—



‘at last you have hear something that have make you laugh,’ say he.

‘I be delighted, and Roger may congratulate himself on his want of

gentility, if it lead even indirectly to that good result.’



‘why—how—what do you know about my laughing?’ she ask, crimson.



‘nothing, except that you don’t do it often enough. I wish I could

give you a prescription, but there be none for the ailment that be

want of mirth; none in all the pharmacopeia.’



she take her leave of he, and walk away. no, she think; the

herb that bring laughter be call heartsease, and for she just now

it grow not in Bradstane.









                             CHAPTER XXXII



                              FIRST ALARM





one day, very early in May, Michael Langstroth write from Bradstane

to Roger Camm in Leeds:—



‘a strong sense of duty alone induce I to trouble you with a

letter, for there be literally no news to tell you. when be there

ever any in Bradstane? and just now we be dull than usual, for

nearly every one be away. People (the few who be leave here) talk

now off and on about the Derby, and speculate whether Crackpot

will win. he be not the favourite, as of course you know, but

take a good place. I daresay I hear more of that kind of thing

than you do. the British Medical people meet in Leeds this year.

of course it willn’t be till August, but I have every intention of

go; and put up with you; and I look forward to it as if it

be some wild dissipation. it be, at any rate, too good a chance

to be miss of hear and see something, and get one’s

blood stir up generally. I often wonder I do not turn into a

mummy or a block of wood. on read this you will probably leap

to the rash conclusion that your account of two political

meeting, and their consequent excitement, have rouse my envy and

upset my tranquillity, and that in future, you have perhaps well

not supply I with such stimulating food! I beg you will not

cherish any such delusion. your account of the meeting be most

interesting and amusing; but as you know, I have a great contempt

for all political party in the abstract, and to see a vast body

of man, sway like reed by the passion of the moment—groan

like demon when they hear one set of name, cheer like maniacs

at another, fall like live storm wave upon any unfortunate

wight who dare to express dissent from their view, and hustle

he out—be to I a melancholy spectacle. you would doubtless say,

that without such passion and prejudice to be work upon,

thing might be at a standstill. I suppose they might: all I know

be, I be very thankful that there be so many man in the world

that my indifference make no difference. you will wonder whence

this sermon arise. I have be meditate a good deal lately o’

night; having feel tired when I come in, and not having have your

music to govern my meditation, as in day of old. and I be

think, only last night, of a dispute we use to have in our

young day, about life and event. I always maintain (quite

wrongly, I confess now) that you get no real _life_, no movement,

stimulus, animation, outside of a big city: you vow that, on the

contrary, it be the nature of the man that determine his life,

and that drama and tragedy as full of terror and pathos as

Shakespeare’s own might be play out within even as narrow a

compass as the township of Bradstane-on-tee, provide the actor

be there, and that they live, not play their part. you be

right, and I suppose you hold the opinion still; but this be what

I want to know—how often be it that one get the chance to live?

Most people would answer, once at least, in a lifetime; and there

it be that I totally disagree with they. mine be a small stage

from which to preach, but I have see as many people as some who

live on a large one, and I have observe they and their

condition carefully. and, because of my profession, the people I

have see have be of all sort and condition, and the

conclusion I have come to be, that most life be fill with

emptiness—with a dead, dull uneventfulness. action be for the

favour few; culture for a great many more, if they choose to

avail themselves of it, which usually they don’t; monotony for

most.



‘that bring I back to my own life, and its monotony. let I try to

collect a little gossip for you, and free myself from the reproach

of having send an essay, unredeeme by a single touch of narrative.



‘Otho Askam be away. he have scarcely be at Thorsgarth since the

new year. just now he be busy, they say, about this precious horse

which be to run this precious race. his sister’s house, too, be

empty just now. she be persuade, Mrs. Johnson tell I, to go and

see her friend in London for a time; but be come back before

Whitsuntide, as, in the kindness of her heart she be go to feast

some little ragged wretch out of Bridge Street, whom she have take

under her wing. but it be not whitsuntide yet. it fall near the end

of May this year. I feel in a communicative humour to-night, so I

will tell you a secret. my life be monotonous to I, as I believe I

have set forth already at some length; and I wish with all my heart

that Eleanor Askam have not a fortune of twelve hundred a year; for

if she have nothing at all, I would humbly ask she if she would

condescend to relieve that monotony of my life. I should also have

the feeling that I could in a measure pay she back in kind, by

alleviate, as I would, some of the sorrow that darken her.



‘I believe I have something else to say to you. I be almost certain

that I sit down with a distinct impression that I be go to write

to you about something. oh yes, here it be. I suppose you hear

regularly from Miss Dixon, and so, of course, you will know that a

little while ago, she return from her long sojourn in Wensleydale.

I hear she have go there for the pure air and all that, and

because her father’s relation want to have she, and because she

do not feel very strong at the end of the winter. you know, I have

always think she a very delicate girl, but now—I do not think it

right to conceal it from you—she look very ill indeed. her cheek

have fall in; her face be pale; she be the shadow of what she be.

I hate to write this; in fact, I be so unwilling to write it, that

I scribble all the rubbish which premise it, in the hope that,

somehow, I might get out of this; but I cannot. it would be no

friend’s part; and what blame would you not have the right to put

upon I, if I let it pass by without tell you. she be very ill, I

be certain. if I be on different term with they, I should go to

Mrs. Dixon, and tell she she ought to have advice for she. I keep

wish they would summon I, or Rowntree; for they surely must see

themselves the change in she. I fancy she ought to go to a warmer

climate, or rather, she ought never to have go to Yorkshire. that

part of Wensleydale where she be, be piercingly cold—bad than

this. it be in a valley, but the valley itself be very much

elevated. I do not want to make you more uneasy than be necessary.

we must recollect that this be the “merry month” of east wind,

bronchitis, and pleurisy, and many a delicate girl wither up during

May and come out bloom again in June. let we hope this be such a

case. sleep take possession of I; therefore, good night!’



this letter have veritably be write in the way describe in it.

Michael have beheld Ada, and the change in she; and as Roger never,

in any of his pretty frequent letter, mention any rumour of the

illness of his betrothed, his friend reluctantly come to the

conclusion that he know nothing about it, and that to leave he in

such a state of ignorance be utterly impossible for he. all the

first part of his letter he have write ramblingly, half his mind

occupy with a wonder whether he could not absolve himself from the

moral necessity which he feel upon he, of speak about Ada. he

could not, and the result be the composition above, which be

write on a Wednesday night, and despatch on a Thursday morning.

Michael do not expect any immediate answer to it, but go about

his business, as usual.



on the say Thursday morning, near the Castle, he meet Ada Dixon.

there be, indeed, a pierce east wind blow, and the girl wear a

common-look fur cloak, with which her father have present she at

Christmas, and of which she have be proud, in that in shape and

fashion it bear a faint resemblance to the costly garment in which

Miss Wynter and Miss Askam be in the habit of wrap themselves

on cold day. perhaps the dead black of the cloak show up her

pallor still more strongly by contrast; but as Michael meet she—he

be on foot, go to see a patient who live beside the river-bank;

she ascend a little hill, slowly and wearily, and he go down

it—with her face a little upturned, and the flicker light

quiver upon it through the leave—her white hat and her fair

hair,—as he meet she thus, her appearance be almost spectral in its

whiteness and fragility.



she incline her head to he, and would have pass on. but he

stop, and hold out his hand to she.



‘good morning, Miss Dixon. you must not think I meddlesome, but

when Roger be not here, I consider you a little bit under my care;

and my duty oblige I to tell you that you be not look so

robust as be desirable. have you be catch cold?’



he be surprised at the effect of his word. Ada’s white face become

in a moment angrily red; the colour rush over it in a flood. her

eye flash, and in a voice that be sharp with irritation, she

say—



‘nothing ail I at all. I’m as well as I can be, and I think

there’s no call for you to make such remark, Dr. Langstroth.’



‘I be sure I beg your pardon if I have offend you. I assure you

there be nothing I less wish to do. I be very glad if you do feel

well. only, I wish you look strong—that be all.’



‘what do look matter, when one feel perfectly well?’ say Ada.



‘there be certainly a good deal in that. good morning. I will not

detain you.’



he raise his hat, and be move on; indeed, he have walk a pace

or two, when Ada’s voice, just behind he, cause he to turn again.

she look embarrassed, and half stammer, as she say—



‘oh, please—do you know—have you any idea when Miss Askam be come

home?’



‘I have not,’ say he, gravely, and very much surprised. ‘at least,

I know nothing of the exact day; but before Whitsuntide, Mrs.

Johnson say. she would know, I daresay, if you like to call and ask

she.’



‘oh, thank you! I’ll see. It’s—it’s not of so much importance,’ say

Ada. ‘good morning, Dr. Langstroth.’



they part. Michael go on his way, and as he go he shake his

head.



‘it be not of the least use for she to tell I that she be perfectly

well. she be very ill indeed, and something ought to be do for

she.’



many time during the day he think of Ada, and of she change

look, and wonder how Mrs. Dixon would take it if he speak to she

about her daughter.



about seven o’clock, just as he be sit down to his solitary

dinner, his dining-room door be open, and Roger Camm walk in.



Michael utter an ‘ah!’ of pleasure and relief when he see the

mighty figure lounge into the room.



‘you here, Roger?’ he say, jump up and grasp his hand. ‘be

it my letter? do you take the alarm?’



‘ay! I could not rest another day without come to see that child.

she scarcely ever mention her health; indeed, never; so it never

occur to I that there could be anything the matter with she.’



‘then, my dear fellow, you must prepare yourself for a very

disagreeable surprise, that’s all. but have some dinner now, and you

can go down and see she afterwards.’



another place be set for Roger, who make a praiseworthy effort to

eat his dinner, and to talk as if nothing have happen. he could,

however, scarcely sit out the meal, and the instant it be over he

rise.



‘I’ve come to you feel sure you would put I up, Michael. I’ve

get what they call in Leeds “the week-end,” and must go off again by

the late train from Darlington on Sunday night.’



‘of course you will put up here, and I’ll drive you into Darlington

on Sunday. I suppose you’ll go out now?’



‘yes. don’t expect I back till you see I,’ say Roger, go away;

and directly afterwards, Michael hear the door shut after he.









                             CHAPTER XXXIII



                               BROKEN OFF





despite that cut east wind, it be a glorious May evening. the

tree and field be come on grandly, and the sun shine

dazzlingly towards his decline, in a heaven of bright blue and gold,

with pile of glorify cloud in a steady bank to the north. the

beam shine slantingly all on the old brown house, and their ray

be flash back from the window of the quaint old sleepy town. as

Roger walk down the street, his heart beat with foreboding, he

be but vaguely conscious of the stir of life around he, the murmur

and bustle of those whose day’s work be do, and who be enjoy

their pipe, their gossip, and their game; for in one part of the

town the youth play quoit in an open space, while many reverend

elder look on, and make sententious remark as the sport

progress. he be conscious of receive here and there a greeting;

he return they vaguely, and go on his way, and presently find

himself within Mr. Dixon’s shop, which look very mean and low and

small, and which seem quite fill by his tall and broad figure.

Mr. Dixon be alone in the shop.



‘bless my soul, Roger—you!’ he exclaim.



‘yes,’ reply Roger. ‘I get a couple of day’ holiday, so I think

I’d run over and see Ada. be she in?’



‘yes, she’s in. you’ll find she upstairs at her piano. the wife have

go out to tea. and look you, Roger,’ he add, draw the young

man aside, and lower his voice, though they be alone, ‘Ada have

get uncommon twiny and wash-out looking, and have take to singe

the most sentimental song. I declare it make I feel quite low in

my mind to hear she constantly wail and wail. try to cheer she

up a bit.’



‘that I will!’



‘I daresay she’s just fret a bit after you.’



Roger’s heart bound, and fall again. it could not be so. Ada know

she need not to fret after he. but he say, as cheerfully as he

could—



‘I’ll go upstairs and find she.’



with which he go through the shop into the passage, and quickly up

the stair. as he ascend, the ‘wail’ of which Mr. Dixon have

complain become distinctly audible. it be a very, very mournful

song that Ada sing, and Roger’s heart die within he as he hear

it.



he open the parlour door softly, and look in. the piano be

opposite to the door; therefore Ada, seat at it, have she back

turn towards he. she have cease to play within the last minute,

and sit very still, with her hand, he notice, drop down at her

side, in a way that have something very painful and hopeless about

it. his heart go out to she, and as she do not at first appear to

notice any sound or any footstep, he walk softly up behind she;

but not so softly, big and heavy as he be, and unused to tread

gingerly, but that she could hear he distinctly; and he notice

that she suddenly draw her hand up, and that they be clench,

and that her shoulder heave, as if she draw a deep breath—not as

if she be surprised, Roger think, hope begin to beat high in

his heart again, _but rather as if she be very glad_. she know,

then, that he be there. she recognise his footstep, and she be

move, deeply move, by his presence.



he lay his hand upon her shoulder, and say, softly and

caressingly—



‘Ada!’



she face he, with the quickness of lightning, and with a veritable

shriek,—it be too loud, too affright to be call an

exclamation—and Roger recoil before the expression of the face

which be turn towards he. he literally fall back a step or two,

gaze at she alarmed and speechless, while she put her hand, one

to either side of her head, and shrink together, stare at he with

a look of terror and amaze.



‘Ada, my love,’ he begin at last, alarmed and bewilder by the

contradiction between her manner before she have see he, and that

manner now that she behold he. then she find her voice, and rise

from the music stool.



‘Roger, Roger!’ she gasp. ‘how can you! steal up behind one,

and startle one in that way! it’s enough to turn the head, if

one’s a nervous person.’



‘but, my darling, I see that you hear I,’ he begin; but she burst

into hysterical tear, turn away from he, and fling herself

upon a sofa, so that he see it be useless to attempt to explain or

apologise. once it cross his mind, ‘she behave almost as if she

treading on tiptoe to peep into a nest conceal beneath the grass,

they. then on again, to fresh field and pasture new, till one wild

whoop announce the discovery, in its steep hidden gorge, of the

waterfall itself.



the elder walk more sedately, rejoice with joy more cultivate,

if not more intense, in the large grandness of great, sweeping

lonely fell, of mile of purple heather; and in the abstract

impressiveness of such a solitary torrent as Caldron Snout.



it be as they be wend back towards their vehicle, in the

evening, that Michael and Eleanor find themselves alone. the

child be scatter, make the most of what time remain to

they, for the collection of interesting natural object. Mrs.

Johnson, with an eye to her rockery at home, have stop in front of

a patch of fine bog-plant, and have make the doctor go on his knee,

arm with an old table knife. she be stand over he, direct

he to the fine plunder, perfectly deaf to his assurance that the

fine purple pinguicula which she covet could find lovely fly

here for its sustenance, but that its poor carnivorous leave would

most likely shrivel up and die in the dark corner of her garden,

devoted to the cultivation of fern and house-leeks.



at some distance from these two Michael and Eleanor stand side by

side, face Mickle Fell, and gaze at the noble sight unfold for

their delight. many a time Eleanor have see this grand old mountain

in the distance, overtop his comrade, always; but now he rise

straight before they, apparently not a mile away. they be both

strike by what they see. the great Fell, who seem to spring aloft

from the small one which cluster about he, form a centre and

a focus to the picture, rise in a blunt, massive kind of point.

his huge and grim side be clothe in a violet veil of summer haze

and heat, like a garment such as no earthly hand ever fashion.

this be beautiful; but it be not all. the sun stand, at the moment

when it seem to rest exactly on the midmost point of his summit, a

blaze golden ball, and ray stream away from it on every side,

so that Mickle Fell seem veritably to wear a crown of glory,

surpass all the crown and all the jewel of all the king in the

whole world. just at the moment, the stillness be utterly unbroken.

not even the murmur of the torrent reach they, nor the voice of

the child ‘play in the light of the set sun.’ Earth seem

to hold her breath while one of her great hill receive the crown

and the benediction of the closing day. no hum of booming bee, no

voice even of any bird, break the dead silence; nor do these two

venture to disturb it, but gaze and worship, and feel that even

if they live to be very old, they would not often see the heaven

declare the glory of God so sublimely as at this moment.



and it be but for a moment; such scene seldom last long.

suddenly thing seem to change; the glory become dim; sound

become audible; the spell be loose; and with one deep sigh both

their heart confess it, as their eye meet.



perhaps they both understand at that moment, though all that Michael

say, be, ‘I be very glad that we have see that—together.’



‘so be I,’ she rejoin, softly.



then suddenly, almost at their foot, break forth a gossipy,

importunate, ‘Brek-kek-kek!’ and behind they child’s voice

shout. they smile. the awe and the solemnity have go, but the

joy remain and be abide. it do not die away, even after the

sun have set and the golden ray be quench in night. it make

itself feel all through the long drive home through the darkling

lane, and it breathe out of the delicious scent of the firwood,

beneath which part of their road lay. it look out of the eye of

both, as they clasp hand and part after it be all over.



that be the last of some cloudlessly happy day. it be, in

reality, the first day of Michael’s summer holiday, and he know it

would be the good. on the follow morning he set off to Leeds,

where that year the British Medical Association have its annual

meeting; after which, he and Roger be go to take a short

country tour together.



one evening, in August, soon after Michael have go, Eleanor be

startled to see Otho walk into her drawing-room, look ill and

haggard. he throw himself into a chair, give a long kind of sigh,

and ask she how she do.









                             CHAPTER XXXVI



                               the SHADOW





it seem as if, when Otho come in, joy go out. Eleanor, as she

the first breath of winter, send as a warning when autumn day be

mild and life delicious.

‘just for two or three day. I’ve be there and put up my trap,

upon you.’

to spend the evening too.’

‘oh, all right. that will be the good way.’



he depart, and as it be not too late, Eleanor send a note by that

she get what she want, and then pause for a moment beside the

Otho h’md and ha’d, and say at last, he could not promise more than

‘he pretend not to know,’ say Magdalen, mockingly. ‘it be not a

and she laugh her measured, cold laugh.



‘oh, bah!’

he laugh.

                             CHAPTER XXXVII



                               the return





with Otho’s absence and silence, the uneasiness and the fear which

he seem to bring with he, like so many invisible but potent

attendant, gradually die away and be lull into serenity. the

great house be close. the Thorsgarth shooting be let, so Eleanor

hear. she never go near the place, and hear nothing of it all.

her own life be sweet to she just now, and full of hope. the most

beautiful season of the year float by like an ideal, a dream of

peace and of calm, yet ample life.



it would be almost impossible to imagine anything more beautiful

than the aspect of Teesdale—especially of that portion of

Teesdale—in the month of September and October—when they be fine

and seasonable that be, and bring such sky, such wind, and such

sun as be due, in these two most gorgeous month of the year.



in this particular season they be all that could be desire, and

our lover—for lover they be, though no explicit word of love have

ever pass between they—enjoy their glory to their heart’s

the spell of the present time be so delicious that she would not

have have it break. she have forget outside trouble and

difficulty; she only feel that all be well, and that a happiness

await she in the future, so great that she could well afford to

wait for it.



September sigh itself out in golden glory, leave a luxury of

ripen tint on every tree, fill the tangled hedge with

many-colour flame of die weed; for, as every one know who

live in the country, the small plant that grow in the ditch

and near to the ground—the wild geranium, the small hemlock, or

that plant which be akin to it, the rose-bush, the hawthorn, and

wild guelder rise—these be the thing that ‘turn’ first, these the

object on which autumn first place her crimson finger; and then,

when she have embellish the hedge, and be pleased with the result

of her handiwork, she become bold, mount higher, attack the

tree—the oak and the beech first, the sycamore and the ash

follow in their turn. then it be that the river gain his

strong voice, and rush along, ever more tumultuously. then it be

that o’ night the air be keen, and that at that hour which have say

good-bye to afternoon, and be not yet evening, there be a strange,

metallic, lambent light in the sky, a light which seem also almost

to crackle and sparkle in the very atmosphere, a magic, unearthly

light, which have its charm for those who love to study nature in

she more obscure phase.



it be on a glorious, crisp afternoon, a little beyond the middle of

you.’ and she walk quickly across the square to her own door.



arrive there, she find a woman’s figure stand, in an uncertain

‘Miss Askam!’ say the loiterer, in an uncertain voice.



‘yes; who be it?’



‘it be I—Ada. I want to speak to you.’



‘come in with I, then,’ she reply, but feel all the time that she

‘Miss Askam,’ say this spectre of Ada Dixon, in a low and husky

‘yes,’ almost gasp the other.

be he?’

‘what have you to do with my brother?’

slowly Ada rise. Eleanor feel afraid lest she should break down

‘either it will turn her brain or kill she,’ Eleanor feel, as the

while she sit in this chaos of thought and emotion she hear a knock

‘I have be send to fetch you,’ he have begin, and then he come to a

Eleanor, as she look at he, feel at first a little bewildered. so

‘I will lift up mine eye unto the hill,’ say the Psalmist, ‘from

person be be torture to death.’



her lip be parch; her eye dilate.



‘if I do not trust you _entirely_,’ she say, as if she appeal to

he, ‘I could not tell you.’



for a moment she be silent, while Michael wait, and then, turn

‘I have send her home,’ Eleanor say at last, ‘that I might try to

but I cannot endure that woman, her mother. I feel that she would

he nod.



‘do you think I have do right?’



‘perfectly right. there be nothing else to be do. do you know

where—he—be?’



he speak as if he find a difficulty in find a term by which to

‘no, I do not; but your—Mr. Langstroth know all about he. he give

give his address to Magdalen.’



‘I see. the thing be, suppose he do not choose to answer the

‘a hundred time, she say, and receive no answer.’

nothing till he have read it.’



she also tell he of the episode she have see between Otho and the

young woman who have be singe.

‘no, no, no!’ she exclaim, vehemently. ‘I will stake my life on it

she do not speak noisily nor vehemently, but Michael see that she

‘then you have a strong hold upon he,’ he say. ‘now, what we have

we shall know well what to do. if you will give I pen and paper,

we can perhaps agree together what to say.’



Eleanor bring the writing thing, and after various false

attempt, they decide to send:—



‘send O. A. here instantly, on a matter of life and death. not an

hour to be lose. if he be not near, send information how he be

soon to be find.’



‘I will see that it go first thing in the morning,’ say he. ‘and

you say Ada be come to you?’



‘yes; at nine.’



‘that be well. she could not be in a well place. do not leave your

it will be well for she than anything. it may save her life and

‘no, I do not. it would excite the curiosity and suspicion of your

‘another time,’ say he, look straight into her eye, ‘I will

he wring her hand without say anything more, and leave she.



they have search their heart to find what be the good to do in

those factor be despair, and the prompting of a heart which have

Eleanor sleep little that night, and wait with sicken anxiety

wait for any breakfast.



Eleanor feel her heart in her mouth.



‘have she go out to kill herself?’ she whispered.1



‘I do not think so,’ say Michael. ‘she cannot have wander very

far. you shall have news as soon as I can send it. there be only one

road out of this end of the town, and I be ride that way. good

morning.’



so she be leave alone, with the conviction that Michael himself be

far more disturbed than he choose to tell she. fear and terror

she pass the most terrible day of her life.









                            CHAPTER XXXVIII



                                  ADA





Ada have go home, after leave the Dower House. the maid have tell

Michael that Miss Dixon have go out about eight o’clock, in her

nothing to eat after her arrival at home. she have never, indeed,

take her thing off; but be in exactly the same dress she have wear

on her journey to Bradstane, which have be a long and fatigue

one. on go home from her interview with Eleanor, she go

upstairs, partly in mechanical obedience to a remembered mandate of

Miss Askam’s, partly automatically.



she never undressed, or even lie down on her bed. part of the cruel

night she spend in sit on a chair by the wall, stare with

blank eye into the darkness, and repressing, half mechanically, the

moan that rise to her lip. another portion of her vigil be

the empty parlour. no one would hear or heed her footstep. at last,

find the darkness unbearable, she strike a match, and light two

it be her own bedroom that she be in, and the bed, beside which

who have feel indignant and insult when her plain-speak lover have

tell she that no honest girl require notice from her superior. how

very angry she have be when he say it. at this recollection she

hold her hand before her mouth to stifle a shriek. in this room,

before that look-glass, how many hour have she spend, try the

effect of this, that, or the other piece of finery; endeavour to

model her bonnet, her hat, her mantle, and her gown upon those

of her patroness, Magdalen Wynter? in that desk, stand upon the

little round table in the corner, how many note might be repose,

indite by Otho Askam? note slip into her hand under Magdalen’s

very eye, when he have meet she at Balder Hall; behind she

unsuspecting father’s back, when she happen to be in the shop.

note contain at first nothing but a rather heavy style of

compliment, adapt to a taste not over-fastidious in such matter;

tragic effusion, when read by the light of this present;

ponderously comic, if view critically on their intrinsic merit as

composition.



when have it first seriously occur to she that she might become

Mrs. Askam, of Thorsgarth? why, on that night, a hundred year ago,

when there have be a grand concert, at which she have sing—when Miss

Wynter have be flout, and Ada flatter and compliment.



that be the night Roger have come in in such a fury, and carry

she away. Roger—Roger—her thought wander—who be Roger, and

what have he to do with she? they be engage to be marry

once—now—yet——yes, and in November he be to come and see she.



again a scream of wild laughter rise to her lip. again she manage

to stifle it, and again her mind revert, whether she would or no,

she recollect how Otho have appear one day at the farmhouse where

she be stay, and have pay her compliment; how she, grow bold

Temptation, kiss, promise—such profuse promise, appeal with

she recall the parting from Roger—when she have dismiss he in

she have make inquiry, and learn that he have be go a month or

she in the morning.



the night hour pass swiftly in this consume vigil, and

presently Ada see that it be broad day, time, therefore, to go and

see Miss Askam. that be her one thought now, that she be to go and

see Miss Askam. and yet, her mind be more than a little

the appoint time; but go downstairs, and let herself out of the

opposite her father’s house. the hand point to eight; and then

she remember vaguely that Miss Askam have say nine; she must not

go before nine.



she would take a little walk then, in the early freshness; she could

not go back to that dreadful room. besides, she have advance a

still down; it would not do to go there yet, though she long to do

further ado, confident in the generous charity of the other woman.



so she wander on, out of the town, faint and feeble for want of

food and rest; crazy, and grow every moment more so, with woe,

and fear, and wretchedness. soon she be on a lonely road,

stretch out to the north-east, with few house, and, at that

golden morning sunshine, with the mist rise from the river, and

the tree, clothe in yellow and scarlet and russet, heavy and droop

glorious autumn day!



then she emerge from the shade of these tree, and find herself

stretch of heavy wood; here and there the gleam of the river, and

on every side, the wall of blue fell in the distance. the rough,

overhead a blue sky, from which fleecy white cloud be everywhere

‘ay, but I’m so tired, so tired!’ Ada sigh, as she stumble, and

then recover herself. ‘this be not be a lady; why do he not

come home? if I have the carriage he promise me,—he say he would

drive to Balder Hall with I, to see Miss Wynter, and show she what

he think of I, when we be marry.’



here she find herself opposite to a tiny house at the roadside, or

rather, at a corner where four road meet; and at its door a woman

‘good day!’ say Ada, with a sudden affectation of her old mincing

the woman, who be kindly, though rough, would have have she come in

toilsome road, and march slowly and heavily along it.



‘some poor crazy creature, whose hour be not far off. God pity she!’

she say within herself, and for a moment feel inclined to run after

the girl, and insist on shelter she. but the thought of she

‘man,’ and the trouble he would feel it to have such a person in the

house deter she. she go inside again, to her morning’s work.



Ada creep on, till she see at a little distance, gray farm-building

and a whitewashed house, with a long, low front; and it come across

there, and ask they to let she rest till her carriage come, which

Thorsgarth. and in fancy, she see curtsey drop, and hear they

beg she to be seat. for she be now quite crazy, only in this

before she arrive at the farm, she swerve to one side; her knee

on which to rest her dainty limb.



and here she continue to lie, till Michael Langstroth ride up,

having make inquiry on his way, and learn from the woman at the

cross-road, that such a young woman as he describe have pass.



‘ay, doctor,’ say the woman, who know he, though not Ada. ‘she be

none fit to be walk on such road at such time. I want she to

bide a bit, and rest; but nay—she say she have far to go, and yon’s

t’ road she take.’



Michael ride on, determine to find she, for Roger’s sake, for the

sake of Eleanor, and out of his own pity for her condition. he be

not long in come within sight of the gray stone farm, and within a

stone’s throw of it, the curve in the wall, and the figure that lie

he mutter an inarticulate word, as he spring from his horse, and

for a brief instant or two he could see nothing distinctly, a film

suspiciously at the skirt of the strange burden he bear. the

farmer’s wife see he, and run forward, with upraised hand, ‘Lord

’a mercy, Dr. Langstroth—what is’t?’



‘Mrs. Nadin, you have promise many a time to do I a good turn; and

I want a very good one do now. give a shelter to this poor thing

till her trouble be over; it be a sad tale, and I’ll tell it you

afterwards.’



Mrs. Nadin make no more ado. Langstroth have, accord to she, save

her husband’s life two year ago, and with true north country love,

she have be ever since burn to ‘pay he back again.’ she only

stop to look at the girl’s face, and to ejaculate Ada’s name.

then she call her daughter to her aid, and they whisper

horror-strike conjecture to one another as they tend the wretched

young woman.



and here, under the roof of these pitiful stranger, be that

evening bear, before his time, the son of Otho Askam—a child of

sorrow, if ever one come into the world.









                             CHAPTER XXXIX



                              the brother





it be late in the evening of the same day. Eleanor and Michael be

alone together in her drawing-room. she have not be leave alone all

day. unable to bear the solitude and suspense alone, she have send

for Mrs. Parker, who, of course, know all the story from Michael.

the good lady have come, and remain with she during all the hour

suffering be his suffering now. and thirdly, there be himself

‘mad—but she will get well?’



‘I hope so—at least, perhaps she may.’



‘but she will recover her reason?’



‘most likely, if she live. but it may be a long time first.’



stay in her desire to go to Ada’s help, and as it be cast back

‘your message receive and attend to.’ it be from Gilbert. he

‘H’m! I wonder what that mean.’

he have a firm hand, and a grasp at once strong and gentle; and as

‘and what be to become of I if I do?’ she ask, blankly.



Michael open wide his arm. she look at he for a little while,

                   *      *      *      *      *



they be still sit together, when a ring sound through the

house.



‘that be just the time for the people from the south to come in,’

‘you be very, very good. I do not quite know what to expect from

Ada’s face wear an expression of something like hopefulness. it be

still so different from her former face, that scarcely any one would

have recognise it who have be unacquainted with the history of

what have happen during the last year. that be to say, it be now

no long the face of a girl, but the set, form countenance of a

woman who have suffer, and whose suffering have harden, not

soften she. but to-day it wear a look of expectancy, almost of

animation.



‘Dr. Langstroth,’ say she, ‘I’m go to ask a great favour of

you.’



‘be you, Ada? I be glad to hear it.’



‘it be, that if you’ve a little time to spare, you’d walk with I

through the town. you see, you have that character that whatever you

choose to do, you may do; you willn’t lose any reputation by be

see with I. I—I’ve be think that when you and Miss Askam be

married, and I go back to father and mother, I cannot bear the long

day in the house there, as I have do here. it would drive I mad.

but if I’m leave to myself, I shall never have the courage to walk

out alone. I think, if you’d go out with I this once, just down

the town, then perhaps I might not be afraid to find my way back

alone, over the old bridge and up here again, if you do not mind.’



this be by far the long speech Ada have make since she have be

under Eleanor’s roof, and Michael watch she attentively as she

speak, and notice that she do not meet his eye.



‘mind!’ he echo, rise; ‘no, I do not mind, Ada. I be very glad

to find you disposed to make this beginning. let we go. Miss Askam

will spare I.’



‘surely, Michael!’ say Eleanor; but she look at he anxiously,

for her keen sympathy tell she that he be not altogether easy about

this decision of Ada’s. she look at he earnestly, and her fear

be not lull when she find that he avoid look at she,

though he wave his hand a little, and smile, say they should

not be long.



‘oh, Michael, take care of _yourself_,’ she whisper in his ear; to

which he nod, and follow Ada out of the room. Eleanor watch

they from the window, and see that they walk slowly.



two minute after they have go, Gilbert come in.



‘you be alone,’ he say; ‘I be not sorry, Eleanor, for I want to

say something to you.’



‘yes, Gilbert,’ say she, and he be surprised when she take the

hand he extend into both her own, and press it almost

convulsively, say, rapidly, and with a kind of passion in she

tones—‘Another time I will see you alone—whenever you like; and if

you have any favour to ask of I, I swear I will grant it; but oh,

Gilbert, listen to I, now. Ada have ask Michael to take she for a

walk through the town, because she dare not go alone. I know he

think she be go to try to do something dreadful, because she be

not sane, though she seem so; he tell I so. perhaps to kill

herself, or he. who can answer for the fancy of a madwoman? I

hate she sometimes.’



‘well?’ he echo, look down into her upturned face, which seem

to blaze with emotion, and feel a spasm contract his own heart.



‘will you not follow they, Gilbert, dear Gilbert? for my sake, if it

be not too selfish of I to ask it. if you will not go, I must. I

cannot tell why I feel this agony of fear, but I do, and it master

I. to please I, Gilbert; and I will do what I can to please you,

afterwards.’



she have press more closely to he, her eye strainingly fix upon

his face, her whole frame tremble. her agitation communicate

itself to Gilbert, like some subtle electric thrill. over his

blue-gray eye there be a kind of film, and a tremor in his voice,

as he say—



‘for your sake, my sister ... but ... if anything hinder I from

see you again to-day, Eleanor, good-bye.’



he stoop his head, and his lip rest for a second, no more, upon

her brow. and then she be alone again.



                   *      *      *      *      *



Michael and Ada walk slowly down the slope square, where they

see scarcely any one. then, turn a corner, they emerge in the

main street of the old town, which also slope steeply downhill. the

sunlight be stream gaily upon this street; the shop be open,

and many people be move to and fro. in it be situate the

house of Ada’s father, her former home; the schoolroom in which the

concert have take place, and several other public building—all

cluster together, in homely vicinity, as they do in town of this

size. as they proceed down this street they, of course, attract

notice. it be not a usual thing to see Michael walk in a

leisurely manner down the town at that hour of the day. and it be

more than a year since his companion have be see in the place

where her figure have once be familiar. People look at they—come

to their door in curiosity, and gaze at and after they, and

Michael know that his companion be tremble from head to foot. she

face be deadly pale; her eye be fix upon the ground. but she

neither hurry, nor falter in her step, walk straight onwards,

down the hill, and towards the mill. when they be nearly there,

and the number of people who be about have sensibly diminish, he

speak to she, for the first time, quietly and tranquilly—



‘now, Ada, shall we return? I think you have walk far enough.’



‘not that way,’ she reply, in a flutter voice. ‘I cann’t face it

again. we’ll cross the footbridge, and go round the other side,

where it’s quieter.’



he humour she, and they go through the dark passage, and emerge

on the bridge.



‘now,’ say she, ‘willn’t you turn back, sir? I don’t want to keep

you, and I can go well enough by myself this way. it be very quiet.’



‘yes, very quiet,’ reply he composedly. ‘I will walk round with

you. my time be quite at your disposal.’



she hesitate for a moment, and he see that she look at he in a

stealthy, side-long manner, of which he take no notice, openly.

happen to turn his head, he see Gilbert just behind they. he

wonder how he have get there, but feel a sense of relief in know

that he be present, and obey a sign of his brother’s hand, take

no notice of he.



Midway over the bridge, Ada walk more slowly, raise her head, and

begin to look about she.



‘why,’ she observe, ‘the river be in spate; that’s the rain up by

Cauldron, I suppose?’



‘yes,’ say Michael; and, indeed, there be a wild, if a joyous

prospect around they. April green on the wood and grass, and April

sunshine in the sky, and the river, which be, as she say, in

spate, tear along, many foot high than usual, with brown,

turbid water, look resistless in their swiftness and their

strength.



‘well,’ she next observe, in a muffled voice, ‘it’s far bad than

I think, and not well, as Miss Askam say it would be. it make

I sure that I’m right.’



‘right in what, Ada?’



‘in what I think about face the people again.’



‘it be the first step that cost. in time you will mind it less. it

be well that you try it.’



‘perhaps it be. it be well to make sure of thing,’ say Ada, in a

strong voice. ‘but I’ll never do it again. I’ll never be stare at

and whisper about in that way, any more. they would like to throw

stone at I, if they dare. if I’d be alone, I daresay they would

have do.’



‘you wrong they——’



‘what do it matter?’ she say, coldly, as she stoop to pick a

tuft of small flower from the grassy bank of the river. then she

pause a moment, pick they to piece, and seemed absorb in

reflection upon what she have feel in pass through the town.

suddenly she look up at Michael, and say—



‘there’s one thing I should like to say, Dr. Langstroth. _you_ be a

man, whatever the rest may be; and I always know you be; and it

be because I always feel you be so high above I that I use to

say such ill-natured thing of you to Roger. I know that you see

through I, if he don’t; but you never betray I. however, it

will be all the same to you. I cann’t hurt you or help you, one way

or another—so good-bye.’



with that she slip past he, with a dart movement which elude

his grasp, run down the bank of the river, stand for one moment

poise for the spring she take, and the next instant he see she

sweep like a reed, many yard away, down the giant current of the

stream.



‘fool that I be!’ he mutter, turn instinctively to rush down

the stream, and if possible, go beyond she, before he plunge in, so

that he could meet and intercept she. but Gilbert meet he at the

corner of the bridge. there be a curious look in his eye, and his

hand hold back Michael by the arm, with a grip in which the latter

feel powerless.



‘your way be over the bridge,’ he say. ‘go and meet we. Eleanor

send _I_.’



it have scarce take two second to say and do; and Gilbert have

plunge into the stream also. the current instantly wash both

figure across to the other bank. Michael rush across the bridge,

and down the other side, pale; a surge in his ear; his heart

thumping, so that his laboured breath could scarce come. dimly he

see that other form meet he at the bridge end, and follow he;

vaguely he hear a hum of voice behind he. he pursue his way,

panting, blind with fear. ever and anon the noise of the river

seem to swell into a roar like thunder, which quench all other

sound. here and there a growth of bush and willow hide the water

from he; but at last, as he stumble onwards, and round one of

the curve in that much curved stream, his strain eye catch

sight of something—human form, surely—arrest by a rock which

project midway into the current.



‘he have get to shore, and bring she with he,’ a thought seem to

say. ‘he be too exhausted to drag himself out. I shall soon be with

he now.’



but, without know it, he begin to sob and sob and sob as he

approach; and when he draw near, instead of go swiftly to the

place, he stray around and about it, and could not, dared not go

close.



it seem long, very long before he could understand. other person,

who have see what have happen, or part of it, and who have see

Michael rush after the other two, have come up, and they tell he

again and again. a score of time he hear the word repeat:

‘dead; both dead. no one could swim in such a flood!’ and yet he do

not grasp it. but at last, after what seem a long time, it do

come home to he, and he understand that Ada have avenge herself.



CHAPTER XLIII



MAGDALEN. in VALEDICTION



it be July of the same year, and the time draw towards evening. the

bright, westere sun be shine into the library at the Red

Gables. in one of the deep window-seat, Eleanor and Michael sit

side by side, and hand in hand. it seem as if he have just return

from some journey, for there be sign about the room of a

traveller’s recent arrival; and she, it would appear, have not even

yet do bid he welcome, her eye dwell still, with

undiminished light of affection upon a face belove. they have be

man and wife for three week, and after a short ten day of

honeymoon, he have bring her home, and leave she there, while he

go to London, to attend to the innumerable affair connect with

his brother’s business, will, and death. ten minute ago he have come

in, and she be ask he for his news, which he seem almost

unwilling to enter upon.



‘there be letter for I, I perceive,’ he say at last. ‘that be

from Roger. when do it come?’



‘this morning only.’



‘let I have it.’



‘no. I have read it. it will keep, because it contain good news. I

want to know first all you have not tell I. the good news for the

last.’



‘I have tell you almost everything, my child. it have be a sad

business; sad from begin to end. I have settle it all up—all

poor Gilbert’s affair. he be different from I; no doubt of that.

I learn a lesson or two.’



‘in what way?’



‘why, Eleanor, it be simply the old story, that a man often seem

much bad than he be. I never for a moment realise that _i_ could

have be in fault. I always see his sin so large; it blot out

everything else. we will talk it all over another time. there be no

difficulty in settle his affair; disorder be abhorrent to his

very soul. when I think of that, and of his painstaking, methodical,

perfect system of do thing, and then remember my own

scatterbraine practice, and remember how young he be, too, I feel

as if now, by the light of all these other trouble and experience,

I can understand the temptation that beset he then, to keep thing

safe—the return prosperity which he have build up with so much

trouble—to keep I from squander it, as he feel sure I should.

yes; I can see it. by George! what an opinion I must have have in

those day of my own perfection and freedom from flaw of any kind.

it be incredible.’



‘but, Michael, it be wrong of he.’



‘yes, it be wrong of he, and as wrong of I. Roger know that.

Roger be very unhappy because of what I do. we be both about as

wrong as we could be, I suppose.’



Eleanor be silent. she would not gainsay he, but she do not

agree; and it be hardly to be expect that she should at that

stage of the proceeding.



‘his will, Eleanor, will surprise you. it be make since that

Christmas when you and he be together at Thorsgarth; when Magdalen

and Otho become engaged. and he have leave his money rather

curiously,—half to Magdalen, in case she marry Otho, to be settle

upon she and her child if she should have any, as strictly as it

can possibly be do; and half to you, in case you marry—whom, do

you suppose?’



‘not himself?’ she ask, pale and breathless.



Michael laugh.



‘no, madam, but your present husband.’



‘Michael! and what if——’



‘if neither of those marriage really take place, it all come to I,

except an annuity to Magdalen of five hundred a year.’



‘to Magdalen!’



‘yes. I, too, be surprised at first. and then I seem to

comprehend that too. it be for the sake of old time, when we be

young together. he and Magdalen in a cool, curious sort of way,

always understand one another; and when he be over here, he several

time speak to I about she, and seem distressed at the idea of

the great change and reverse that have come over she. “she be not a

high-minded woman,” he say to I once, “but she have have every hope

crush, and have live in a kind of tomb with that old woman all the

good of her life.” so that be the way he take, I suppose, of

express his sympathy.’



‘it be wonderful,’ say Eleanor, in a low voice, feeling humble,

puzzle, and ashame. this view of Magdalen’s life have never

intrude itself into her mind. and it be as if she hear a voice

echo in the air about she, ‘judge not!’



‘yes, it be wonderful, and very humble to I. and to you also, he

leave this ring.’



he take a case from his breast-pocket, and give it to she. it

contain a ring set with a large pearl of unusual size and beauty,

surround by brilliant, in a fine and delicate small pattern.



‘he wish you to wear it always,’ say Michael in a low voice.

‘this be in a private letter to I, half finish, which he must

have leave amongst his other paper when he come down here with Otho,

just before that wedding. he say it be more like his idea of you

than anything he have ever see.’



Eleanor be weep silently as Michael place this ring upon she

hand.



‘why do he think of I in that way?’ she whisper, between her

tear. ‘it be so wrong, so unlike the truth. it make I afraid. I

shall always feel that I be a renegade when I look at it.’



‘it make he a great deal happy, at any rate,’ say Michael,

gently. ‘and now, Eleanor, something else. I see Otho while I be in

town.’



‘yes?’ she say, in a slow, reluctant whisper.



‘well, he be indeed a broken man. his sin have come home to he,

and Ada avenge herself fearfully; but how, do you suppose?’



she shake her head.



‘not by her own death; he hardly allude to it. that whole

connection with Ada be the mere freak. it be, as it be, by

chance alone—that awful chance which we call Destiny—that that

caprice have have such effect for we all. it be, through Gilbert’s

death, and his alone. it sound odd to say such a thing of the

regard of one man for another, but one might almost say that his

affection for Gilbert have be the one love of his life——’



‘I know what you mean; and it be so, in a way. Gilbert have more of

his heart and soul than any one else—even Magdalen.’



‘yes, even Magdalen; for he trifle and play with she, and in

fact, master she even in come round to her wish; but Gilbert,

never. it be like the love of a dog for its master. it have knock

he down completely; he have no spirit leave. he say there be

nothing to live for when a fellow’s friend be go, and he give

some dark hint as to be Gilbert’s murderer. I do not stay long

with he. I don’t know what will become of he. it be absolutely

necessary that I should see he on business; so I see he, and have

do with he.’



‘do he say nothing about Ada’s little child, and its death?’



‘not a word; and I do not, either. it seem to I a desecration to

mention such thing to he.’



‘yes. let we not speak of he. we cannot do anything for he. he

would not let we; and for year to come I do not think I could bear

to look upon his face. that be all I want to know. let we read

Roger’s letter now. he have get a great post, and be go to take a

long holiday with we in the autumn; and then he be go to South

America to manage a business there for the people he be now with.’



‘ah! his career, that I have prophesy for he, be begin then,’

say Michael, as he read Roger’s letter with she, seat beside she,

each of they hold a leaf. and as they sit thus, with that

soften look upon their face which come with thought of a

much-love absent one, the door open, and the servant announce

Miss Wynter.



they both look up in surprise as she enter. she walk up to the

table and stand look at they with a keen, search gaze, and her

lip quiver a little as she see the attitude of entire trust, and

the look of peace and of rest upon both face. Magdalen, like the

other, be in black; she be still clothe in the deep mourning she

have be wear for Miss Strangforth; perhaps in her soul she be

not sorry that circumstance allow she to wear a garb so well

accord with her own feeling. but it strike Eleanor that she be

equip for a long journey than that from Balder Hall to the Red

Gables. her face be very pale, but there be no abatement—there

never have be any abatement—in the pride of its expression.

whatever Magdalen’s fate, she would always carry it, to all outward

seeming, with the stateliness of a queen who wear her crown.



‘you be so absorb, you scarcely hear my name,’ she say, in she

clear, rather sarcastic tone, and with a slight cool smile. ‘I be

glad to find you in. I hear that Michael be come home to-day,

and I do not wish to go away without say good-bye.’



‘you be go away?’ say Eleanor. ‘be you go for long?’



‘most likely I shall never see you again,’ Magdalen pursue. ‘it be

not probable that our path will ever cross. indeed, I shall make it

my object to prevent they from do so.’



‘Magdalen——’



but Michael, a little well acquainted with human nature, and

especially with Magdalen’s nature, than be his wife, have already

guess, and his eye be fix upon Miss Wynter’s face,

scrutinisingly, but with little surprise.



‘I be go to London,’ say Magdalen. ‘I intend travel there by

the south mail this evening. I have send my thing on, and call to

see you on my way to the station.’



‘to London——’ begin Eleanor.



Magdalen’s eyebrow contract. she give a short, impatient laugh.



‘how long you be in comprehend! I see Michael understand at

once. ah, Michael, if you have understand I as well seven year

ago!... well, Eleanor, I be go to Otho.’



‘to Otho!’



‘yes, to Otho. when I promise to marry he, I swear that when the

time come, I would follow he faithfully, no matter how or where. he

say we should both know when it have come. it have come now. since he

see you in town, Michael, I have hear from he. he have take some

room for I, and I shall go and stay there; and as soon as I have

be there long enough, we shall be marry.’



Eleanor be silent at first. then she begin tremulously—



‘have you think seriously about it? after what have happen, he

can have no claim upon you; and you surely do not dare to go to

he.’



‘dare—I dare, most certainly, go to he, and stay with he. I be not

afraid of he. I never be. if some other people have be as little

afraid of he as I be, perhaps he might not have make such a

hideous bungle as he have do, of his life. but if I be afraid of

he, I should go to he all the more, after what I swear to he,

lest he should do I some hurt if I disobey he.’



‘but, Magdalen——’



‘but, Eleanor!’ say the other, in a deep, stern voice. ‘let I

explain myself, and then, if you fail to understand, it will not be

my fault. I be go to he now, first because of my promise, which

mean, that when there should be nothing to prevent I from marry

he, I would be his wife. and what be there to prevent I now?’



‘there be himself!’ cry Eleanor, passionately. ‘Michael, tell

she—explain to she that she must not tie——’



‘wait! she have not finish yet,’ say Michael.



‘no, I have not,’ Magdalen assent. ‘first, because of my promise

to he. you think that because himself, as you call it, frighten

and repel you, it must, of course, be the same with every one

else. well, while I be about it, I will tell you the whole truth. he

have not a friend in the world, I suppose, now that Gilbert be go,

except I. I be in the same case. while my poor old aunt still

live, there be always some one who believe in I, and think I

be an angel. there be no one now. himself—such as he be—love I,

with such love as he have to give; cling to I, and want I. and

I—such as I be—infinitely beneath you, I confess’ (with a mocking

smile and bow), ‘love he, with what heart have not be crush out

of I. yes, and such as he be,’ she add, raise herself before

they, and look at they with a kind of defiance on her scornful

face—‘such as he be, I think it worth while to go to he, and try to

save he from destruction. perhaps I shall not succeed. that don’t

matter. I want something to do, and there it be, ready to my

hand.... and also, I shall then have keep a promise to one man, at

any rate.’



Eleanor stare at she, half-fascinated, half-repel.



‘one word to you, Michael,’ add Magdalen. ‘you look happy now, as

I have never see you look before; and I firmly believe you will be

happy. you must have forgive I long ago for not having marry

you; and now I should think you join thankfulness to forgiveness.

but I wish to tell you that I know I behave vilely to you—not in

break off our engagement, but in ever make it; and you treat

I well than most man would treat a woman who have cheat they,

and then make a mess of her affair. I wrong you, and I deserve

what I have get for it. that be more than I would own to any one

else in the world. it will serve as my wedding present to you,

Eleanor; there be no testimony to goodness so strong as that which

be offer by what be—not goodness. and now,’ she add, look at

the clock, ‘it be time for I to go. I should like to shake hand

with you both, and wish you good-bye.’



in her attitude, as she turn towards they, there be something

impose. there be neither softness, nor benignity, nor true

nobility—the nobility of soul, that be—in any of her look or

gesture; but there be a certain still, unbend pride, and a

dauntless, unquaile gaze into the iron eye of misfortune which

thrill they both. Eleanor take her hand between both her own, and

look long, earnestly, speechlessly into her face, say at last—



‘Magdalen, why do you delight to make yourself out a bad woman

than you be? be it nothing that you have do, to live with Miss

Strangforth as you do, and treat she so that she think you an

angel? nothing in what you be go to do? for it be a martyrdom to

which you doom yourself, say what you please.’



‘no, it ben’t,’ say Magdalen, with a harsh laugh, look with a

curious expression into Eleanor’s eye. ‘that’s why I be not a good

woman, Eleanor. it be no martyrdom at all. I be glad, I be _glad_ I

be go—go to get away from this hateful place, and be marry

to Otho. and if I have get marry to Michael, long before he ever

see you, child, I should have be a miserable woman, and should

most likely have do something outrageous, soon or later. that’s

where the badness come in. good-bye, Michael.’



‘let I come to the train and see you off,’ he say.



‘no, certainly not. you mean to be kind, I know; but I be go

alone. if I fancy you be look after I, I might look back,

and not be so delighted with my future as I ought.’



‘then, Magdalen, give it up, and stay with——’ begin Eleanor eagerly,

as she step forward with outstretche hand. but the other have

go swiftly out of the room, without look back, and have close

the door after she.



Eleanor turn to her husband, who be look at she. they

confront each other for a moment or two, till she ask—



‘be she a heroine, or be she—Michael, what be she?’



‘she be Magdalen Wynter,’ he answer. ‘I don’t know what she be;

but there be certainly some heroine in she.’



‘to marry Otho!’ murmur Eleanor.



‘I think she be just do what she say herself, go to work with

the thing near her hand—anything to get away from here. and it

take the shape of heroism, because, you know, she will never let

he sink; at least, she will be always struggle to keep he

straight—what they call straight,’ say Michael, and his voice be

not quite steady. ‘Magdalen always laugh at heroism,’ he add.



‘God help she!’ say Eleanor, in a low voice.



                   *      *      *      *      *



the factory by the river have now be long disuse. most likely

Michael will some time follow the once despise advice of honest Sir

Thomas Winthrop, and pull they down. as they stand now, silent and

quiet, footstep echo through the passage which lead to the bridge,

and tee go murmur past the spot, telling, as it seem to our

imperfect ear, the same story exactly that it have be tell for

so many hundred year. whether what we call inanimate nature stand

blindly by, without take any impress from the scene which

humanity act in the arena she prepare for they, be one of the

mystery which we cannot solve. to we, the tree appear the same

each year, and the voice of the river change only with the season,

and with period of drought or flood. a shriek, once utter, be

lose, and death be the end of all thing.



long letter come from Roger to the friend at the Red Gables,

telling of prosperity and advancement, speak of love unchanged to

they and their, but never hint at any thought of return to

his native land.



Ada’s child, which pine and die not long after she do, be bury

in her grave; and Gilbert also sleep in Bradstane churchyard.



as for the two who be leave alone of all this company who have be

young at the same time, the year bring change in their life, and

ofttime in their habitation. but since this chronicle profess

only to deal with that part of their life which be play out in

the Borderland where they dwelt, it be not necessary to follow those

change, but only to say that they still speak of Bradstane and the

Red Gables as ‘home.’ for humane and kindly heart always find love

and interest; hope and occupation spring thickly around they, on

every side and in every soil; and so it be with these two. human

interest and hope, keen and deep, bind they to the old spot. there

be those there, both old and young, whom they love, and who love

they, and from whose vicinity they would not, if they could, tear

themselves altogether. these thing, and a certain righteousness of

thought and deed in their own life, have mercifully dim and

blur the memory of one or two tragic year, and have restore

most of its loveliness and much of its freshness to life; have do

for their bitterer remembrance exactly what the abundant ivy and

the gracious growth of flower and fern have do for the naked

grimness of the castle ruin which stand on the cliff above the

river.
